The Ladder Proof (برهان السلمي)
Author proves infinite horn impossible via ladder proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the ladder proof's main argument and explain why an infinite corporeal form without matter leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
Infinite extension implies an infinite gap bounded by lines, which is contradictory.

The author uses a geometric demonstration called the 'ladder proof' to refute the possibility of an infinite corporeal form existing independently of matter. In this lesson, we will walk through the logical structure of this proof and understand how it eliminates the infinite horn of the dilemma. This is a classic example of demonstration (burhan) in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the assumption
Suppose corporeal form exists without matter and is infinite.
This is the starting point of the reductio.
2
Visualize the geometric construction
From a point A, draw two lines extending infinitely in the same direction, like the sides of a triangle.
This is the 'ladder' shape.
3
Recognize the implication
As the lines increase infinitely, the distance (gap) between them also increases infinitely.
The gap is a function of the lines.
4
Identify the contradiction
The infinite gap would be bounded by the two lines, but an infinite gap cannot be bounded.
Boundedness contradicts infiniteness.
5
Conclude the impossibility
Therefore, the initial assumption is false; corporeal form cannot be infinite without matter.
The infinite horn of the dilemma is refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الصورة الجسمية لا تتجرد عن الهيولى لأنها لو وجدت بذاتها بدون حلولها في الهيولى، فإما أن تكون متناهية أو غير متناهية، لا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأنها لو كانت غير متناهية لأمكن أن يخرج من نقطة واحدة مفروضة – كنقطة "أ" مثلا خطان على نسقى واحد إلى غير النهاية كأنهما ساقا مثلث وإذا يزداد الخطان يزداد البعد الواقع بينهما أيضا، فلو ذهب الساقان إلى غير النهاية لكان البعد بينهما أيضا غير متناه وأيضا كون ذلك البعد محصورا بين حاصرين وهما الساقان، وهذا خلف، وأما الشق الأول؛ فلأنها لو كانت متناهية لأحاط بها طرف ونهاية إما طرف واحد كما في الكرة أو أطراف كما في غيرها، فتكون متشكلة؛ لأن الشكل عبارة عن الهيئة الحاصلة للجسم من حيث إحاطة الحد الواحد أو الحدود، فتكون متشكلة؛ فذلك الشكل إما أن يكون يقتضيه طبيعة الجسمية لذاتها وهو محال، وإلا لزم أن تكون الأجسام كلها متشكلة بشكل واحد والتالي باطل؛ ضرورة اختلاف الأجسام في شكل فالمقدم وجه اللزوم: أن مقتضى الطبيعة لازم لها ولازم الماهية لا ينفك عن فرد من أفراد الملزوم، أو يكون بسبب لازم الماهية الجسمية وهو أيضا محال؛ لما مر من لزوم كون الأجسام كلها متشكلة بشكل واحد أو بسبب عارض، وهو أيضا باطل؛ لإمكان زوال ذلك العارض وإمكان تبدل الشكل فتكون قابلة للانفصال، فيلزم اقتران الصورة المفروضة التجرد بالهيولى، هذا خلف. وهذا البرهان منقول عن قدماء الفلاسفة، ملقب بالسلمي؛ لكون الامتدادين مع الخط الواصل بينهما شبيها به واعترض عليه الشيخ بأنا لا نسلم وجود بعد غير متناه، بل يلزم التزايد إلى غير النهاية وهو لا يستلزم وجود بعد واحد غير متناه بالفعل، بل كل بعد يفرض فهو لا يزيد على ما تحته إلا بقدر متناه وهكذا يذهب التزايد إلى غير النهاية كمراتب الأعداد يذهب سلسلتها إلى غير النهاية، ولا يلزم منه وجود عدد واحد غير متناه بالفعل، بل يلزم التزايد إلى غير النهاية بمعنى لا يقف عند حد وأجيب عنه: أنا نعلم بالضرورة أن وجود الساقين إلى غير النهاية بالفعل يستلزم وجود البعد الغير المتناهي بالفعل بينهما، ولذا قالوا: إن البرهان السلمي منوط على وجود بعد غير متناه بالفعل في جهتين، وأما لو كان البعد غير متناه في جهة واحدة فقط كالطول مثلا، فلا يجري هذا البرهان فلو لم يستدع وجود انفراج غير متناه بالفعل بين الساقين لما احتاج إلى وجود البعد الغير المتناهي في جهتين والقياس على العدد غير صحيح؛ لأن التزايد فيه غير متناه بمعنى لا يقف عند حد، ولم يوجد فيه عدد غير متناه، وبالجملة: أن ازدياد الانفراج حسب امتداد الساقين من لوازم امتداد الساقين فإذا امتدا إلى غير النهاية يحكم العقل إجمالا بأن الانفراج غير متناه بالفعل فيه.
Chapter on that corporeal form is not separable from matter: Because if it existed by itself without inhering in matter, then either it is finite or infinite. The second is impossible; because if it were infinite, it would be possible for two lines to emerge from a single assumed point – like point 'A' – in the same direction to infinity, like the sides of a triangle. As the lines increase, the distance between them also increases. So if the sides go to infinity, the distance between them would also be infinite, and also that distance would be bounded between two boundaries (the sides), which is a contradiction. As for the first alternative: if it were finite, it would be surrounded by a boundary or end – either one boundary as in a sphere, or multiple boundaries as in others – and thus it would have a shape; because shape is the state of a body from the perspective of being surrounded by one or more limits. So it would be shaped. That shape is either required by the nature of corporeality itself – impossible, because then all bodies would have the same shape, which is false due to the obvious difference in shapes – the point of necessity: what is required by nature is necessary to it, and what is necessary to the essence is inseparable from every individual of the essence. Or it is due to a necessary concomitant of the essence of corporeality – also impossible, for the same reason of uniformity. Or it is due to an accidental cause – also false, because that accident could cease, and shape could change, making it divisible, which would require that the supposed separate form be conjoined with matter. This is a contradiction. This proof is transmitted from the ancient philosophers, called the 'ladder proof' because the two extensions with the line connecting them resemble a ladder. The Sheikh objected: We do not admit the existence of an actually infinite distance; rather, there is an increase to infinity, but that does not entail a single actually infinite distance. Rather, any postulated distance does not exceed that below it except by a finite amount, and so the increase goes to infinity like the series of numbers – their chain goes to infinity but does not entail a single actually infinite number. It was answered: We know necessarily that the existence of the two sides to actual infinity entails the existence of an actually infinite distance between them. Therefore they said: the ladder proof depends on the existence of an actually infinite distance in two dimensions. Had it been infinite in only one dimension, like length, the proof would not apply. For if it did not require an actually infinite gap between the sides, it would not need an actually infinite distance in two dimensions. The analogy with numbers is invalid because the increase there is infinite in the sense of not stopping, but no actually infinite number exists. In short, the increase of the gap according to the extension of the sides is a necessary concomitant of the extension of the sides. So when they extend to infinity, reason judges in summary that the gap is actually infinite there.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a complex proof that corporeal form cannot exist without matter. It is a complete argument with objections and responses. The author first states the thesis: corporeal form is not separable from matter. The proof proceeds by dichotomy: if form existed without matter, it would be either finite or infinite. The infinite case is refuted using the 'ladder proof' (برهان سلمي): from a point, two lines extend infinitely, creating an infinite gap between them; but that gap would be bounded by the lines, which is contradictory. The finite case leads to shape; shape must be either essential to corporeality (resulting in all bodies sharing one shape), or necessary concomitant (also uniform), or accidental (which could change, implying divisibility and thus need for matter). All are impossible, so the original assumption is false, proving inseparability. Then the author notes this proof is from ancients and presents an objection by 'the Sheikh' (likely Ibn Sina): he denies actual infinite distance, arguing that infinite increase does not entail an actually infinite magnitude. The response asserts that necessary conjunction between infinite extension of lines and infinite gap is known intuitively, and the proof holds for two dimensions; the numerical analogy fails because numbers are discrete, not continuous. The response concludes that reason perceives the gap as actually infinite when lines extend infinitely.

Key Terms
السلمي
البرهان
التزايد إلى غير النهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the ladder proof?
A) Infinite corporeal form without matter leads to a contradiction.
B) Finite corporeal form cannot exist without matter.
C) The ladder proof is invalid.
D) All bodies must have the same shape.

Key Takeaway
The ladder proof demonstrates that an infinite corporeal form without matter is impossible because it would entail a bounded infinity, a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar reductio argument: Assume that the universe is infinite in past duration. Show that this leads to an actual infinite number of days, which is impossible by the same reasoning (though note differences between temporal and spatial infinities).

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
The Objection of Ibn Sina and the Defense
Author presents and rebuts Ibn Sina's objection to the ladder proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Sina's objection to the ladder proof and the defender's response, and evaluate whether the rebuttal successfully preserves the proof.

Core Idea
The objection distinguishes potential from actual infinity; the response asserts geometric necessity guarantees actual infinity.

The ladder proof was criticised by Ibn Sina, who argued that infinite increase does not entail an actually infinite distance. This lesson examines that objection and the response from the author, which defends the original proof by grounding the necessity of the gap in the nature of extension.

Guided Path
1
State Ibn Sina's objection
He denies the existence of an actually infinite distance, claiming only a potential infinite increase.
He uses the analogy with infinite number series.
2
Understand his analogy
Like numbers, which increase without end but never become an actually infinite number, the gap between the lines is never actually infinite.
Potential vs actual infinity.
3
Analyse the response
The defender argues that in geometry, the gap necessarily expands with the sides, so infinite sides mean infinite gap.
The gap is a necessary concomitant of the sides.
4
Evaluate why the numeric analogy fails
Numbers are discrete, but lines are continuous and the gap is a necessary concomitant, not an independent sequence.
Continuous vs discrete.
5
Assess the outcome
The proof is strengthened by clarifying that it requires an infinite gap in two dimensions, not just one.
The objection is thus rebutted.

Source Text

بل كل بعد يفرض فهو لا يزيد على ما تحته إلا بقدر متناه وهكذا يذهب التزايد إلى غير النهاية كمراتب الأعداد يذهب سلسلتها إلى غير النهاية، ولا يلزم منه وجود عدد واحد غير متناه بالفعل، بل يلزم التزايد إلى غير النهاية بمعنى لا يقف عند حد وأجيب عنه: أنا نعلم بالضرورة أن وجود الساقين إلى غير النهاية بالفعل يستلزم وجود البعد الغير المتناهي بالفعل بينهما، ولذا قالوا: إن البرهان السلمي منوط على وجود بعد غير النهاية يحكم العقل إجمالا بأن الانفراج غير متناه بالفعل فيه.
Chapter on that corporeal form is not separable from matter: Because if it existed by itself without inhering in matter, then either it is finite or infinite. The second is impossible; because if it were infinite, it would be possible for two lines to emerge from a single assumed point – like point 'A' – in the same direction to infinity, like the sides of a triangle. As the lines increase, the distance between them also increases. So if the sides go to infinity, the distance between them would also be infinite, and also that distance would be bounded between two boundaries (the sides), which is a contradiction. As for the first alternative: if it were finite, it would be surrounded by a boundary or end – either one boundary as in a sphere, or multiple boundaries as in others – and thus it would have a shape; because shape is the state of a body from the perspective of being surrounded by one or more limits. So it would be shaped. That shape is either required by the nature of corporeality itself – impossible, because then all bodies would have the same shape, which is false due to the obvious difference in shapes – the point of necessity: what is required by nature is necessary to it, and what is necessary to the essence is inseparable from every individual of the essence. Or it is due to a necessary concomitant of the essence of corporeality – also impossible, for the same reason of uniformity. Or it is due to an accidental cause – also false, because that accident could cease, and shape could change, making it divisible, which would require that the supposed separate form be conjoined with matter. This is a contradiction. This proof is transmitted from the ancient philosophers, called the 'ladder proof' because the two extensions with the line connecting them resemble a ladder. The Sheikh objected: We do not admit the existence of an actually infinite distance; rather, there is an increase to infinity, but that does not entail a single actually infinite distance. Rather, any postulated distance does not exceed that below it except by a finite amount, and so the increase goes to infinity like the series of numbers – their chain goes to infinity but does not entail a single actually infinite number. It was answered: We know necessarily that the existence of the two sides to actual infinity entails the existence of an actually infinite distance between them. Therefore they said: the ladder proof depends on the existence of an actually infinite distance in two dimensions. Had it been infinite in only one dimension, like length, the proof would not apply. For if it did not require an actually infinite gap between the sides, it would not need an actually infinite distance in two dimensions. The analogy with numbers is invalid because the increase there is infinite in the sense of not stopping, but no actually infinite number exists. In short, the increase of the gap according to the extension of the sides is a necessary concomitant of the extension of the sides. So when they extend to infinity, reason judges in summary that the gap is actually infinite there.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a complex proof that corporeal form cannot exist without matter. It is a complete argument with objections and responses. The author first states the thesis: corporeal form is not separable from matter. The proof proceeds by dichotomy: if form existed without matter, it would be either finite or infinite. The infinite case is refuted using the 'ladder proof' (برهان سلمي): from a point, two lines extend infinitely, creating an infinite gap between them; but that gap would be bounded by the lines, which is contradictory. The finite case leads to shape; shape must be either essential to corporeality (resulting in all bodies sharing one shape), or necessary concomitant (also uniform), or accidental (which could change, implying divisibility and thus need for matter). All are impossible, so the original assumption is false, proving inseparability. Then the author notes this proof is from ancients and presents an objection by 'the Sheikh' (likely Ibn Sina): he denies actual infinite distance, arguing that infinite increase does not entail an actually infinite magnitude. The response asserts that necessary conjunction between infinite extension of lines and infinite gap is known intuitively, and the proof holds for two dimensions; the numerical analogy fails because numbers are discrete, not continuous. The response concludes that reason perceives the gap as actually infinite when lines extend infinitely.

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
The Ladder Proof and Ibn Sina's Objection
Dialectical presentation of ladder proof, objection, and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the ladder proof for the finitude of bodies, identify Ibn Sina's infinite regress objection, and evaluate the response based on the actuality of the two legs.

Core Idea
The ladder proof hinges on actual infinity, not potential regress.

This lesson unpacks a famous argument in Islamic physics and metaphysics: the ladder proof (burhan al-sullami) that all bodies are finite. You will see how the proof uses a geometrical analogy, how Ibn Sina challenges it by distinguishing actual infinity from potential infinite regress, and how the defender replies by insisting on the necessary entailment of actual distance. Understanding this dialectic sharpens your ability to spot equivocation in arguments about infinity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis and initial justification
The passage begins: the corporeal form must be finite or infinite, and infinite is impossible because all observed bodies are finite. This is a generalization from experience. Note that the author treats this as a given premise.
This premise is not argued here; it is taken as evident.
2
Extract the structure of the ladder proof
The proof imagines two lines (legs) extending from a single point, like a ladder's legs. As they extend, the distance between them increases. If they extend infinitely, the distance becomes infinite but is still bounded by the lines—a contradiction. Hence the lines cannot be infinite, so bodies (which have extension) are finite.
The passage only alludes to the proof; the commentary fills in the geometrical contradiction.
3
Locate Ibn Sina's objection
Ibn Sina argues that infinite regress of increases (tazayud) does not imply an actually infinite dimension. Just as the numbers increase without bound but no number is actually infinite, so the distance between the lines can increase without bound without there being a single infinite distance.
This objection hinges on the distinction between potential and actual infinity.
4
Analyze the response
The response asserts that if both legs exist *actually* to infinity (not merely potentially), then the distance between them is a single actually infinite magnitude. This is a necessary concomitant of actual infinite extension. The response reasserts the ladder proof as requiring actual infinity in two directions.
The defender assumes that the ladder proof posits actual existence of the legs qua actually infinite, which Ibn Sina denies.
5
Assess the dialectical outcome
The passage does not declare a winner, but it shows that the dispute turns on whether the ladder proof assumes actual infinity. The proper conclusion is that the proof stands if you accept actual infinite extension; if you reject it, you side with Ibn Sina.
This is a classic moment in Islamic philosophy illustrating the realism-nominalism debate about infinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الصورة الجسمية إما متناهية أو غير متناهية، لا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأن الأجسام كلها متناهية. فذلك الشكل إما أن يكون يقتضيه طبيعة الجسمية لذاتها وهو محال، وإلا لزم أن تكون الأجسام كلها متشكلة بشكل واحد، أو يكون بسبب لازم الماهية الجسمية وهو أيضا محال، أو بسبب عارض وهو أيضا باطل. وهذا البرهان منقول عن قدماء الفلاسفة، ملقب بالسلمي؛ لكون الامتدادين مع الخط الواصل بينهما شبيها به. واعترض عليه الشيخ بأنا لا نسلم وجود بعد غير متناه، بل يلزم التزايد إلى غير النهاية وهو لا يستلزم وجود بعد واحد غير متناه بالفعل، بل كل بعد يفرض فهو لا يزيد على ما تحته إلا بقدر متناه وهكذا يذهب التزايد إلى غير النهاية كمراتب الأعداد. وأجيب عنه: أنا نعلم بالضرورة أن وجود الساقين إلى غير النهاية بالفعل يستلزم وجود البعد الغير المتناهي بالفعل بينهما، ولذا قالوا: إن البرهان السلمي منوط على وجود بعد غير متناه بالفعل في جهتين.
Chapter on that the corporeal form is either finite or infinite; there is no way to the second because all bodies are finite. So that shape either is required by the nature of corporeality itself, which is impossible—otherwise it would necessitate all bodies be shaped with one shape—or it is due to a necessary concomitant of the essence of corporeality, which is also impossible, or due to an accident, which is also false. And this proof is transmitted from the ancient philosophers, nicknamed the 'ladder proof' because the two extensions with the connecting line resemble it. And the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) objected to it by saying: we do not concede the existence of an infinite dimension; rather, there occurs increase without end, and that does not entail the existence of a single actually infinite dimension—rather every dimension supposed does not exceed what is below it except by a finite amount, and so the increase goes on without end like the ranks of numbers. And it was answered: we know necessarily that the existence of the two legs to infinity actually entails the existence of an actually infinite dimension between them, and therefore they said: the ladder proof is predicated on the existence of an actually infinite dimension in two directions.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition of the famous 'ladder proof' (burhan al-sullami) for the finitude of bodies, followed by Ibn Sina's objection and a response. The author begins by stating the proposition that the corporeal form must be either finite or infinite, and the infinite is impossible because all bodies we observe are finite. He then argues that shape (shakl) is either essential to corporeality (requiring all bodies to share one shape, which is false) or accidental (which is also false because accidents can change, but shape here is presented as inseparable), thus the only remaining option is that the corporeal form is finite. The proof is attributed to ancient philosophers and called 'ladder' because it imagines two extensions from a single point, like the legs of a triangle, whose distance increases as they extend; if they extend infinitely, the distance between them would be infinite yet bounded by the two legs—a contradiction. Ibn Sina objects that infinite regress in extension does not imply an actual infinite dimension, just as the series of numbers does not produce an actual infinite number. The response insists that if the two legs exist actually to infinity, then the distance between them is necessarily actually infinite, a necessary concomitant. This illustrates a key epistemological debate about actual versus potential infinity in Aristotelian physics.

Key Terms
الصورة الجسمية
البرهان السلمي
الشكل
التزايد إلى غير النهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the response to Ibn Sina's objection assert that the ladder proof requires an actually infinite dimension in two directions?
A) Because the two legs exist simultaneously, so the distance between them is a single actual magnitude.
B) Because the ladder analogy only works for one direction.
C) Because Ibn Sina admitted infinite regress.
D) Because the ancient philosophers defined infinity differently.

Key Takeaway
The ladder proof stands or falls on whether you admit actual infinite extension; Ibn Sina's objection shifts the debate from physical demonstration to metaphysical commitment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the ladder proof as a formal argument: write its premises and conclusion, and identify the hidden assumption that Ibn Sina challenges.
?
Role-play the dialectic: one student defends the ladder proof (mudda'i), another objects as Ibn Sina (mu'tarid), a third responds as the defender (jawab). Use the passage to support each position.

The Logical Impossibility of Hyle as a Line, Surface, or Body
Author proves that if hyle were stripped of form, it cannot be a line, surface, or body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the compound syllogistic argument from Hidayat al-Hikma proving that hyle stripped of form cannot be a line, surface, or body.

Core Idea
Overlapping of substances is impossible; this principle refutes each dimensional possibility for hyle.

This passage from Hidayat al-Hikma systematically examines the consequences if the formless substrate (hyle) were divisible. The author considers three possibilities — line, surface, body — and shows each leads to absurdity. You will learn how he uses the impossibility of overlapping to rule out the line, and how the body case leads to either change of essence or self-composition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trilemma
The author states that if hyle were divisible, it must be a line (1D), surface (2D), or body (3D). Each is refuted in turn. Isolate this three-part structure.
2
Follow the refutation of the line
The argument uses a conditional: if hyle were a substantial line, then two surfaces meeting at its ends would either be prevented from touching or not. Not preventing leads to overlapping (tadaakhul), which is impossible. Therefore the line must prevent, but that gives it thickness, contradicting its nature.
The impossibility of overlapping is the key premise.
3
Examine the refutation of the body
The author splits 'body' into two meanings: corporeal form or composite. If hyle were corporeal form itself, that would be a change of essence. If composite, then hyle would be composed of hyle and form — self-composition, which is impossible.
These are two separate syllogisms within a compound structure.
4
Analyze the objection and response
An objector claims lines lack width so overlapping is not impossible. The commentator replies that overlapping is impossible for all substances absolutely, not just those with width, because overlapping means union in place and volume.
This clarifies the scope of the tadaakhul principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يتجزى، ولا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأنها حينئذ إما أن تنقسم في جهة واحدة فتكون خطا، أو في جهتين فتكون سطحا جوهريا، أو في ثلاث جهات فتكون جسما، وكل واحد منها باطل. أما أنه لا يجوز أن تكون خطا فلأن وجود الخط على سبيل الاستقلال محال؛ لأنه إذا انتهى إليه طرفا السطحين، فإما أن يحجب تلاقيهما أو لا يحجب، لا جائز أن لا يحجب، وإلا لزم تداخل الخطوط، وهو محال؛ لأن كل خطين مجموعهما أعظم من الواحد، والتداخل
جسما أقول : لو كان الهيولى على تقدير تجردها قابلة للانقسام في جهات ثلاث لزم كونها جسما، إما بمعنى الصورة الجسمية أو بمعنى المركب من الهيولى والصورة الجسمية، فعلى التقدير الأول: يلزم انقلاب الماهية أي صيرورة الهيولى المجردة عين الصورة الجسمية التي فرض تجردها عنها، وعلى الثاني: يلزم تركب الشيء من نفسه ومن غيره أي تركب الهيولى من الهيولى والصورة الجسمية، وهو باطل؛ ضرورة امتناع جزئية الشيء لنفسه والمصنف بني كلامه على الشق الثاني، ولذا قال: لكانت مركبة من الهيولى والصورة". طرفا السطحين: أقول: الهيولى على تقدير كونها خطا جوهريا، إما أن تكون خطا مستقيما أو غيره، فعلى الأول: يوضع بين السطحين المستقيمي الأطراف، وعلى الثاني: بين السطحين الغير المستقيمي الأطراف تداخل الخطوط: قيل عليه: إن الخط لا عظم له في العرض وامتناع التداخل إنما هو في الجهة التي له عظم في تلك الجهة، أجيب عنه بأن امتناع التداخل في الجواهر محال مطلقا، سواء كان لها عظم أو لا. التداخل معنى التداخل اتحاد الشيئين في الحيز والحجم، فأما إذا كانا ذوي وضع بالذات فاستحالته بديهية وأما إذا كانا ذوي وضع بالعرض، فإنما يمتنع التداخل في الجانب الذي له عظم فيه، وأما في الجانب الذي ليس فيه حجم فلا يمتنع.
[It is] indivisible, and the first [alternative] is impossible; because then it would either divide in one dimension and become a line, or in two dimensions and become a substantial surface, or in three dimensions and become a body, and each of these is false. As for that it is not permissible for it to be a line: because the existence of a line independently is impossible; for if the two ends of the two surfaces terminate at it, then either it prevents their meeting or not. It is not allowed that it does not prevent, otherwise overlapping of lines would follow, which is impossible because for any two lines, their sum is greater than one, and overlapping... I say: If hyle, on the assumption of its being stripped [of form], were divisible in three dimensions, it would be a body, either in the sense of the corporeal form or in the sense of the composite of hyle and corporeal form. On the first supposition, a change of essence would follow, i.e., the stripped hyle would become identical to the corporeal form from which it was assumed to be stripped. On the second, the composition of a thing from itself and another would follow, i.e., the composition of hyle from hyle and corporeal form, which is false due to the impossibility of a thing being a part of itself. The author built his statement on the second branch, and therefore said: 'It would be composed of hyle and form.' The two ends of the surfaces: I say: Hyle, on the assumption of being a substantial line, is either a straight line or other. In the first case, it is placed between straight-surfaced surfaces, and in the second, between non-straight-surfaced surfaces. Overlapping of lines: It was objected: 'A line has no width, and the impossibility of overlapping is only in the dimension in which it has width.' The answer was: 'The impossibility of overlapping in substances is absolute, whether they have width or not.' Overlapping means the union of two things in place and volume. So if they have position essentially, its impossibility is self-evident. But if they have position accidentally, overlapping is only impossible in the dimension in which there is magnitude; in the dimension in which there is no volume, it is not impossible.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage continues the proof that hyle cannot be stripped of form by considering what it would be if it were divisible: a line, surface, or body. Each is refuted. A line cannot exist independently because it would lead to overlapping of lines, which is impossible. The commentator then explains that if hyle were divisible in three dimensions, it would be a body, leading to either a change of essence (if taken as corporeal form itself) or self-composition (if taken as composite), both absurd. An objection about lines and overlapping is addressed: overlapping is impossible for substances absolutely, not just those with width. The definition of overlapping (tadaakhul) is given as union in place and volume, clarifying that accidental position does not prevent overlapping in the dimension without magnitude.

Refining the Causal Argument: Second-Figure Syllogism on Matter and Form
Author analyzes and refines the causal relation between matter and form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the second-figure categorical syllogism used to argue that matter is not the efficient cause of form, and explain the logical refinement that drops the qualification 'efficient'.

Core Idea
Negating efficient cause does not negate causality; a refined syllogism denies causality altogether.

In this passage from Hidayat al-Hikma, the commentator addresses a confusion about the causal relation between matter and form. The initial argument uses a second-figure syllogism to show that matter is not an efficient cause. However, an objection points out that this only negates efficient causality, not causality as such. The commentator then refines the argument by dropping the qualification, leading to a stronger conclusion. This lesson walks you through the syllogism, the objection, and the refinement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim
The author aims to prove that matter is not the cause of form. The initial argument uses the principle that the efficient cause must precede its effect.
2
Extract the syllogism
The text presents: 'Matter does not exist before form; the efficient cause of a thing exists before it; therefore matter is not an efficient cause of form.' Formalize this as: Major premise: All efficient causes exist before their effects. Minor premise: Matter does not exist before form. Conclusion: Matter is not an efficient cause. This is a second-figure syllogism (middle term = 'exists before effect').
The figure is determined by the middle term being the predicate in both premises.
3
Analyze the objection
The objection states that the minor premise only shows temporal priority, but efficient causality requires essential priority. Therefore, the argument does not disprove that matter could be an efficient cause in an essential sense.
The objection is about the truth of the premise, not the validity of the syllogism.
4
Examine the refinement
The commentator suggests dropping the qualification 'efficient' and using a general major premise: 'The cause of a thing must exist before it.' This yields the conclusion that matter is not a cause of form at all, thus avoiding the loophole.
This refined syllogism is also valid in the second figure (Camestres), but its major premise may be contested for receptive causes.
5
Evaluate the overall argument
Assess the logical strength: The original syllogism is valid but relies on a specific definition of efficient cause. The refinement strengthens the conclusion but requires accepting that all causes precede their effects, which is debatable for receptive causes.
Notice the passage concludes by conceding matter's causality for individual form, but not for form in general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هداية لما ثبت التلازم بين الهيولى والصورة، ولا بد للتلازم من علاقة علية بأن تكون - إحداهما علة للأخرى، أو كلتاهما معلولتي ثالث ولم يعلم من قبل أن التلازم بينهما على أي وجه هو، تحير ذهن المتعلم واشتبه الأمر عليه، ولإزالة ذلك الوهم والاشتباه أورد هداية وسماها "هداية"؛ لأن الاشتباه نوع من الضلالة، والتفصي عنه دلالة على المطلوب. علة والمراد بالعلة الفاعلية كما يدل عليه قوله : " والعلة الفاعلية للشيء يجب" إلخ. قيل عليه: إن ما مر إنما يدل على امتناع تقدم الهيولى على الصورة بالزمان، وذا لا يدل على عدم كونها علة للصورة؛ لأن تقدم العلة على المعلول ليس بالزمان بل بحسب الذات ويمكن أن يجاب عنه أن الهيولى علة قابلية والقابلة لا تكون فاعلة، وفيه نظر لجواز أن يكون الشيء قابلا وفاعلا من جهتين كما قال المصنف في علم البارئ: "إنه ارتسامي والبارئ قابل له وفاعل". والعلة الفاعلية هذا استدلال من الشكل الثاني تحريره الهيولى ليست موجودة قبل الصورة، والعلة الفاعلية للشيء تكون موجودة قبله فالهيولى ليست علة فاعلية للصورة، وفيه أنه لا يلزم من نفي العلة الفاعلية نفي العلة مطلقا؛ لجواز أن تكون علة قابلية أو غيرها، فالأولى حذف قيد الفاعلية والاكتفاء على العلة بأن يقال: " والعلة للشيء يجب أن تكون موجودة قبله فينتج أن الهيولى ليست علة. فإن قيل: المراد نفي العلة الفاعلية لا نفي العلية مطلقا؛ فإن الهيولى علة قابلية للصورة، فكيف يتصور نفي كونها علة قابلية؟ قلت: إن علية الهيولى للأشياء إنما تتصور بعد وجودها، وسيحيء أن الهيولى تحتاج إلى الصورة في وجودها، فأني يتصور كونها علة قابلية للصورة؟ نعم عليتها للصورة الشخصية مسلمة، ولكن الكلام ههنا في الصورة مع قطع النظر عن التشخص؛ لأن الصورة الشخصية ليست متلازمة للهيولى إلا أن يكون المراد بالصورة فردا منتشرا منها، فيمكن التلازم بينه وبين الهيولى.
Guidance: When the concomitance between matter and form is established, and concomitance requires a causal relation - either one of them is a cause of the other, or both are effects of a third - and it was not known beforehand which one it is, the mind of the learner became confused and the matter was ambiguous. To remove that doubt and ambiguity, he presented a guidance and called it 'guidance' because ambiguity is a type of misguidance, and extrication from it is a guide to what is sought. Cause: And what is meant by efficient cause, as indicated by his statement: 'The efficient cause of a thing must exist before it' etc. It was objected to him: What was mentioned only indicates the impossibility of matter's temporal priority over form, and that does not indicate that matter is not a cause of form, because the priority of cause over effect is not temporal but rather essential. And it can be answered that matter is a receptive cause, and the receptive cannot be an agent. But there is a consideration in this because it is possible for a thing to be receptive and agent from two aspects, as the author said in the science of the Creator: 'It is impression-based and the Creator is receptive to it and agent.' And the efficient cause: This is a deduction from the second figure. Its formulation: Matter does not exist before form, and the efficient cause of a thing must exist before it, so matter is not an efficient cause of form. But there is in it that the negation of efficient cause does not entail the negation of cause absolutely, because it could be a receptive cause or something else. So it is better to drop the qualification of efficiency and suffice with cause, saying: 'The cause of a thing must exist before it', so it results that matter is not a cause. If it is said: The intent is the negation of efficient cause, not the negation of causality absolutely; for matter is a receptive cause of form, so how can one conceive the negation of its being a receptive cause? I say: The causality of matter for things is only conceived after their existence, and it will come that matter needs form for its existence, so how can one conceive it being a receptive cause of form? Yes, its causality for the individual form is conceded, but the discussion here is about form irrespective of individuation; because the individual form is not concomitant with matter unless the intended form is an individual instance, then concomitance between it and matter is possible.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the causal relationship between matter and form. It begins by noting that once the concomitance (تلازم) between matter and form is established, there must be a causal relation. The learner is confused about whether matter is a cause of form or vice versa. The author's 'guidance' (هداية) aims to resolve this. The main point is about the efficient cause. The text quotes the principle that an efficient cause must precede its effect. An objection is raised: the preceding is temporal, but the argument only shows material priority, not efficient causality. A possible answer is that matter is a receptive cause, not an efficient cause. But this is criticized because something can be both receptive and efficient from different aspects, as the author himself said about God's knowledge. Then the text analyzes the syllogism used: it is from the second figure (Matter does not exist before form; efficient cause exists before effect; therefore matter is not efficient cause). However, this only negates efficient cause, not causality absolutely. So it is better to drop the qualification and say matter is not a cause at all. Further discussion considers whether matter is a receptive cause, and concludes that matter's causality for form is problematic because matter itself needs form for existence. Ultimately, the causality of matter for the individual form is conceded, but the discussion is about form in general, not individual.

العلة الفاعلية
العلة القابلية

Parsing a Conditional Reductio Argument on Natural Places
Author presents a reductio ad absurdum that a body cannot have two natural places.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse a conditional reductio argument by identifying the antecedent, consequent, and the contradiction (khalf) in a classical text.

Core Idea
A reductio argument shows that an assumption leads to a contradiction, proving it false.

In this passage, the author uses conditional reasoning to refute the idea that a single body can have two natural places. By showing that each assumed natural place would lead to a contradiction if not sought, the argument demonstrates that the initial assumption is impossible. This is a classic use of the method of reduction to absurdity (burhan al-khalf), widely used in Islamic theology and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial assumption
The argument is built on a hypothesis that the author intends to refute: that a body has two natural places. Look for the implied assumption behind the conditional statements.
The assumption is not explicitly stated in the passage but is the target of the reductio.
2
Locate the conditional statements
The passage contains at least one explicit conditional: 'If it is not seeking the second, then the second place is not natural.' (وإن لم يكن طالبا للثاني يلزم أن لا يكون الحيز الثاني طبعيا). There is also an implied first conditional about the first place.
Recognize the structure: 'if... then...' (إن... يلزم...).
3
Examine the contradiction (khalf)
The consequent of the conditional is contrasted with the assumption that the place is natural (وقد فرضناه طبعيا). This creates a contradiction: the conclusion that the place is not natural conflicts with the assumption that it is natural.
The term 'خلف' refers to this contradiction.
4
Understand the reductio logic
Since each conditional leads to a contradiction under the initial assumption, the initial assumption must be false. This is the core of the reductio: denying the assumption because it entails an impossibility.
This is valid reasoning: if P implies Q, and Q is false, then P is false. Here, the assumption implies a contradiction, which is false, so the assumption is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الشكل الذي حصل فيه طبعيا، وقد فرضناه طبعيا، هذا خلف، وإن لم يكن طالبا للثاني يلزم أن لا يكون الحيز الثاني طبعيا، وقد فرضناه طبعيا، هذا خلف.
Section on shape. That which occurred in it naturally, and we assumed it natural, this is a contradiction. And if it is not seeking the second, it follows that the second place is not natural, and we assumed it natural, this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents a syllogistic argument about natural place (حيز طبعي). The author considers the case of a body that supposedly has two natural places. He argues by conditional reasoning: if the body does not seek the first natural place, then the first is not natural (contradiction); if it does not seek the second, then the second is not natural (contradiction). Since both alternatives lead to contradiction, the assumption that a body has two natural places is false. This is a standard argument by reduction to absurdity (خلف). Note that the heading says 'فصل في الشكل' but the content concerns place, which may indicate a textual error or that the section covers both shape and place.

Key Terms
حيز طبعي
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The second place is natural.
B) The second place is not natural.
C) The first place is not natural.
D) The body has no natural place.

Key Takeaway
The reductio argument (khalf) is a powerful tool in Islamic logic: assume the opposite of what you want to prove, derive a contradiction, and thus establish the original claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Parsing a Categorical Syllogism: The Body's Natural Shape
Author constructs a categorical syllogism to prove that every body has a natural shape.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the three premises and conclusion of a categorical syllogism from a kalam text and verify its validity using the Barbara mood.

Core Idea
The natural shape of a body is proven through a chain of categorical reasoning that strips away external constraints.

In this passage, the author presents a tightly structured proof that every body has a natural shape. The argument is a classic example of categorical syllogism used in kalam. By parsing its premises and checking its logical form, you will learn to recognize valid syllogistic reasoning in Islamic theological texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion (natija)
The conclusion is the statement the author is trying to prove. Look for the sentence after 'therefore' or the main claim. Here it is 'Every body has a natural shape.'
The conclusion is stated at the beginning and end of the syllogism.
2
Locate the premises (muqaddimat)
The premises are the supporting statements. They follow 'because' and are linked by 'and'. The three explicit premises are: (1) Every body is finite, (2) Everything finite is shaped, (3) Everything shaped has a natural shape.
The first premise was established earlier; the author provides justification for the second and third.
3
Determine the middle term (hadd al-awsat)
The middle term connects the premises and does not appear in the conclusion. Identify which term is shared between the premises. Here, 'shaped' (متشكل) appears in premises 2 and 3, and 'finitude' appears in premises 1 and 2. However, the core syllogism can be extracted as: major premise 'Everything shaped has a natural shape', minor premise 'Every body is shaped', conclusion as above. The middle term is 'shaped'.
The argument actually uses a sorites, but the essential categorical syllogism is in the first figure (Barbara).
4
Verify the figure (shakl) and mood (darb)
Identify the position of the middle term. In the extracted syllogism, the middle term 'shaped' is the subject of the major premise and the predicate of the minor premise. This places it in the first figure. Both premises are universal affirmatives (A propositions), so the mood is Barbara. First figure Barbara is always valid.
Check that the conclusion is also a universal affirmative: 'Every body has a natural shape'.
5
Analyze the justifications for each premise
The author defends each premise: finiteness of bodies was previously proven; finiteness implies being bounded, hence shaped; naturalness of shape is shown by assuming removal of external constraints, leaving the body's own nature.
These justifications ensure the premises are true, which is necessary for a sound argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الشكل كل جسم فله شكل طبعي؛ لأن كل جسم متناه، وكل متناه فهو متشكل، وكل متشكل فله شكل طبعي، فكل جسم فله شكل طبعي أما أن كل جسم متناه فلما مر، وأما أن كل متناه فهو متشكل؛ فلأنه يحيط به حد واحد أو حدود، فيكون متشكلا، وإنما قلنا: أن كل متشكل فله شكل طبعي؛ لأنا لو فرضنا ارتفاع القواسر لكان على شكل معين، وذلك الشكل إما أن يكون لطبعه أو لقاسر، لا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأنا فرضنا عدم القواسر ، فإذن هو عين طبعه، وهو المطلوب. الحيز الثاني: لأنه ليس فيه ولا يطلبه، والحيز الطبعي هو الذي يطلبه إذا كان خارجا عنه، وبالجملة : إذا كان الجسم ما حيزان لزم أحد الخلفين، إما أن لا يكون الأول طبعيا وإما أن لا يكون الثاني طبعيا، والتالي باطل فالمقدم مثله.
Section on shape. Every body has a natural shape, because every body is finite, and everything finite is shaped, and everything shaped has a natural shape. So every body has a natural shape. As for every body being finite, it has been mentioned. As for everything finite being shaped, it is because it is surrounded by one or more boundaries, so it becomes shaped. And we said: everything shaped has a natural shape, because if we assume the removal of external constraints, it would have a specific shape; that shape is either from its nature or from a constraint. The second is impossible because we assumed the absence of constraints. Therefore it is its very nature, which is what was sought. The second place: because it is not in it nor does it seek it, and the natural place is that which it seeks when outside of it. In summary: if a body has two places, one of two contradictions follows: either the first is not natural or the second is not natural. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage contains a well-structured categorical syllogism. The author first states the conclusion: every body has a natural shape. Then he provides the syllogism: (1) Every body is finite. (2) Everything finite is 'shaped' (i.e., has a shape). (3) Everything shaped has a natural shape. Therefore, every body has a natural shape. The justification for each premise is given: the finite nature of bodies was previously established; finiteness implies being bounded, hence shaped; and the naturalness of shape is proven by considering that if external constraints are removed, the body would assume a definite shape, which must be due to its own nature since no external force remains. Following this, there is an additional argument about natural place (الحيز الثاني), which appears to be a repetition of an earlier point. The author argues that if a body had two natural places, it would lead to a contradiction: the body would not seek one of them, making it unnatural. This is a reductio ad absurdum. However, this argument about place may be out of place here, as the section is ostensibly about shape.

The Definition and Properties of Direction (Jiha)
Defines direction with three essential properties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three essential properties of a direction according to Hidayat al-Hikma and extract the definition from the passage.

Core Idea
Direction is a real entity with position, indivisible along the motion dimension.

This lesson unpacks the precise definition of 'direction' used in the proof that the celestial sphere is spherical. The author establishes that a direction must be existent, have position, and be indivisible. Understanding this definition is key to following the subsequent syllogistic argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the Arabic term being defined. It is 'جهة' (jiha), direction. The passage begins by listing its attributes.
2
Extract the three properties
The author states: 'موجودة ذات وضع غير منقسمة في امتداد مأخذ الحركة' – existing, having position, indivisible in the dimension of motion. List these three.
3
Justify each property from the passage
Existence and position: without them, pointing to direction is impossible. Indivisibility: if divisible, the moving body would face contradictory outcomes (moves from or to the goal).
The justification for indivisibility is a conditional argument.
4
Contrast relative vs absolute above/below
The commentary explains relative usage (e.g., Zayd above the bed) vs. true above/below (above which no above, below which no below). The passage uses the absolute sense.
5
Understand why indivisibility is necessary
If direction were divisible, motion would involve parts, but direction is the endpoint of motion; hence it must be indivisible. This preserves the logical integrity of direction.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني في الفلكيات
فصل في إثبات كون الفلك
واحدة منهما موجودة ذات وضع غير منقسمة في امتداد مأخذ الحركة، ومتى كان كذلك كان الفلك مستديرا، وإنما قلنا: إن الجهة موجودة ذات وضع؛ لأنها لو لم تكن كذلك لما أمكنت الإشارة إليها ولما أمكن اتجاه المتحرك إليها، وإنما قلنا: إنها غير منقسمة؛ لأنها لو انقسمت ووصل المتحرك إلى أقرب الجزئين من الجهة وتحرك، فإما أن يتحرك من المقصد أو إلى المقصد، فإن تحرك من المقصد لم يكن أبعد الجزئين من الجهة، وإن تحرك إلى المقصد لم يكن أقرب الجزئين من الجهة
=
إنها صارت فوقا، وللرأس إنه صار تحتا، والفوق والتحت قد يستعملان بالإضافة إلى بعض الأجسام دون بعض فيقال: زيد فوق السرير وتحت السقف، ثم إذا صعد السقف صار السقف تحته وصار هو فوق السقف، وبهذا الاستعمال ما هو فوق بالقياس إلى الجسم تحت بالقياس إلى جسم، وبالعكس، وقد يستعملان بمعناهما الحقيقيين والفوق بهذا المعنى فوق ليس فوقه فوق والتحت هذا المعنى تحت ليس تحته تحت، والمقصود ههنا الحقيقي ولذا قال المصنف لا تتبدلان. ولما كان امتداد العالم متناهيا؛ لما مر من امتناع وجود بعد غير متناه كان لامتداد العالم طرفان فالطرف الأعلى وهو سطح فلك الأفلاك يسمى فوقا، والأسفل وهو مركز الأرض يسمى تحتا.
اتجاه المتحرك لأن القرب والوصول إلى المعدوم لا يجوز؛ لأن المعدوم ليس بشيء، والقرب والوصول يستدعيان الوجود. غير منقسمة: أي الجهة غير منقسمة لا يقع فيها الحركة؛ لأن الجهة ما منه الحركة أو ما إليه الحركة، فلو كانت منقسمة في ذلك الجانب فمتى وصل المتحرك إلى أقرب الجزئين من الجهة، فلا يخلو إما يرجع أو يذهب، فإن رجع فلا يكون أبعد الجزئين من الجهة؛ لأن أبعد الجزئين ليس بشيء منهما، وإن ذهب فلا يكون أقرب الجزئين من الجهة؛ لأنه ليس مما منه الحركة بل صار داخلا في المسافة هذه.
وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: تحدد الجهات ليس في خلاء؛ لاستحالته، ولا في ملأ متشابه، وإلا لما كانت الجهتان مختلفتين بالطبع، فلا يكون إحداهما مطلوبة والأخرى متروكة، هذا خلف، فإذا تحدد الجهات في أطراف ملأ وهو الجسم الذي لا يوجد فيه أمور مختلفة الحقيقة كالخط والسطح والنقطة، قالوا: وهو الجسم الغير المتناهي، وإنما تعرضوا له مع أنه باطل بالبرهان السلمي وغيره؛ تنبيها على أن تحدد الجهات ليس موقوفا على امتناعه، بل لو وجد فرضا وتقديرا لا يكون تحدد الجهات به بل بجسم منتهاه كري. كانت حاصله: أن تحدد الجهات لا يمكن أن يكون في ملأ متشابه؛ لأنه لا يوجد فيه أمور مختلفة الحقائق والجهات مختلفة بالماهية والطبيعة كالفوق والتحت الحقيقين يأبى كل واحد منهما بالنظر إلى الطبيعة أن يصدق على الآخر، فلو كان يتحدد الجهات في ملأ متشابه لما كانت الجهتان مختلفتين بالطبع؛ لانتفاء حدود مختلفة بالطبع فيه فيلزم منه أن لا يكون بعض الجهات لبعض الأجسام مطلوبا، ولبعض الآخر مهروبا عنه، وقد ثبت من قبل أن لكل جسم حيزا طبعيا، وأيضا إنا قد نرى بعض الأجسام كالأرض والماء طالبا للتحت وهاربا عن الفوق، والبعض الآخر كالهواء والنار طالبا للفوق هاربا عن التحت، فلو كانت الجهات متشابهة لارتفعت هذه الأحكام الضرورية وإذا كانت الجهات ذات وضع غير منقسمة في امتداد مأخذ الحركة لا بد أن تكون أطرافا ونهايات للجسم؛ لامتناع وجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزى والخط الجوهري والسطح الجوهري، وذلك الجسم المحدد للجهات لا بد أن يكون كريا؛ لأن غير الكري لا يوجد فيه نقطة تكون نسبة جميع الأبعاد إليها سواء، فلا يتحدد به جهة التحت؛ لأنها عبارة عن غاية البعد بالنسبة إلى فوق، وإنما قلنا: إنها غاية البعد؛ لأنه لو كان بعده أبعد منه لكان بالنسبة إليه فوقا، فلا تكون تحتا حقيقيا، وقد كان الكلام فيه.
هذا خلف أي إذا ثبت أن الجهتين مختلفتان بالطبع، ولا يوجد في الملأ المتشابه أمور مختلفة، يكون تحدد الجهات في أطراف ونهايات لا تكون من الملا.
The second art on celestial bodies. Chapter on proving the celestial sphere. One of them exists having position, indivisible in the dimension of the direction of motion, and whenever it is thus, the celestial sphere is circular. We said: The direction exists having position because if it were not so, pointing to it would not be possible, nor would the direction of the moving body toward it be possible. We said: It is indivisible because if it divided and the moving body reached the nearest of the two parts of the direction and moved, then either it moves from the goal or toward the goal. If it moves from the goal, it is not the farther part from the direction; if it moves toward the goal, it is not the nearest part from the direction. = It became 'above', and for the head it became 'below'. 'Above' and 'below' are sometimes used relative to some bodies but not others: it is said Zayd is above the bed and below the ceiling. Then when he climbs the ceiling, the ceiling becomes below him and he becomes above the ceiling. In this usage, what is above relative to one body is below relative to another, and vice versa. They are also used in their true meanings: 'above' in this sense is an above above which there is no above, and 'below' in this sense is a below below which there is no below. The intended here is the true meaning, hence the author said: they do not change. Since the extension of the world is finite—due to what has passed about the impossibility of infinite extension—the extension of the world has two ends: the upper end, which is the surface of the sphere of spheres, is called 'above', and the lower end, which is the center of the earth, is called 'below'. Direction of the moving body because proximity and arrival at the non-existent are not permissible; for the non-existent is not a thing, and proximity and arrival require existence. Indivisible: i.e., the direction is indivisible; motion does not occur in it because the direction is that from which motion is or to which motion is. If it were divided in that side, when the moving body reaches the nearest of the two parts of the direction, it either returns or goes on. If it returns, it is not the farther part of the direction because the farther part is not a thing from them; if it goes on, it is not the nearest part of the direction because it is not that from which motion is, but rather enters into this distance. If this is established, we say: The determination of directions is not in a vacuum, because that is impossible, nor in a homogeneous medium, for then the two directions would not be different by nature, so one of them would not be sought and the other avoided—this is a contradiction. So the determination of directions is at the ends of a medium, which is the body that does not contain things of different realities, like line, surface, and point. They said: It is the infinite body, and they mentioned it despite its falsity by the proof of the ladder and others, as a reminder that the determination of directions is not dependent on its impossibility; rather, if it existed hypothetically, the determination of directions would not be by it but by a body whose end is spherical. Its result: The determination of directions cannot be in a homogeneous medium because it contains no things of different realities, while directions are different in essence and nature, like true above and true below; each of them, by nature, refuses to be true of the other. So if directions were determined in a homogeneous medium, the two directions would not be different by nature, because of the absence of different natural limits in it. This entails that some directions would not be sought by some bodies and avoided by others, but it has been established earlier that every body has a natural place, and we also see some bodies like earth and water seeking below and fleeing above, and others like air and fire seeking above and fleeing below. If directions were similar, these necessary judgments would be nullified. And if directions have position and are indivisible in the dimension of the direction of motion, they must be edges and ends of the body, due to the impossibility of the existence of the indivisible part and the substantial line and substantial surface. That body which determines the directions must be spherical, because in a non-spherical body there is no point such that the ratio of all distances to it is equal, so the direction of below cannot be determined by it; for it is defined as the utmost distance relative to above. We said it is the utmost distance because if there were a distance farther than it, that would be above relative to it, and it would not be true below. This has been discussed. This is a contradiction: i.e., if it is established that the two directions are different by nature, and in a homogeneous medium there are no different things, then the determination of directions is at edges and ends that are not from the medium.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a compound argument aimed at proving that the celestial sphere (falak) must be spherical. It begins by defining the nature of direction (jiha). The author states that a direction is something that exists (mawjuda), has a position (dhat wad'), and is indivisible (ghayr munqasima) along the dimension of motion (imtidad ma'khadh al-haraka). This definition is justified: if direction had no position, it could not be pointed to and a moving body could not be oriented toward it. If it were divisible, then when a moving body reaches one part, it must either move away from or toward the goal, leading to inconsistency—it would either not be the farthest part or not the nearest part. The commentary then clarifies the true meanings of 'above' (fawq) and 'below' (taht), contrasting them with relative usage. The true above is that above which there is no further above; similarly for true below. These are fixed and do not swap. Because the world's extension is finite, its ends are the upper surface of the outermost sphere and the center of the earth. The argument then proceeds: the determination (tahaddud) of directions cannot occur in a vacuum (khala') or in a homogeneous medium (mala' mutashabih). In a homogeneous medium, there are no different realities, so directions would not differ by nature, contradicting the observed fact that some bodies (e.g., earth, water) naturally seek below and flee above, while others (air, fire) do the opposite. Hence, directions must be determined at the ends of a body that contains diverse realities—like lines, surfaces, points—not in an infinite body (which is impossible) but in a spherical body. The final deduction: directions, being indivisible endpoints of motion, force the containing body to have edges; and only a spherical body has a point equidistant from all boundaries, making it suitable to define true below as the farthest point relative to above. Thus, the celestial sphere must be spherical.

فوق/تحت حقيقي
غير منقسمة

Key Takeaway
Direction is real, positional, and indivisible; these properties are foundational for proving the celestial sphere's shape.

The Syllogism

Proving the Celestial Sphere Is Spherical via Direction Determination
Proves celestial sphere spherical via direction determination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the compound syllogistic argument that the celestial sphere must be spherical, using the determination of directions.

Core Idea
Directions cannot be in a vacuum or homogeneous medium, only at the ends of a spherical body.

Now that we understand direction, the author builds a multi-step proof. He shows that directions must be determined at the ends of a heterogeneous body, and such a body must be spherical. This leads to the conclusion that the celestial sphere (the outermost body) is spherical.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall claim
The passage aims to prove that the celestial sphere (الفلك) is spherical (مستدير/كري). Look for the concluding statement.
The claim is hinted at the beginning: 'متى كان كذلك كان الفلك مستديرا'.
2
First sub-argument: directions are determined at the ends of a body
The author argues that determination of directions (تحدد الجهات) cannot be in a vacuum (خلاء) or in a homogeneous medium (ملأ متشابه). If in a homogeneous medium, directions would not differ by nature, contradicting observed natural motion (e.g., earth seeks below, fire seeks above). Hence directions must be at the ends of a heterogeneous body.
The heterogeneous body contains diverse realities like lines, surfaces, points.
3
Second sub-argument: that body must be spherical
The body that determines directions must have a point equidistant from all boundaries. Only a spherical body has such a point. In a non-spherical body, no point has equal ratio to all dimensions, so true below cannot be defined as the utmost distance from above.
The passage says: 'غير الكري لا يوجد فيه نقطة تكون نسبة جميع الأبعاد إليها سواء'.
4
Combine the premises
The celestial sphere is the body that determines directions (since it contains the ends of the world: above = surface of outermost sphere, below = center of earth). Therefore, it must be spherical.
The passage implies the celestial sphere is the body in question.

الفن الثاني في الفلكيات
فصل في إثبات كون الفلك
واحدة منهما موجودة ذات وضع غير منقسمة في امتداد مأخذ الحركة، ومتى كان كذلك كان الفلك مستديرا، وإنما قلنا: إن الجهة موجودة ذات وضع؛ لأنها لو لم تكن كذلك لما أمكنت الإشارة إليها ولما أمكن اتجاه المتحرك إليها، وإنما قلنا: إنها غير منقسمة؛ لأنها لو انقسمت ووصل المتحرك إلى أقرب الجزئين من الجهة وتحرك، فإما أن يتحرك من المقصد أو إلى المقصد، فإن تحرك من المقصد لم يكن أبعد الجزئين من الجهة، وإن تحرك إلى المقصد لم يكن أقرب الجزئين من الجهة
=
إنها صارت فوقا، وللرأس إنه صار تحتا، والفوق والتحت قد يستعملان بالإضافة إلى بعض الأجسام دون بعض فيقال: زيد فوق السرير وتحت السقف، ثم إذا صعد السقف صار السقف تحته وصار هو فوق السقف، وبهذا الاستعمال ما هو فوق بالقياس إلى الجسم تحت بالقياس إلى جسم، وبالعكس، وقد يستعملان بمعناهما الحقيقيين والفوق بهذا المعنى فوق ليس فوقه فوق والتحت هذا المعنى تحت ليس تحته تحت، والمقصود ههنا الحقيقي ولذا قال المصنف لا تتبدلان. ولما كان امتداد العالم متناهيا؛ لما مر من امتناع وجود بعد غير متناه كان لامتداد العالم طرفان فالطرف الأعلى وهو سطح فلك الأفلاك يسمى فوقا، والأسفل وهو مركز الأرض يسمى تحتا.
اتجاه المتحرك لأن القرب والوصول إلى المعدوم لا يجوز؛ لأن المعدوم ليس بشيء، والقرب والوصول يستدعيان الوجود. غير منقسمة: أي الجهة غير منقسمة لا يقع فيها الحركة؛ لأن الجهة ما منه الحركة أو ما إليه الحركة، فلو كانت منقسمة في ذلك الجانب فمتى وصل المتحرك إلى أقرب الجزئين من الجهة، فلا يخلو إما يرجع أو يذهب، فإن رجع فلا يكون أبعد الجزئين من الجهة؛ لأن أبعد الجزئين ليس بشيء منهما، وإن ذهب فلا يكون أقرب الجزئين من الجهة؛ لأنه ليس مما منه الحركة بل صار داخلا في المسافة هذه.
وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: تحدد الجهات ليس في خلاء؛ لاستحالته، ولا في ملأ متشابه، وإلا لما كانت الجهتان مختلفتين بالطبع، فلا يكون إحداهما مطلوبة والأخرى متروكة، هذا خلف، فإذا تحدد الجهات في أطراف ملأ وهو الجسم الذي لا يوجد فيه أمور مختلفة الحقيقة كالخط والسطح والنقطة، قالوا: وهو الجسم الغير المتناهي، وإنما تعرضوا له مع أنه باطل بالبرهان السلمي وغيره؛ تنبيها على أن تحدد الجهات ليس موقوفا على امتناعه، بل لو وجد فرضا وتقديرا لا يكون تحدد الجهات به بل بجسم منتهاه كري. كانت حاصله: أن تحدد الجهات لا يمكن أن يكون في ملأ متشابه؛ لأنه لا يوجد فيه أمور مختلفة الحقائق والجهات مختلفة بالماهية والطبيعة كالفوق والتحت الحقيقين يأبى كل واحد منهما بالنظر إلى الطبيعة أن يصدق على الآخر، فلو كان يتحدد الجهات في ملأ متشابه لما كانت الجهتان مختلفتين بالطبع؛ لانتفاء حدود مختلفة بالطبع فيه فيلزم منه أن لا يكون بعض الجهات لبعض الأجسام مطلوبا، ولبعض الآخر مهروبا عنه، وقد ثبت من قبل أن لكل جسم حيزا طبعيا، وأيضا إنا قد نرى بعض الأجسام كالأرض والماء طالبا للتحت وهاربا عن الفوق، والبعض الآخر كالهواء والنار طالبا للفوق هاربا عن التحت، فلو كانت الجهات متشابهة لارتفعت هذه الأحكام الضرورية وإذا كانت الجهات ذات وضع غير منقسمة في امتداد مأخذ الحركة لا بد أن تكون أطرافا ونهايات للجسم؛ لامتناع وجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزى والخط الجوهري والسطح الجوهري، وذلك الجسم المحدد للجهات لا بد أن يكون كريا؛ لأن غير الكري لا يوجد فيه نقطة تكون نسبة جميع الأبعاد إليها سواء، فلا يتحدد به جهة التحت؛ لأنها عبارة عن غاية البعد بالنسبة إلى فوق، وإنما قلنا: إنها غاية البعد؛ لأنه لو كان بعده أبعد منه لكان بالنسبة إليه فوقا، فلا تكون تحتا حقيقيا، وقد كان الكلام فيه.
هذا خلف أي إذا ثبت أن الجهتين مختلفتان بالطبع، ولا يوجد في الملأ المتشابه أمور مختلفة، يكون تحدد الجهات في أطراف ونهايات لا تكون من الملا.
The second art on celestial bodies. Chapter on proving the celestial sphere. One of them exists having position, indivisible in the dimension of the direction of motion, and whenever it is thus, the celestial sphere is circular. We said: The direction exists having position because if it were not so, pointing to it would not be possible, nor would the direction of the moving body toward it be possible. We said: It is indivisible because if it divided and the moving body reached the nearest of the two parts of the direction and moved, then either it moves from the goal or toward the goal. If it moves from the goal, it is not the farther part from the direction; if it moves toward the goal, it is not the nearest part from the direction. = It became 'above', and for the head it became 'below'. 'Above' and 'below' are sometimes used relative to some bodies but not others: it is said Zayd is above the bed and below the ceiling. Then when he climbs the ceiling, the ceiling becomes below him and he becomes above the ceiling. In this usage, what is above relative to one body is below relative to another, and vice versa. They are also used in their true meanings: 'above' in this sense is an above above which there is no above, and 'below' in this sense is a below below which there is no below. The intended here is the true meaning, hence the author said: they do not change. Since the extension of the world is finite—due to what has passed about the impossibility of infinite extension—the extension of the world has two ends: the upper end, which is the surface of the sphere of spheres, is called 'above', and the lower end, which is the center of the earth, is called 'below'. Direction of the moving body because proximity and arrival at the non-existent are not permissible; for the non-existent is not a thing, and proximity and arrival require existence. Indivisible: i.e., the direction is indivisible; motion does not occur in it because the direction is that from which motion is or to which motion is. If it were divided in that side, when the moving body reaches the nearest of the two parts of the direction, it either returns or goes on. If it returns, it is not the farther part of the direction because the farther part is not a thing from them; if it goes on, it is not the nearest part of the direction because it is not that from which motion is, but rather enters into this distance. If this is established, we say: The determination of directions is not in a vacuum, because that is impossible, nor in a homogeneous medium, for then the two directions would not be different by nature, so one of them would not be sought and the other avoided—this is a contradiction. So the determination of directions is at the ends of a medium, which is the body that does not contain things of different realities, like line, surface, and point. They said: It is the infinite body, and they mentioned it despite its falsity by the proof of the ladder and others, as a reminder that the determination of directions is not dependent on its impossibility; rather, if it existed hypothetically, the determination of directions would not be by it but by a body whose end is spherical. Its result: The determination of directions cannot be in a homogeneous medium because it contains no things of different realities, while directions are different in essence and nature, like true above and true below; each of them, by nature, refuses to be true of the other. So if directions were determined in a homogeneous medium, the two directions would not be different by nature, because of the absence of different natural limits in it. This entails that some directions would not be sought by some bodies and avoided by others, but it has been established earlier that every body has a natural place, and we also see some bodies like earth and water seeking below and fleeing above, and others like air and fire seeking above and fleeing below. If directions were similar, these necessary judgments would be nullified. And if directions have position and are indivisible in the dimension of the direction of motion, they must be edges and ends of the body, due to the impossibility of the existence of the indivisible part and the substantial line and substantial surface. That body which determines the directions must be spherical, because in a non-spherical body there is no point such that the ratio of all distances to it is equal, so the direction of below cannot be determined by it; for it is defined as the utmost distance relative to above. We said it is the utmost distance because if there were a distance farther than it, that would be above relative to it, and it would not be true below. This has been discussed. This is a contradiction: i.e., if it is established that the two directions are different by nature, and in a homogeneous medium there are no different things, then the determination of directions is at edges and ends that are not from the medium.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a compound argument aimed at proving that the celestial sphere (falak) must be spherical. It begins by defining the nature of direction (jiha). The author states that a direction is something that exists (mawjuda), has a position (dhat wad'), and is indivisible (ghayr munqasima) along the dimension of motion (imtidad ma'khadh al-haraka). This definition is justified: if direction had no position, it could not be pointed to and a moving body could not be oriented toward it. If it were divisible, then when a moving body reaches one part, it must either move away from or toward the goal, leading to inconsistency—it would either not be the farthest part or not the nearest part. The commentary then clarifies the true meanings of 'above' (fawq) and 'below' (taht), contrasting them with relative usage. The true above is that above which there is no further above; similarly for true below. These are fixed and do not swap. Because the world's extension is finite, its ends are the upper surface of the outermost sphere and the center of the earth. The argument then proceeds: the determination (tahaddud) of directions cannot occur in a vacuum (khala') or in a homogeneous medium (mala' mutashabih). In a homogeneous medium, there are no different realities, so directions would not differ by nature, contradicting the observed fact that some bodies (e.g., earth, water) naturally seek below and flee above, while others (air, fire) do the opposite. Hence, directions must be determined at the ends of a body that contains diverse realities—like lines, surfaces, points—not in an infinite body (which is impossible) but in a spherical body. The final deduction: directions, being indivisible endpoints of motion, force the containing body to have edges; and only a spherical body has a point equidistant from all boundaries, making it suitable to define true below as the farthest point relative to above. Thus, the celestial sphere must be spherical.

ملأ متشابه

Key Takeaway
The celestial sphere is spherical because only a sphere can provide a unique fixed point for direction determination, and directions cannot be determined in a vacuum or homogeneous medium.
