The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Celestial Sphere Must Be Spherical: A Compound Argument
Author proves that the body determining directions is spherical, whether single or multiple.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the compound proof that the celestial sphere is spherical, identifying the disjunctive syllogism and the sub-arguments for single and multiple bodies.

Core Idea
Sphericity is proven by the need for a fixed direction of 'below' through a disjunctive syllogism.

In this passage, the author presents a rigorous proof that the celestial sphere (falak) must be spherical. The argument is a compound syllogism: it first divides the possible ways directions are determined (by one body or many), then shows each case leads to sphericity. Even an objection involving multiple bodies in a spherical arrangement is addressed. This lesson will guide you through unpacking this dense argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall disjunctive structure
The argument begins by stating that the determination of directions (like 'below') is either by a single body or by multiple bodies. This disjunction sets up the main proof.
The concept of 'below' (جهة السفل) is defined as the farthest distance from the center or from above.
2
Analyze the single-body case
If directions are determined by a single body, that body must be spherical. Why? Because only a sphere has a unique center where all surface points are equidistant, fixing 'below' as a constant farthest distance. A non-spherical body would cause 'below' to vary relative to different parts, contradicting its fixed nature.
3
Analyze the multiple-body case with surrounding
If multiple bodies determine directions, they must surround each other (i.e., be concentric). Then only the outermost body matters; the inner ones are redundant. The outermost must be spherical by the same reasoning as the single-body case.
This entails that the celestial sphere is the outermost body.
4
Analyze the multiple-body case without surrounding
If the bodies do not surround each other, no single point can serve as the sole reference for 'below'. Any proposed farthest point would be closer to another body, so direction would not be fixed. Hence, this case is impossible.
The author uses a thought experiment with lines connecting bodies to show the inconsistency.
5
Consider the objection and its resolution
The author notes a potential counterexample: multiple bodies arranged spherically with a common orientation (bases up, heads down) could determine directions. This objection is acknowledged but does not undermine the main conclusion, as it still requires an overall spherical arrangement.
This shows the depth of the argument: anticipating and addressing objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إثبات كون الفلك بجسم ونهايات خارجة عن الملأ المتشابه، ومتى كان كذلك كان تحددها كري؛ لأن تحددها إما أن يكون بجسم واحد أو بأكثر، فإن كان واحد وجب أن يكون كريا؛ لأن الجسم الذي ليس بكري لا يتحدد به جهة السفل؛ لأن جهة السفل غاية البعد، وإلا لتبدلت بالنسبة إلى ما هو أبعد منه، ولا يتحدد به غاية البعد فلا يتحدد به جهة السفل، وإن كان بأجسام متعددة وجب أن يحيط بعضها ببعض، وإلا لم يتعين بها غاية البعد؛ لأن ما هو أبعد عن بعضها فهو أقرب من الآخر، وكلما يفرض غاية البعد عن بعضها لم يكن غاية البعد عن المجموع أبعد ما إلخ أي البعد الذي هو أبعد من جهة السفل فلا تكون جهة السفل؛ لأن الكلام ههنا في الجهة التي لا تتبدل. أن يحيط لو كان تحدد الجهات بأجسام متعددة لوجب أن يكون بعض الأجسام محيطا ببعض آخر فيكون الجسم المحيط محددا للجهات بمركزه ومحيطه، ويصير الحشو لغوا في التحديد، ويكون المحيط كريا، وإلا لم يتحدد به جهة السفل كما مر، وإن لم يحط بعض الأجسام ببعض آخر لم يتحدد بها غاية البعد؛ لأن البعد الذي هو عن بعضها فهو أقرب من بعض آخر؛ لأن الخط الذي خرج عن واحد، ووصل بالآخر، يتصل طرفه الآخر بجسم آخر، فذلك الطرف الآخر أبعد من الجسم الذي خرج الخط منه، وأقرب من جسم آخر، فلا يكون غاية البعد عن كل منهما فلا يكون جهته؛ لأن الجهة عبارة عن غاية البعد لئلا يلزم التبدل وبالجملة على تقدير تحديد الجهات بأجسام متعددة كل بعد يفرض أنه غاية البعد عن بعض الأجسام لا يكون غاية البعد عن المجموع؛ لكونه غاية القرب من بعض آخر وههنا بحث: لأنه لو كان الأجسام المتعددة واقعة على هيئة الاستدارة، وتجعل قواعدها فوقا، ورؤوسها تحتا، لجاز تحدد الجهات بها ولم يلزم عدم تحدد الجهات بهذه الصورة.
Chapter on proving that the celestial sphere is a body. And extremities outside the homogeneous plenum, and when it is so, their determination is spherical. Because their determination is either by one body or by more. If it is one, it must be spherical, because a non-spherical body does not determine the direction of below, for the direction of below is the utmost distance, otherwise it would change relative to what is farther from it, and the utmost distance is not determined by it, so the direction of below is not determined by it. And if it is by multiple bodies, they must surround each other, otherwise the utmost distance is not determined by them. Because what is farther from some is closer to another, and whenever one posits the utmost distance from some, it is not the utmost distance from the whole. 'Aba'd ma' etc., i.e., the distance that is farther than the direction of below, so it would not be the direction of below, because the discussion here is about the direction that does not change. Surrounding: if the determination of directions is by multiple bodies, then some bodies must surround others. Then the surrounding body determines the directions by its center and circumference, and the filler becomes superfluous in determination, and the surrounding must be spherical, otherwise the direction of below is not determined by it, as previously. And if some bodies do not surround others, the utmost distance is not determined by them, because the distance from some is closer from another; because the line that goes out from one and reaches another, its other end connects to another body, so that other end is farther from the body from which the line came, and closer to another body, so it is not the utmost distance from each of them, hence not their direction. For direction is an expression of the utmost distance, to avoid change. In summary, on the supposition of determination of directions by multiple bodies, every distance supposed to be the utmost distance from some bodies is not the utmost distance from the whole, because it is the utmost closeness to another. And here there is a discussion: because if the multiple bodies are in a spherical arrangement, and their bases are made above and their heads below, it would be possible for directions to be determined by them, and the lack of determination would not follow in this form.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the celestial sphere must be a spherical body. The author presents a disjunctive argument: the determination of directions (e.g., below) is either by a single body or multiple bodies. If by a single body, it must be spherical, because a non-spherical body lacks a point equidistant from all parts, so it cannot provide a fixed 'below' as the farthest distance. If by multiple bodies, they must surround each other; the outermost body becomes spherical and determines directions, rendering the inner bodies irrelevant. If they do not surround each other, then no single point can serve as the center for all, so the direction of below would vary with reference point, contradicting its fixed nature. The author also notes a potential objection: if multiple bodies are arranged spherically with a common orientation (e.g., all bases up and heads down), directions could be determined. This shows the author anticipates objections and refines the argument. The style is typical of a philosophical commentary (sharh) expanding on a concise matn.

Conditional Proof for Sphericity of the Celestial Sphere
Author proves that if directions are determined by the celestial sphere, it must be spherical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional argument for the sphericity of the celestial sphere and identify its modus tollens structure.

Core Idea
Only a sphere can fix a unique direction of low; thus a single-body celestial sphere must be spherical.

In this passage, the author demonstrates a fundamental proof in Aristotelian-Islamic cosmology: the celestial sphere's sphericity follows from its role as the determinant of directions. This is a tightly reasoned conditional syllogism that illustrates how logical inference is applied to physical principles. We will break it down step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main conditional claim
Find the statement: 'If it is one, then it must be spherical.' This is the conclusion the author proves. Note the antecedent (determination by one body) and consequent (spherical).
2
Define the direction of low
The passage defines 'direction of low' as the extreme distance (ghayat al-bu'd). Understand that a non-spherical body cannot have a unique farthest point; the farthest point would vary with reference.
3
Recognize the intermediate conditional
The author argues: 'A body that is not spherical does not determine the direction of low.' This is a key premise: if not spherical, then not determine low.
4
Apply modus tollens
Since the celestial sphere does determine directions (including the direction of low), it cannot be non-spherical. Therefore, it must be spherical. This is modus tollens: if not spherical then not determine low, but it does determine low, so not not spherical (i.e., spherical).
5
Summarize the logical structure
The overall argument: (1) If the celestial sphere is not spherical, it does not determine the direction of low. (2) The celestial sphere does determine the direction of low. (3) Therefore, the celestial sphere is spherical.
This matches the conditional reasoning form with modus tollens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إثبات كون الفلك بجسم ونهايات خارجة عن الملأ المتشابه، ومتى كان كذلك كان تحددها كري؛ لأن تحددها إما أن يكون بجسم واحد أو بأكثر، فإن كان واحد وجب أن يكون كريا؛ لأن الجسم الذي ليس بكري لا يتحدد به جهة السفل؛ لأن جهة السفل غاية البعد، وإلا لتبدلت بالنسبة إلى ما هو أبعد منه، ولا يتحدد به غاية البعد فلا يتحدد به جهة السفل
Chapter on proving that the celestial sphere is a body and that its ends are outside the homogeneous plenum. Whenever that is the case, its determination (of directions) is spherical. Because its determination is either by one body or by more. If it is one, then it must be spherical, because a body that is not spherical does not determine the direction of low. Because the direction of low is the extreme distance, otherwise it would change relative to what is farther than it, and it does not determine the extreme distance, so it does not determine the direction of low.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the celestial sphere is spherical based on its role in determining directions. The author first asserts that the celestial sphere's ends are outside the homogeneous plenum, implying it has boundaries. He then states that when this is the case, the determination of directions must be spherical. The proof proceeds by enumerating two possibilities: determination by one body or by multiple bodies. In this lesson, we examine the first possibility: if determination is by a single body, then that body must be spherical. The reasoning is that a non-spherical body cannot determine the direction of low (the extreme distance). The direction of low is defined as the farthest point away. If the body were not spherical, then what is considered low would change depending on the point of reference, and there would be no fixed extreme distance. Thus, only a sphere can unambiguously determine the direction of low. This is a fundamental argument in Aristotelian cosmology for the spherical shape of the heavens.

The Conditional Argument for the Sphericity of the Celestial Sphere
Author argues that multiple bodies determining directions must surround each other, and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional argument for celestial sphericity and identify its antecedent, consequent, and the objection-response exchange.

Core Idea
If multiple bodies determine directions, they must be nested and spherical — the conditional proves the sphere's necessity.

In this passage, the author argues that whether direction is determined by one or multiple bodies, the celestial sphere must be spherical. He uses a conditional syllogism: if multiple bodies determine directions, they must surround each other; otherwise the extreme distance is ambiguous. An objection about circular arrangement is raised and answered, reinforcing the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional claim
Find the statement that expresses a conditional relationship: 'If multiple bodies determine directions, then some bodies surround others.'
This is the main thesis being defended.
2
Locate antecedent and consequent
The antecedent is 'determination of directions by multiple bodies' and the consequent is 'some bodies surround others'.
The author justifies the consequent by showing that otherwise no extreme distance is determined.
3
Understand the proof of the conditional
The proof uses modus tollens: if bodies do not surround each other, then no unique extreme distance exists, which contradicts the assumption that directions are determined.
Thus, the conditional is necessary.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The objector suggests a circular arrangement where bodies do not surround each other. The author responds that even in that case, the outermost body surrounds the inner ones, so the conditional holds.
The objection fails to refute the conclusion.

وإن كان بأجسام متعددة وجب أن يحيط بعضها ببعض، وإلا لم يتعين بها غاية البعد؛ لأن ما هو أبعد عن بعضها فهو أقرب من الآخر، وكلما يفرض غاية البعد عن بعضها لم يكن غاية البعد عن المجموع أبعد ما إلخ أي البعد الذي هو أبعد من جهة السفل فلا تكون جهة السفل؛ لأن الكلام ههنا في الجهة التي لا تتبدل. أن يحيط لو كان تحدد الجهات بأجسام متعددة لوجب أن يكون بعض الأجسام محيطا ببعض ،آخر فيكون الجسم المحيط محددا للجهات بمركزه ومحيطه، ويصير الحشو لغوا في التحديد، ويكون المحيط كريا، وإلا لم يتحدد به جهة السفل كما مر، وإن لم يحط بعض الأجسام ببعض آخر لم يتحدد بها غاية البعد؛ لأن البعد الذي هو عن بعضها فهو أقرب من بعض آخر؛ لأن الخط الذي خرج عن واحد، ووصل بالآخر، يتصل طرفه الآخر بجسم آخر، فذلك الطرف الآخر أبعد من الجسم الذي خرج الخط منه، وأقرب من جسم آخر، فلا يكون غاية البعد عن كل منهما فلا يكون جهته؛ لأن الجهة عبارة عن غاية البعد لئلا يلزم التبدل وبالجملة على تقدير تحديد الجهات بأجسام متعددة كل بعد يفرض أنه غاية البعد عن بعض الأجسام لا يكون غاية البعد عن المجموع؛ لكونه غاية القرب من بعض آخر وههنا بحث: لأنه لو كان الأجسام المتعددة واقعة على هيئة الاستدارة، وتجعل قواعدها فوقا، ورؤوسها تحتا، لجاز تحدد الجهات بها ولم يلزم عدم تحدد الجهات بهذه الصورة. فيجب أن يكون بعضها محيطا بالآخر، فحصل المطلوب.
And if it is by multiple bodies, then it is necessary that some of them surround others; otherwise the extreme distance would not be determined by them, because what is farther from some of them is closer to another, and whenever one assumes an extreme distance from some of them, it is not the extreme distance from the totality. [Commentary: 'A farther what, etc.' meaning the distance that is farther than the direction of low, so it is not the direction of low; because the discussion here is about the direction that does not change. 'That they surround' – if the determination of directions is by multiple bodies, then some bodies must surround others. Then the surrounding body determines the directions by its center and circumference, and the interior becomes irrelevant in determination, and the surrounding body is spherical; otherwise, the direction of low would not be determined by it, as has been mentioned. And if some bodies do not surround others, the extreme distance would not be determined by them, because the distance from some of them is closer to others; because a line that goes out from one and reaches another, its other end connects to another body, so that other end is farther from the body from which the line came and closer to another body, so it is not the extreme distance from each of them, and thus not its direction; because direction is defined as extreme distance to avoid change. In summary, on the assumption of direction determination by multiple bodies, every distance assumed to be the extreme distance from some bodies is not the extreme distance from the totality, because it is the extreme closeness to some others. And here is a discussion: because if the multiple bodies are arranged in a circular shape, and their bases are placed above and their heads below, then it would be permissible for directions to be determined by them, and the failure of direction determination would not follow. Therefore, some of them must surround others, and the required is obtained.]
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage examines the second possibility for direction determination: multiple bodies. The author argues that these bodies must surround each other; otherwise no single extreme distance can be determined. The commentary elaborates: if multiple bodies do not surround each other, then a line drawn from one to another will have endpoints at different distances, so no endpoint is truly the extreme from all bodies. An objection is then raised: suppose the multiple bodies are arranged circularly with their bases at the top and heads at the bottom; in that case, directions could be determined. The answer is that in such an arrangement, the bodies must still surround each other (i.e., one is inside another), so the outermost body is spherical. Thus, the required conclusion—that the celestial sphere is spherical—is established, regardless of whether direction determination is by one or multiple bodies.

The celestial sphere must be spherical because direction determination requires a unique extreme distance, which only a surrounding spherical body can provide.

The Syllogism

Proving the Celestial Sphere is Simple via Conditional Syllogism
Author proves celestial sphere is simple via conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and validate the conditional syllogism used to prove the celestial sphere's simplicity.

Core Idea
The celestial sphere's simplicity is proven by its lack of rectilinear motion.

The author argues that the celestial sphere cannot accept rectilinear motion because it determines directions, and anything that moves rectilinearly has directions determined prior to it. Thus, it must be simple. In this lesson, you will break down the conditional reasoning and verify its validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main conclusion
The passage aims to prove that the celestial sphere (falak) is simple (basit). Locate the final statement affirming simplicity.
The conclusion is: 'فلك بسيط'
2
Extract the first conditional chain
Find the conditional: 'If a body accepts rectilinear motion, then directions are determined prior to it.' Then note the observation that the celestial sphere determines directions (negation of consequent).
This is a modus tollens inference: ¬Q → ¬P, given P→Q and ¬Q.
3
Extract the second conditional chain
Identify the conditional: 'If a body does not accept rectilinear motion, then it is simple.' Combine with the intermediate conclusion (no rectilinear motion) to derive simplicity via modus ponens.
P→Q, P, therefore Q.
4
Formalize both inferences
Write the conditionals in abstract form using variables, then test validity with truth tables or logical rules.
Ensure the argument is valid: modus tollens and modus ponens are both valid forms.
5
Consider the alternative (reductio)
The passage also includes a brief argument that if the celestial sphere were composite, its parts would be spherical, which is not observed. Analyze this as an additional support.
This is a separate conditional: if composite then parts spherical; but parts not spherical; therefore not composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الفلك بسيط أي لم يتركب من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع؛ لأنه لا يقبل الحركة المستقيمة، ومتى كان كذلك كان بسيطا، أما أنه لا يقبل الحركة المستقيمة فلأن كل ما يقبل الحركة المستقيمة فإنه متجه إلى جهة وتارك لأخرى، وكل ما هذا شأنه فالجهات متحددة قبله لا به والفلك ليس كذلك، بل يتحدد به الجهات فلا يكون قابلا للحركة المستقيمة، ومتى كان كذلك وجب أن يكون بسيطا؛ إذ لو كان مركبا فإما أن يكون كل واحد من أجزائه على شكل طبعي أو قسري، لا سبيل إلى الأول وإلا لكان كل واحد منها كريا؛ لأن الشكل الطبعي للبسيط هو الكري كما علمت
Chapter on that the celestial sphere is simple, i.e., it is not composed of bodies with different natures, because it does not accept rectilinear motion. Whenever that is the case, it is simple. As for it not accepting rectilinear motion, it is because everything that accepts rectilinear motion is directed to one direction and leaving another, and everything with this property has directions determined prior to it, not by it. But the celestial sphere is not like that; rather, directions are determined by it, so it cannot accept rectilinear motion. When that is the case, it must be simple. For if it were composite, then either each of its parts would have a natural shape or a forced shape. The first is impossible because then each part would be spherical, since the natural shape of a simple body is spherical, as you have learned.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage proves that the celestial sphere is simple (not composed of different natures). The proof is based on its lack of rectilinear motion. The author argues that any body that moves rectilinearly must have directions determined prior to it—it is seeking one direction and leaving another, so directions exist independently of it. The celestial sphere, however, determines directions itself (as established in the previous sections), so it does not move rectilinearly. Hence, it is simple. Additional support is given: if it were composite, its parts would either have a natural shape or a forced shape. The natural shape of a simple body is spherical, so if the parts were naturally shaped, each would be a sphere, which is not observed. Thus, the celestial sphere must be simple. This argument relies on the earlier conclusion that the celestial sphere determines directions and that a body with rectilinear motion cannot be the determiner of directions.

Conditional syllogisms, especially modus tollens and modus ponens, are the backbone of many Islamic theological proofs.

The Syllogism

The Syllogistic Proof of Celestial Circular Motion
Author proves that the celestial sphere is capable of circular motion, using the nature of its parts and the concept of spherical surface.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism that proves the celestial sphere undergoes circular motion and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
The sphere's inability to undergo rectilinear motion forces it to accept circular motion.

This passage from Hidayat al-Hikma presents a logical proof that the celestial sphere (falak) undergoes circular motion, not rectilinear. The author uses a case analysis: either the parts are spherical or not. Both possibilities lead to contradictions, leaving circular motion as the only coherent option. We will translate this into a categorical syllogism and test its validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The text aims to prove: 'the celestial sphere is capable of circular motion'. Find the phrase: 'فلك قابل للحركة المستديرة'.
2
Extract the two conditional premises
(a) If parts were spherical, a continuous spherical surface is impossible (gap argument). (b) If parts are non-spherical, each part would seek its natural shape via rectilinear motion, implying the whole sphere would be capable of rectilinear motion.
These are the two possibilities the author considers.
3
Formulate the categorical syllogism
Major premise: Everything that cannot have rectilinear motion and cannot consist of spherical parts is capable of circular motion. Minor premise: The celestial sphere cannot have rectilinear motion (since it determines directions) and cannot consist of spherical parts (due to gap argument). Conclusion: Therefore, the celestial sphere is capable of circular motion.
This is a simplified representation; the original argument is more complex but fits this form.
4
Test validity
The syllogism is in the second figure (Camestres-like). The middle term 'lacks rectilinear motion and spherical parts' appears as predicate in major and subject in minor. The mood is valid (AEE-2). Check that both premises are true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الفلك قابل للحركة المستديرة لأن كل جزء من أجزائه المفروضة فيه لا يختص بما يقتضي حصول تحقيقه في بحث الشكل، فلو كان كل واحد من أجزائه على شكل كري لاستحال أن يحصل من مجموع كرات سطح كري متصل؛ لأن تماس الكرة بالكرة لا يكون إلا بنقطة، ويبقى فرج فيما بينهما، ولا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأن أجزاءه تكون طالبة للشكل الطبعي فيلزم أن يكون الفلك قابلا للحركة المستقيمة، وهذا خلف. الكرة: وهو الجسم الذي يكون جميع الأبعاد الخارجة من نقطة مفروضة في وسطه متساوية. سطح كري: لأن تماس الكرة بالكرة على نقطة، ويبقى فيما بينهما فرج، فكيف يحصل من مجموع الكرات سطح كري متصل؟. طالبا: والطلب يستلزم الحركة المستقيمة، فيكون كل واحد قابلاً للحركة المستقيمة، فيلزم أن يكون الفلك قابلاً للحركة المستقيمة لأن طلب كل واحد منها للشكل الطبعي يستدعي كة يجتمع أجزاؤه التي كانت متفرقة في الكل، وذلك لا يخلو عن حر مستقيمة، فلما كان كل واحد من أجزاء الفلك قابلا للحركة المستقيمة لكان الفلك أيضا قابلا للحركة المستقيمة وهذا خلف؛ لأنه محدد للجهات، ولا شيء من محدد للجهات بقابل للحركة المستقيمة، فالفلك ليس بقابل للحركة المستقيمة. للحركة وهي لا يفارق كل الجسم مما هو كل وضعه ومكانه ويفارق كل واحد من أجزاء هوضعه ومكانه لو كان له مكان، وإن لم يكن له مكان فالتبدل في الوضع فقط، كالفلك
Section on that the celestial sphere is capable of circular motion, because each part of its supposed parts is not distinguished by something that necessitates its actualization in the investigation of shape. For if each of its parts were in a spherical shape, it would be impossible for a continuous spherical surface to arise from the aggregate of spheres, because contact of sphere with sphere is only at a point, and a gap remains between them. And the second possibility is not possible, because its parts would be seeking the natural shape, which would necessitate that the celestial sphere is capable of rectilinear motion, and this is a contradiction. The sphere: It is a body from which all distances from a hypothetical point in its center are equal. Spherical surface: Because contact of sphere with sphere is at a point, and a gap remains between them, so how can a continuous spherical surface arise from the aggregate of spheres? Seeking: And seeking necessitates rectilinear motion, so each part would be capable of rectilinear motion, so it would necessitate that the celestial sphere is capable of rectilinear motion, because the seeking of each part for its natural shape necessitates that its parts, which were separated in the whole, come together, and that is not free from rectilinear motion. Since each part of the celestial sphere is capable of rectilinear motion, the celestial sphere would also be capable of rectilinear motion, and this is a contradiction because it determines directions, and nothing that determines directions is capable of rectilinear motion. So the celestial sphere is not capable of rectilinear motion. For the motion [circular], it does not depart the whole body as a whole from its position and place, but each of its parts departs from its position and place if it had a place; and if it had no place, then the change is only in position, like the celestial sphere.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a syllogistic proof that the celestial sphere (falak) is capable of circular motion (haraka mustadira). The author argues by first considering the parts of the sphere. If each part were spherical (as natural for simple bodies), then from many spheres a continuous spherical surface cannot be formed because spheres touch only at points, leaving gaps. So the parts cannot all be spherical. Nor can they be non-spherical, because then each part would seek its natural spherical shape through rectilinear motion, which is incompatible with the sphere's nature as a direction-determining body. Therefore, the only remaining possibility is that the sphere as a whole is capable of circular motion, which allows it to persist as a continuous body without rectilinear motion. The author also defines the sphere and explains that circular motion involves a change in position (wad‘) without translation, as seen in the celestial sphere's rotation.

Key Terms
حركة مستديرة
كرة
سطح كري
وضع

A) Because spheres cannot combine to form a continuous spherical surface.
B) Because spheres are not natural shapes.
C) Because each part would then be capable of rectilinear motion.
D) Because spheres only rotate, contradicting celestial motion.

Key Takeaway
The celestial sphere's circular motion is deduced by eliminating the possibilities of spherical parts or rectilinear motion.

Defining the Sphere and Its Motion
Author proves that the celestial sphere is capable of circular motion, using the nature of its parts and the concept of spherical surface.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of a sphere and distinguish between motion in place and motion in position.

Core Idea
The sphere is defined by equal radii; its motion is a change in position, not place.

The passage provides a precise definition of the sphere and clarifies the nature of its motion. In Islamic physics, motion is not only translation but also rotation (change in position). This lesson unpacks these definitions and their logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Find the definition of 'sphere'
Locate: 'الكرة: وهو الجسم الذي يكون جميع الأبعاد الخارجة من نقطة مفروضة في وسطه متساوية.' This gives the hadd of the sphere.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
Genus: body (jism). Differentia: all distances from a central point are equal.
The definition is complete and clear.
3
Analyze the definition's completeness
Does it include all spheres and exclude non-spheres? Yes: any body with equal radii is a sphere, and only spheres have that property.
4
Understand the concept of motion in position
The text explains that circular motion involves change in position (wad‘) not place. For the whole sphere, it remains in the same place; parts change position relative to each other. This distinguishes rotation from translation.
This is crucial for understanding celestial motion.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Proof that the Celestial Sphere Cannot Move Rectilinearly
Author proves sphere's motion is circular by eliminating rectilinear motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism proving that the celestial sphere is not receptive to rectilinear motion and therefore must move circularly.

Core Idea
The sphere determines directions, so rectilinear motion is impossible for it.

The author begins by establishing that the celestial sphere (al-falak) cannot undergo rectilinear motion. He uses the fact that the sphere determines the directions of the universe, and anything that does so cannot move rectilinearly because that would disrupt its orienting function. He also argues that the sphere's parts are simple and equal in nature, so no part has a fixed position, allowing only positional change without translation – i.e., circular motion. This argument is a clear example of a categorical syllogism used in Islamic natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main conclusion
The conclusion the author aims to prove is that the celestial sphere is receptive to circular motion. He first eliminates rectilinear motion.
2
Extract the key premise about directions
The author states that the sphere determines the directions (محدد للجهات). He then asserts that nothing which determines directions is receptive to rectilinear motion.
This is a universal negative premise.
3
Form the categorical syllogism
Using the premise that no determinant of directions is receptive to rectilinear motion, and the minor that the sphere is a determinant of directions, the conclusion follows that the sphere is not receptive to rectilinear motion.
This is a first-figure syllogism (Celarent).
4
Understand the alternative: circular motion
Since the sphere cannot move rectilinearly and must move (it does move, as known from astronomy), the only possible motion is circular. The author also explains that the sphere's parts are equal in nature, so no part has a fixed position, allowing each part to change position without the whole moving place – i.e., positional motion (حركة وضعية), which is circular.

فصل في أن الفلك قابل للحركة المستديرة لأن كل جزء من أجزائه المفروضة فيه لا يختص بما يقتضي حصول تحقيقه في بحث الشكل، فلو كان كل واحد من أجزائه على شكل كري لاستحال أن يحصل من مجموع كرات سطح كري متصل؛ لأن تماس الكرة بالكرة لا يكون إلا بنقطة، ويبقى فرج فيما بينهما، ولا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأن أجزاءه تكون طالبة للشكل الطبعي فيلزم أن يكون الفلك قابلا للحركة المستقيمة، وهذا خلف. الكرة: وهو الجسم الذي يكون جميع الأبعاد الخارجة من نقطة مفروضة في وسطه متساوية. سطح كري: لأن تماس الكرة بالكرة على نقطة، ويبقى فيما بينهما فرج، فكيف يحصل من مجموع الكرات سطح كري متصل؟. طالبا: والطلب يستلزم الحركة المستقيمة، فيكون كل واحد قابلاً للحركة المستقيمة، فيلزم أن يكون الفلك قابلاً للحركة المستقيمة المستقيمة لأن طلب كل واحد منها للشكل الطبعي يستدعي كة يجتمع أجزاؤه التي كانت متفرقة في الكل، وذلك لا يخلو عن حر مستقيمة، فلما كان كل واحد من أجزاء الفلك قابلا للحركة المستقيمة لكان الفلك أيضا قابلا للحركة المستقيمة وهذا خلف؛ لأنه محدد للجهات، ولا شيء من للجهات بقابل للحركة المستقيمة، فالفلك ليس بقابل للحركة المستقيمة. للحركة وهي لا يفارق كل الجسم مما هو كل وضعه ومكانه ويفارق كل واحد من أجزاء هوضعه الحركة الوضعية، وقد عرفت في بحث الحركة معناها، وحاصلة أن وضعه ومكانه لو كان له مكان، وإن لم يكن له مكان فالتبدل في الوضع فقط، كالفلك وضع معين ومحاذاة متعينة لتساوي الأجزاء في الطبيعة، فكل جزء الذي ليس وراءه جسم آخر حتى يكون له مكان بل له وضع بالنسبة إلى ما تحته. لتساوي حاصله : أنه لا يكون الجزء من أجزاء الفلك اختصاص بوضع معين ومحاذاة معينة من جهة الطبيعة؛ لأن الفلك بسيط كما تقرر من قبل، والبسيط لا يكون فيه قوى متخالفة، فلما كان بسيطا كان جميع الأجزاء متساوية في الطبيعة، فيمكن أن ينتقل كل جزء منه عن وضعه؛ لعدم لزوم ذلك الوضع له، فلما كان كل جزء منه صالحا للانتقال عن وضعه كان الفلك قابلا للحركة المستديرة إذ يمتنع عليه الحركة المستقيمة، ثم نقول: إنه كما كان قابلا للحركة المستديرة، وجب أن يكون فيه مبدأ ميل مستدير، يعني أنه يجب أن يتحقق في ذاته قوة محركة بالحركة المستديرة، ولو لم يكن فيه مبدأ ميل مستدير لما كان قابلا للحركة المستديرة والتالي باطل؛ لما مر من أنه قابل لها، فالمقدم مثله بيان الملازمة: أنه لو لم يكن في ذاته مبدأ ميل مستدير لما كان قابلا للميل من أمر خارج فيمتنع أن يتحرك؛ لأن الحركة لا تكون بدون الميل، ومعنى الميل عندهم أنه كيفية مدافعة لما يمانع الجسم عن الحركة، وهي كيفية محسوسة في الحركة المكانية والوضعية، ومن وضع اليد على الجسم حين التحرك وجدها. وإنما قلنا: إنه لو لم يكن في ذاته مبدأ ميل مستدير لم يكن قابلا للميل من خارج؛ لأنه لو قبل الميل من خارج أي قاسر ،وتحرك لتحرك مسافة في زمان ما؛ ضرورة أن الحركة لا توجد بدون مسافة وزمان وفرضنا أن القاسر حرك جسما آخر له ميل طبعي بمثل تلك القوة التي حرك بها الجسم الأول الذي ليس فيه مبدأ ميل أصلا في عين تلك المسافة، فزمان حركة هذا الجسم ذي الميل الطبعي زائد من زمان حركة الجسم الأول المجرد عن الميل الطبعي؛ لأن طبع الجسم الثاني معاوق ومزاحم لتأثير القاسر، فتكون حركته بطيئة، فيكون زمانه أزيد من الأول، وإن لم يكن زمانه أزيد لكان الحركة التي مع المانع والتي بلا مانع متساويين في الزمان، وهذا باطل، وإلا لم يكن المانع مانعا فيلزم الخلف، فلا بد أن يكون زمان حركة الجسم الأول أقصر من زمان حركة الجسم الثاني، ويكون لذلك الزمان الأقصر نسبة إلى الزمان الأطول كأن يكون نصفا، وفرضنا جسما ثالثا ذا ميل طبعي ميلا قل من ميل :
Chapter that the celestial sphere is receptive to circular motion because each hypothetical part of it does not have a specific requirement for obtaining its realization in the study of shape. For if each of its parts were spherical in shape, it would be impossible for a continuous spherical surface to result from a sum of spheres; because the contact of a sphere with a sphere is only at a point, and a gap remains between them. And it is not possible to take the second [option]; because its parts would be seeking the natural shape, so it would be necessary that the sphere be receptive to rectilinear motion, and that is a contradiction. The sphere: it is the body whose all dimensions emanating from a hypothetical point in its center are equal. Spherical surface: because the contact of a sphere with a sphere is at a point, and a gap remains between them, so how can a continuous spherical surface result from a sum of spheres? Seeking: and seeking entails rectilinear motion, so each one would be receptive to rectilinear motion, so it would be necessary that the sphere be receptive to rectilinear motion, because the seeking of each one of them for the natural shape necessitates that its parts that were scattered gather together, and that is not free from rectilinear motion. Since each one of the parts of the sphere is receptive to rectilinear motion, then the sphere also would be receptive to rectilinear motion, and that is a contradiction; because it determines directions, and nothing that determines directions is receptive to rectilinear motion. So the sphere is not receptive to rectilinear motion. As for motion, which does not leave the whole body in its entirety and its position and place, and leaves each one of its parts in its position, that is positional motion, and you have known its meaning in the discussion of motion. Its summary is that its position and place, if it had a place, and if it has no place, then the change is only in position, like the sphere having a specific position and specific orientation due to the equality of parts in nature. So every part that has no other body behind it such that it would have a place, but rather has a position relative to what is below it. The result of equality: that a part of the parts of the sphere does not have a specific position and specific orientation by nature; because the sphere is simple as established before, and the simple does not have conflicting powers. When it is simple, all parts are equal in nature, so it is possible for each part to move from its position, due to the non-necessity of that position for it. When each part is suitable for moving from its position, the sphere is receptive to circular motion, since rectilinear motion is impossible for it. Then we say: just as it is receptive to circular motion, it is necessary that it have a principle of circular inclination, meaning that there must be realized in itself a motive power for circular motion. And if it did not have a principle of circular inclination, it would not be receptive to circular motion, and the consequent is false; because it has been established that it is receptive to it, so the antecedent is likewise. Explanation of the implication: if it did not have in itself a principle of circular inclination, it would not be receptive to inclination from an external thing, so it would be impossible for it to move; because motion does not occur without inclination. And the meaning of inclination according to them is a quality that repels what opposes the body in motion, and it is a perceptible quality in spatial and positional motion, and one who places a hand on a body while it is moving finds it. We said: if it did not have in itself a principle of circular inclination, it would not be receptive to inclination from outside; because if it received inclination from outside, i.e., a compeller, and moved, it would move a distance in some time; necessarily, motion does not exist without distance and time. And we suppose that the compeller moved another body that has a natural inclination with the same force with which it moved the first body that has no principle of inclination at all, over the same distance. Then the time of motion of this body with natural inclination is greater than the time of motion of the first body stripped of natural inclination; because the nature of the second body is resistant and interfering with the effect of the compeller, so its motion would be slow, so its time would be greater than the first. And if its time were not greater, then motion with an impediment and motion without an impediment would be equal in time, and that is false; otherwise the impediment would not be an impediment, so a contradiction would follow. So it is necessary that the time of motion of the first body be shorter than the time of motion of the second body, and that shorter time would have a ratio to the longer time, such as being half. And we suppose a third body having a natural inclination with an inclination less than the inclination [of the second].
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage provides a multi-step proof that the celestial sphere undergoes circular motion and must possess an innate circular inclination. The author first repeats the earlier argument that if the sphere's parts were spheres, they could not form a continuous surface, and if not, they would seek rectilinear motion, which is impossible for the sphere because it determines directions. He then clarifies the nature of positional motion (حركة وضعية), where the whole body remains in its place but parts change position, as in a sphere. Since the sphere is simple and its parts are equal in nature, no part has a fixed position, so each part can move from its position, making the sphere receptive to circular motion because rectilinear motion is impossible. The author then argues that this receptivity necessitates an innate principle of circular inclination (مبدأ ميل مستدير); otherwise the sphere could not receive motion from an external source, because motion requires inclination. He supports this with a thought experiment: if an external force moves two bodies over the same distance—one with no innate inclination and one with natural inclination—the body with natural inclination moves slower due to resistance. The same force would produce a faster motion in the body with no inclination, proving that without an innate inclination, the sphere could not move at all. The passage concludes mid-sentence with a hypothetical third body of weaker inclination, but the intended point is that the sphere must have its own circular inclination to account for its motion.

Checkpoint
Why does the author conclude that the celestial sphere cannot undergo rectilinear motion?
A) Because its parts are spherical and cannot form a continuous surface.
B) Because it determines directions, and nothing that determines directions can move rectilinearly.
C) Because it is composed of fire and air.
D) Because it is too large to move.

Key Takeaway
The celestial sphere's role as a direction determiner logically prohibits rectilinear motion, necessitating circular motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the categorical syllogism from the passage: formalize the premises and conclusion using the terms 'determines directions', 'receptive to rectilinear motion', and 'celestial sphere'. Identify the figure and mood.
?
Consider a counterargument: someone claims the sphere could move rectilinearly. Using the same premise form, prove why this would be contradictory. Write a short response in syllogistic form.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Proof of Innate Circular Inclination in the Celestial Sphere
Author proves innate circular inclination via conditional syllogism and time comparison thought experiment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the conditional syllogism (modus tollens) that proves the celestial sphere has an innate principle of circular inclination.

Core Idea
No external force can induce motion without an innate inclination to resist.

Having established that the celestial sphere moves circularly, the author now proves that this motion originates from an internal principle, not an external compeller. He uses a conditional argument: if the sphere lacked an innate principle of circular inclination, it could not receive motion from outside, because motion always involves a resisting quality (mayl). The thought experiment comparing motion times of bodies with and without natural inclination demonstrates that without innate inclination, motion would be impossible, so the sphere must have its own innate circular inclination.

Guided Path
1
State the claim to be proved
The claim is that the celestial sphere possesses a principle of circular inclination (مبدأ ميل مستدير) in itself.
This is an intrinsic motive power for circular motion.
2
Understand the conditional premise
If the sphere had no innate circular inclination, then it could not receive inclination from an external thing (since it would have no quality to interact with the external force), and so it could not move at all.
This is the antecedent-consequent relation.
3
Analyze the thought experiment
Suppose an external force moves two bodies over the same distance: one with no innate inclination (body A) and one with natural inclination (body B). The force is the same. Body B moves slower because its nature resists. So body A's motion time is shorter.
This shows that a body with no inclination moves faster under the same force.
4
Apply modus tollens
We know that the celestial sphere does move (as established earlier). Therefore, it cannot be that it lacks an innate inclination. So it must have a principle of circular inclination.
The argument is a valid modus tollens conditional syllogism.

This passage provides a multi-step proof that the celestial sphere undergoes circular motion and must possess an innate circular inclination. He supports this with a thought experiment: if an external force moves two bodies over the same distance—one with no innate inclination and one with natural inclination—the body with natural inclination moves slower due to resistance. The same force would produce a faster motion in the body with no inclination, proving that without an innate inclination, the sphere could not move at all. The passage concludes mid-sentence with a hypothetical third body of weaker inclination, but the intended point is that the sphere must have its own circular inclination to account for its motion.

ميل
كيفية مدافعة
معاوق
مزاحم

The celestial sphere must have an innate principle of circular inclination because without it, no external force could cause its motion, as the thought experiment about motion times demonstrates.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Proof that the celestial sphere has a principle of circular inclination
Conditional syllogism for celestial motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional syllogism (modus tollens) used to prove that the celestial sphere has an innate circular inclination and explain the underlying ratio argument.

Core Idea
The celestial sphere must have an internal inclination; without it, external motion leads to contradiction.

In this passage, the author deploys a conditional syllogism to prove that the celestial sphere possesses an innate principle of circular motion. The argument proceeds by assuming the opposite and showing it leads to an impossibility (reductio ad absurdum). As you work through the logical steps, you will see how conditional reasoning grounds a key physical claim in Islamic natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the 'if...then' statement: 'If the sphere did not have a principle of circular inclination in its nature, then it would not receive inclination from outside, so it would not be able to move circularly.'
This is the major premise of the conditional syllogism.
2
Identify the exception (istithna')
The argument asserts that the consequent is false: the sphere does move circularly, so it is receptive to circular motion. Thus the consequent 'it is not receptive' is denied.
This denial of the consequent is the minor premise.
3
Conclude by modus tollens
From 'if P then Q' and 'not Q', we conclude 'not P'. Here, not P means the sphere does have a principle of circular inclination.
The conclusion is that the principle exists.
4
Understand the sub-argument for the conditional
The author includes a detailed physical argument comparing motion times of bodies with and without inclination to justify the conditional connection: without innate inclination, external motion would imply an impossibility.
This sub-argument uses ratios of times and obstacles to establish the conditional.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الفلك قابل للحركة المستديرة. وكل جزء منه يمكن أن يزول عن وضعه، ومتى كان كذلك كان قابلا للحركة المستديرة، ونقول أيضا يجب أن يكون فيه مبدأ ميل مستدير يتحرك به، وإلا لما كان قابلا للحركة المستديرة، لكن التالي كاذب، فالمقدم مثله. بيان الشرطية: أنه لو لم يكن في طبعه مبدأ ميل مستدير لما قبل الميل من خارج، فلا يكون فيه ميل أصلا، فيمتنع أن يتحرك على الاستدارة. وإنما قلنا: إنه لو لم يكن في طبعه مبدأ ميل مستدير لما قبل الميل من خارج؛ لأنه لو تحرك من خارج لتحرك مسافة في زمان، ويكون ذلك الزمان أقصر من زمان حركة ذي ميل يتحرك بمثل تلك القوة في عين تلك المسافة، وإلا لكان الشيء مع العائق الطبعي كـ[؟] لا معه، الجسم الثاني بحيث يكون نسبة ميله إلى ميل الجسم الثاني كنسبة الزمان الأقصر إلى الزمان الأطول، فإذا حرك القاسر ذلك الجسم الثالث بمثل تلك القوة في عين تلك المسافة يكون زمان حركته مثل زمان الجسم الأول؛ لأن الحركة تزداد سرعتها بقلة الموانع وبطؤها بكثرة الموانع والنسبة بين زماني تينك الحركتين كنسبة بين موانعها، فلما كانت نسبة ميل الجسم الثالث إلى ميل الجسم الثاني كنسبة الزمان الأقصر إلى الزمان الأطول كان نسبة زمان حركة الجسم الثالث إلى زمان حركة الجسم الثاني كنسبة الزمان الأقصر إلى الزمان الأطول، وقد فرضنا أن الزمان الأقصر نصف الزمان الأطول، فيكون زمان حركة الجسم الثالث أيضا نصف زمان حركة الجسم الثاني فيلزم تساوي زمان حركة الجسم الأول عديم الميل زمان حركة الجسم الثالث القليل الميل، هذا خلف؛ لأن زمان الحركة بلا مانع وزمان الحركة مع مانع وإن كان قليلا، لا يتساويان أبدا. وإلا : أي وإن لم يكن زمان حركة الجسم الأول العديم الميل أقصر من زمان حركة الجسم الثاني ذي الميل.
Chapter on that the celestial sphere is receptive to circular motion. And every part of it can change its position, and when it is thus, it is receptive to circular motion. We also say: it must have in it a principle of circular inclination by which it moves, otherwise it would not be receptive to circular motion, but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise. Explanation of the conditional: if it did not have in its nature a principle of circular inclination, it would not receive inclination from outside, so there would be no inclination in it at all, then it would be impossible for it to move circularly. And we said: if it did not have in its nature a principle of circular inclination, it would not receive inclination from outside; because if it moved from outside, it would move a distance in time, and that time would be shorter than the time of motion of a body with inclination moving with the same force over the same distance, otherwise the thing with a natural obstacle would be like [?] without it, the second body such that the ratio of its inclination to the inclination of the second body is as the ratio of the shorter time to the longer time. So when the external mover moves that third body with the same force over the same distance, the time of its motion would be like the time of the first body; because motion increases in speed with fewer obstacles and slows with more obstacles, and the ratio between the times of those two motions is as the ratio between their obstacles. Since the ratio of the inclination of the third body to the inclination of the second body is as the ratio of the shorter time to the longer time, the ratio of the time of motion of the third body to the time of motion of the second body is as the ratio of the shorter time to the longer time. And we assumed that the shorter time is half the longer time, so the time of motion of the third body is also half the time of motion of the second body, resulting in equality between the time of motion of the first body without inclination and the time of motion of the third body with little inclination. This is a contradiction; because the time of motion without an obstacle and the time of motion with an obstacle, even a small one, are never equal. Otherwise: i.e., if the time of motion of the first body without inclination were not shorter than the time of motion of the second body with inclination.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the celestial sphere (al-falak) must have an innate principle of circular inclination (mabda' mayl mustadeer). The author begins by stating that every part of the sphere can change its position, which makes it receptive to circular motion. He then asserts that if it is receptive, it must have such a principle, otherwise it would not be receptive. This is a conditional argument: if no principle, then not receptive; but it is receptive, so the principle exists. The author explains the conditional step: without an innate circular inclination, the sphere could not receive inclination from an external mover. Why? Because if an external mover (qasir) moved it, it would traverse a distance in a certain time. The author then compares two bodies: one with natural inclination and one without. The body with natural inclination resists the external mover, so its motion is slower and takes more time. The body without inclination moves faster, taking less time. The author assumes a ratio (e.g., half) between the shorter and longer times. Then he introduces a third body with a smaller inclination, such that the ratio of its inclination to the second body's inclination equals the ratio of the shorter time to the longer time. When the same external force moves this third body, its motion time would equal that of the first body (without inclination) because the ratio of obstacles matches. This leads to a contradiction: the time with a small obstacle equals the time without any obstacle, which is impossible. Therefore, the original assumption (that the sphere has no innate circular inclination) is false. The argument uses a reduction ad absurdum common in Aristotelian physics.

Key Terms
مبدأ ميل مستدير (mabda' mayl mustadeer): the principle of circular inclination
القاسر (al-qasir): the external mover
الميل (al-mayl): inclination

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) modus ponens
B) modus tollens
C) disjunctive syllogism
D) categorical syllogism

Key Takeaway
The author uses modus tollens: if no principle then no motion; but motion exists, so principle exists.

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
The Celestial Sphere's Natural Circular Inclination
Author argues that the celestial sphere has a natural circular inclination, using a conditional proof and reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional proof that the celestial sphere has a natural circular inclination and identify the reductio ad absurdum used in the argument.

Core Idea
The celestial sphere's circular motion proves it has a natural circular inclination.

In this passage, the author establishes that the celestial sphere must possess a natural circular inclination. He uses a conditional syllogism: if it did not have such an inclination, it would not be capable of circular motion. Since circular motion is observed, the antecedent must be false, so the inclination exists. He also provides a detailed reductio argument to show that a body without natural inclination cannot receive circular motion from an external force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim and conditional structure
Find the 'if...then' statement and conclusion in the Arabic. The author states: 'if it did not have a natural circular inclination, it would not be receptive to circular motion' and then denies the consequent.
Look for 'فإنه لو لم يكن ... لما قبل' and 'لكن التالي كاذب فالمقدم مثله'.
2
Extract the reductio argument
Follow the reasoning that supposes a body without natural inclination moves by external force, leading to an absurd equality of speeds. He shows that if a body without inclination moves, its motion time would be less than that of one with inclination; then by assuming a proportionally weaker inclination, the body with weak inclination would equal the no-inclination body in speed, which is impossible.
The author uses ratios and the concept of 'natural obstacle' (العائق الطبعي).
3
Clarify the distinction between natural and forced inclination
The author emphasizes that 'no inclination' means no natural inclination, not the absence of forced inclination. The moving body still has forced inclination from the external mover.
See: 'أصلا ليس المراد به السلب الكلي للميل سواء كان طبعيا أو قسريا'.
4
Reconstruct the conditional syllogism formally
The argument is a modus tollens: If P then Q, not Q, therefore not P. P: There is no natural circular inclination. Q: It is not receptive to circular motion. Not Q: It is receptive to circular motion. Therefore, not P: It has natural circular inclination.
This matches the conditional syllogism structure in classical logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الفلك قابل
yمكن أن يزول عن وضعه، ومتى كان كذلك كان قابلا للحركة المستديرة، ونقول أيضا يجب أن يكون فيه مبدأ ميل مستدير يتحرك به، وإلا لما كان قابلا للحركة المستديرة، لكن التالي كاذب، فالمقدم مثله. بيان الشرطية: أنه لو لم يكن في طبعه مبدأ ميل مستدير لما قبل الميل من خارج، فلا يكون فيه ميل أصلا، فيمتنع أن يتحرك على الاستدارة. وإنما قلنا: إنه لو لم يكن في طبعه مبدأ ميل مستدير لما قبل الميل من خارج؛ لأنه لو تحرك من خارج لتحرك مسافة في زمان، ويكون ذلك الزمان أقصر من زمان حركة ذي ميل، يتحرك بمثل تلك القوة في عين تلك المسافة، وإلا لكان الشيء مع العائق الطبعي كـهو لا معه. 
فإذا فرضنا ذا ميل آخر ميله أضعف من ذي الميل الأول بحيث يكون نسبته إلى الميل الأول مثل نسبة الزمان الأقصر إلى الزمان الأطول، فيتحرك بمثل تلك القوة في مثل زمان عديم الميل مثل مسافة؛ لأن الحركة تزداد سرعتها بقدر انتقاص القوة الميلية التي في الجسم؛ لأنه لو انتقص شيء من القوة التي في الجسم، ولا يزداد السرعة لم تكن القوة الميلية مانعة من الحركة، هذا خلف، فظهر أن الجسم القليل الميل والذي لا ميل فيه متساويان في السرعة، وهو محال، وهذا المحال إنما لزم من فرض تحرك ذلك الجسم الذي لا ميل فيه أصلا أو من فرض الميل الذي نسبته إلى الميل الأول كنسبة زمان عديم الميل إلى زمان ذي الميل. وهو محال ضرورة أن الحركة مع العائق لا تكون مثل الحركة من بلا عائق في السرعة. أصلا ليس المراد به السلب الكلي للميل سواء كان طبعيا أو قسريا، كيف وقد فرض تحرك ذلك الجسم بقاسر، فلا بد أن يكون فيه ميل قسري، بل المراد به سلب الميل الطبعي؛ لأن المفروض أن ذلك الجسم ليس فيه ميل طبعي وتحرك من خارج.
Chapter on that the celestial sphere is receptive. It is possible for it to leave its position, and whenever it is like that, it is receptive to circular motion. We also say: It must have in it a principle of circular inclination by which it moves, otherwise it would not be receptive to circular motion. But the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. Explanation of the conditional: If there were not in its nature a principle of circular inclination, it would not receive inclination from outside, so there would be no inclination in it at all, thus it would be impossible for it to move circularly. And we said: If there were not in its nature a principle of circular inclination, it would not receive inclination from outside, because if it moved from outside it would traverse a distance in time, and that time would be shorter than the time of a body with inclination moving with the same force in the same distance, otherwise the thing with a natural obstacle would be like one without it. Then if we suppose another inclination weaker than the first such that its ratio to the first inclination is like the ratio of the shorter time to the longer time, then it would move with the same force in the same time as the body without inclination over the same distance; because speed increases with the decrease of the inclinational force in the body; because if something of the force in the body decreased and speed did not increase, then the inclinational force would not be an obstacle to motion — this is a contradiction. Thus it appears that the body with little inclination and the body with no inclination are equal in speed, which is absurd. This absurdity follows only from assuming that body with absolutely no inclination moves, or from the assumption of an inclination whose ratio to the first inclination is like the ratio of the time of the body without inclination to the time of the body with inclination. And it is absurd necessarily because motion with an obstacle is not like motion without an obstacle in speed. By 'absolutely no inclination' is not meant the complete negation of inclination whether natural or forced, since we assumed that body moves by a force, so it must have a forced inclination. Rather, what is meant is the negation of natural inclination, because it is assumed that that body has no natural inclination and moves from outside.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the celestial sphere must possess a natural circular inclination (ميل مستدير). The author begins by stating the claim: the celestial sphere is receptive to circular motion, therefore it must have a principle of circular inclination. This is argued via a conditional syllogism: If it did not have such an inclination, it would not be receptive to circular motion (the antecedent implies the consequent). But the consequent is false (since we observe circular motion), so the antecedent is false — thus it does have the inclination.

The author then explains the conditional more rigorously. He argues that if there were no natural circular inclination, the sphere could not receive any inclination from outside, so there would be no inclination at all, making circular motion impossible. To prove that it could not receive inclination from outside, he compares the motion of a body with natural inclination and one without. If a body without natural inclination were moved by an external force, it would traverse a certain distance in a time shorter than that of a body with natural inclination moving with the same force over the same distance — otherwise, having a natural obstacle would make no difference. This leads to a reductio ad absurdum.

Next, he considers a weaker inclination. Suppose there is an inclination whose ratio to the original inclination equals the ratio of the shorter time (of the body without inclination) to the longer time (of the body with the original inclination). Then that body with weaker inclination, moved by the same force, would move in the same time as the body with no inclination over the same distance. This implies that a body with little inclination and one with none are equal in speed, which is impossible because an obstacle (natural inclination) should slow motion. Therefore, the original assumption — that a body moves without any natural inclination — is false.

The author clarifies that 'no inclination' here means no natural inclination, not no forced inclination, since the body is being moved by an external force (قاسر) and thus has forced inclination. The conclusion is that the celestial sphere must have a natural circular inclination for its circular motion to be possible.

Key Terms
الميل
الفلك
الحركة المستديرة
العائق الطبعي

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the logical form of the main argument that the celestial sphere has a natural circular inclination?
A) Modus ponens: If P then Q; P is true; therefore Q is true.
B) Modus tollens: If P then Q; Q is false; therefore P is false.
C) Hypothetical syllogism: If P then Q; If Q then R; therefore if P then R.
D) Disjunctive syllogism: Either P or Q; not P; therefore Q.

Key Takeaway
A conditional proof (modus tollens) combined with reductio ad absurdum demonstrates that the celestial sphere must have a natural circular inclination.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Reductio ad Absurdum: Proving Every Moving Body Has a Principle of Inclination
First part of passage: reductio proof for inclination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a reductio ad absurdum argument and identify the contradictory premise using conditional reasoning.

Core Idea
A body without inclination cannot move without absurdity.

This lesson unpacks the first part of the passage, where the author uses a reductio (khalaf) to prove that every moving body must have a principle of inclination (mayl). The argument is a classic conditional proof: assume the opposite, derive an impossibility, and conclude the original. We will walk through the logical steps and examine how the impossibility is generated through a proportion between inclination, time, and speed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim to be proved
The author wants to prove that every moving body has a principle of inclination (mayl). Locate the statement of the claim in the Arabic.
The claim is implicit in the conclusion: 'so it is established that every moving body must have a principle of inclination.'
2
Extract the reductio assumption
The reductio assumes the opposite: that a body can move with no inclination at all. Find where this assumption is made.
The assumption is: 'assuming the motion of that body which has no inclination at all.'
3
Follow the derivation of the contradiction
The author postulates another body with a weaker inclination such that the ratio of inclinations equals the ratio of times. This leads to the conclusion that the no-inclination body and the weak-inclination body would have equal speed, which is impossible because motion with an impediment (inclination) cannot be as fast as without.
The contradiction is: motion with impediment equals motion without impediment in speed.
4
Formalize as a conditional argument
The argument has the form: If a body lacks inclination, then it would move in some time T. If a body with a weaker inclination can be chosen proportionally, then it would also move in time T, implying equal speed. But equal speed is impossible. So the assumption that a body lacks inclination leads to an impossibility. Therefore, the assumption is false.
This is a conditional proof: If P (no inclination) then Q (contradiction). Not Q, so not P.
5
State the conclusion
Since the assumption of a body without inclination leads to impossibility, all moving bodies must have an inclination. The author then clarifies that 'no inclination' means no natural inclination, as a body may still have coercive inclination from an external mover.
The conclusion is: 'Thus it is established that every moving body must have a principle of inclination.'

الفن الثاني في الفلكيات
فصل في أن الفلك قابل للحركة المستديرة.
...
هذا خلف، وذلك الزمان الأقصر له نسبة لا محالة إلى الزمان الأطول، فإذا فرضنا ذا ميل آخر ميله أضعف من ذي الميل الأول بحيث يكون نسبته إلى الميل الأول مثل نسبة الزمان الأقصر إلى الزمان الأطول، فيتحرك بمثل تلك القوة في مثل زمان عديم الميل مثل مسافة؛ لأن الحركة تزداد سرعتها بقدر انتقاص القوة الميلية التي في الجسم؛ لأنه لو انتقص شيء من القوة التي في الجسم، ولا يزداد السرعة لم تكن القوة الميلية مانعة من الحركة، هذا خلف، فظهر أن الجسم القليل الميل والذي لا ميل فيه متساويان في السرعة، وهو محال، وهذا المحال إنما لزم من فرض تحرك ذلك الجسم الذي لا ميل فيه أصلا أو من فرض الميل الذي نسبته إلى الميل الأول كنسبة زمان عديم الميل إلى زمان ذي الميل... نسبة مثلا أن يكون الزمان الأقصر نصف الزمان الأطول، فلو كان الزمان الأقصر نصف ساعة كان الزمان الأطول ساعة مثل :زمان مثلا لو كان زمان حركة الجسم الأول الذي هو عديم الميل نصف ساعة كان زمان حركة ذي الميل الثاني أيضا نصف ساعة. القوة الميلية إلخ أي العائق الطبيعي الذي هو ذي الجسم في الميل الحركة. وهو محال ضرورة أن الحركة مع العائق لا تكون مثل الحركة من عائقا بلا عائق في السرعة.
أصلا ليس المراد به السلب الكلي للميل سواء كان طبيعيا أو قسريا، كيف وقد فرض تحرك ذلك الجسم بقاسر ، فلا بد أن يكون فيه ميل قسري، بل المراد به سلب الميل الطبيعي؛ لأن المفروض أن ذلك الجسم ليس فيه ميل طبيعي وتحرك من خارج. الميل: أي الميل الثاني الذي تحقق في الجسم الثالث.

الفن الثاني في الفلكيات
فصل في أن الفلك قابل
الأول، لكن فرض الميل على النسبة المذكورة ممكن، فهذا المحال إنما لزم من فرض تحرك الجسم الذي لا ميل فيه أصلا، فيكون محالا، ونقول أيضا إن الفلك لا يكون في طبعه مبدأ ميل مستقيم، وإلا لكانت الطبيعة الواحدة تقتضي الأثرين المتنافيين، هذا خلف.
فرض الميل: فإن قيل: الميل من مقولة الكيف، والزمان من مقولة الكم، فكيف يقاس بينهما؛ لأن القياس فرع التجانس، قلت: الميل يتكمم باعتبار العدد العارض له، أو باعتبار الجسم الذي هو فيه، فبهذا الاعتبار يتجانسان فيقاسان بأنه أي نسبة لأحدهما إلى الآخر، فإن قيل: يجوز أن يلزم المحال من المجموع، فكيف يلزم من إمكان الميل على النسبة المذكورة أن المحال إنما لزم من فرض تحرك الجسم الذي لا ميل فيه؟ قلت لما كان المجموع مركبا من الشقين المذكورين وتبين إمكان أحدهما أعني الميل على النسبة المذكورة، تعين أن المحال إنما لزم من الشق الآخر، وهو تحرك الجسم الذي لا ميل فيه فيكون ذلك محالا، فثبت أن كل جسم متحرك لا بد فيه مبدأ ميل ،والفلك أيضا قابل للحركة المستديرة فلا بد فيه من مبدأ ميل مستدير، وهو المطلوب.
طبعه إذ ثبت أن الفلك في طبعه مبدأ ميل مستدير لا يكون فيه مبدأ ميل مستقيم، وإلا فلا يخلو إما أن يقتضي الطبيعة الواحدة ذينك الميلين، فيلزم اجتماع الأمرين المتنافيين؛ لأن الميل المستدير يقتضي بقاء الجسم في حيزه وتبدل وضع الأجزاء، والميل المستقيم انتقال الجسم عن حيزه، وإما أن يكون لكل واحد منهما طبيعة على حدة، وهو باطل؛ لأن الطبيعة هي الصورة النوعية، وتقوم حقيقة واحدة بصورتين باطل؛ لأن الصورة النوعية الواحدة لما انضمت بالمادة كملت الحقيقة وتحصلت وليس لها افتقار إلى صورة أخرى أصلا، ولو انضمت الصورة بالأخرى إلى المادة لحصلت حقيقة أخرى، فلو تقومت حقيقة واحدة بصورتين لزم كون حقيقة واحدة ذات حقيقتين.
The second art on celestial bodies. Chapter on that the celestial sphere is receptive to circular motion. ... This is a contradiction. That shortest time has a relationship necessarily to the longer time. If we suppose another body with an inclination weaker than the first inclination such that its ratio to the first inclination is like the ratio of the shortest time to the longest time, then it will move with that [weaker] force in a time equal to that of the body without inclination over the same distance; because speed increases as the inclinational force in the body decreases; because if something of the force in the body decreased and speed did not increase, then the inclinational force would not be an impediment to motion. This is a contradiction. So it appears that the body with little inclination and the body with no inclination are equal in speed, which is impossible. This impossibility resulted only from assuming the motion of that body which has no inclination at all, or from assuming an inclination whose ratio to the first inclination is like the ratio of the time of the body without inclination to the time of the body with inclination... For example, the shorter time is half the longer time. If the shorter time is half an hour, the longer time is an hour. For example, if the time of motion of the first body, which is without inclination, is half an hour, then the time of motion of the second body with inclination is also half an hour. The inclinational force etc., i.e., the natural impediment which belongs to the body in inclinational motion. It is impossible necessarily that motion with an impediment is equal in speed to motion without an impediment. Absolutely, by this is not meant the complete negation of inclination, whether natural or coercive; how could it, when it was assumed that the body moves by a coercer, so it must have a coercive inclination. Rather, by it is meant the negation of natural inclination; because it is assumed that that body has no natural inclination and moves from outside. Inclination: i.e., the second inclination which is realized in the third body. The second art on celestial bodies. [Chapter on that the celestial sphere is receptive]. The first [premise], but the assumption of inclination in the mentioned ratio is possible. So this impossibility resulted only from assuming the motion of the body which has no inclination at all, so it is impossible. We also say that the celestial sphere does not have in its nature a principle of straight inclination; otherwise one nature would require two contradictory effects; this is a contradiction. Assumption of inclination: If it is said: Inclination is from the category of quality, and time is from the category of quantity, so how can they be compared? Because comparison is a branch of homogeneity. I say: Inclination is quantified by considering the number that attaches to it, or by considering the body in which it is. By this consideration they become homogeneous and are compared, meaning a ratio of one to the other. If it is said: It is possible that the impossibility resulted from the combination; so how does it follow from the possibility of inclination in the mentioned ratio that the impossibility resulted only from assuming the motion of the body without inclination? I say: Since the combination is composed of the two mentioned parts, and the possibility of one of them, namely the inclination in the mentioned ratio, became clear, it is determined that the impossibility resulted only from the other part, namely the motion of the body without inclination, so that is impossible. Thus it is established that every moving body must have a principle of inclination, and the celestial sphere is also receptive to circular motion, so it must have a principle of circular inclination, which is what is sought. As for its nature, since it is established that the celestial sphere in its nature has a principle of circular inclination, it does not have a principle of straight inclination; otherwise it would be unavoidable: either the single nature would require both inclinations, leading to the combination of two contradictory things—because circular inclination requires the body to remain in its place and the positions of its parts to change, while straight inclination requires the body to be transferred from its place—or each of them would have a separate nature, which is false; because nature is the specific form, and a single reality being constituted by two forms is false; because when a single specific form is joined to matter, the reality is completed and actualized, and it has no need for another form at all. If another form were joined with the first to matter, another reality would result. So if a single reality were constituted by two forms, it would necessitate one reality having two realities.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical proof that every moving body must have a principle of inclination (mayl), and that the celestial sphere has a circular inclination, not a straight one. The author uses a reduction ad absurdum (khalaf) and responds to objections. The argument proceeds as follows: Suppose a body with no natural inclination moves; it will take some time to traverse a distance. Consider another body with a weaker inclination such that the ratio of its inclination to the first body's (nonexistent) inclination equals the ratio of the shorter time to a longer time. Then the weaker-inclined body would move in the same time as the no-inclination body over the same distance. But this is impossible because motion with an impediment (inclination as a natural resistance) cannot equal motion without impediment in speed. The only way out is that the assumption of a body with no inclination moving is impossible, so all moving bodies must have an inclination. The author then clarifies that 'no inclination' means no natural inclination, as the body may have coercive inclination if moved externally. Next, the author argues that the celestial sphere cannot have a straight inclination, because its nature (circular) would then require two contradictory effects: staying in place (circular) versus transferring (straight). An objection is raised: how can inclination (quality) be compared to time (quantity)? The response: inclination is quantified by the body it is in or by the number attaching to it, making them commensurable. Another objection: the impossibility might arise from the combination of assumptions. The response: since one part (the ratio assumption) is possible, the impossibility must stem from the other part (motion of the no-inclination body). Finally, the conclusion: every moving body has a principle of inclination; the celestial sphere moves circularly, so it has a circular inclination, not straight. This is a typical falsafah argument about celestial motion, drawing on Aristotelian physics and the concept of natural place.

طبيعة
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the reductio derive?
A) That a body with no inclination would move faster than any other body.
B) That a body with weak inclination would move at the same speed as a body with no inclination.
C) That a body with weak inclination would not move at all.
D) That a body with no inclination would require infinite force.

Key Takeaway
A reductio ad absurdum uses conditional reasoning: assume the opposite, derive an impossibility, and conclude the original claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a reductio argument to show that if a body had no acquired inclination (mayl qasri), it would stop immediately upon losing contact with the mover. Use the principle that motion with impediment is slower.
?
Identify the conditional form in the passage: What is the antecedent and consequent? Check if the modus tollens is correctly applied.
