The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Applying the Principle: The Celestial Sphere Has Circular Inclination
Second part of passage: applying to celestial sphere and responding to objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of inclination to the celestial sphere and argue against straight inclination using the impossibility of contradictory effects from a single nature.

Core Idea
A single nature cannot produce two contradictory effects.

Having established that every moving body has a principle of inclination, the author now applies this to the celestial sphere. He argues that the celestial sphere cannot have a straight inclination because that would require a single nature to produce both circular and straight motions, which are contradictory. The passage also addresses an objection about comparing inclination (quality) to time (quantity) and clarifies the logical structure of the reductio.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
The author wants to prove that the celestial sphere has a principle of circular inclination and no straight inclination. Find where this is stated.
The claim is in the conclusion: 'the celestial sphere does not have in its nature a principle of straight inclination' and 'it must have a principle of circular inclination.'
2
Examine the argument against straight inclination
The author argues that if the celestial sphere had a straight inclination, then either its single nature would produce two contradictory effects (circular and straight) which is impossible, or it would have two separate natures, which is also impossible because a single essence cannot have two specific forms.
The two contradictory effects are: circular motion (staying in place but changing orientation) vs. straight motion (changing place).
3
Analyze the objection about comparing inclination and time
An objector says: inclination is a quality, time is a quantity; how can they be compared? The author responds that inclination is quantified by the number or the body it is in, making them homogeneous for comparison.
This objection targets the proportional reasoning in the first part of the passage.
4
Understand the response about the source of impossibility
Another objection: maybe the impossibility came from the combination of assumptions (no inclination + proportional inclination) not just one. The author replies that since the proportional assumption is possible, the impossibility must stem from the no-inclination assumption alone.
This shows careful logical analysis: isolating the problematic assumption.
5
Synthesize the overall argument
The passage concludes: every moving body needs inclination; the celestial sphere is moving (circular); therefore it has circular inclination and not straight. This is a categorical syllogism applied to the celestial sphere.
The minor premise: the celestial sphere is a moving body. The major premise: every moving body has an inclination.

الفن الثاني في الفلكيات
فصل في أن الفلك قابل للحركة المستديرة.
...
هذا خلف، وذلك الزمان الأقصر له نسبة لا محالة إلى الزمان الأطول، فإذا فرضنا ذا ميل آخر ميله أضعف من ذي الميل الأول بحيث يكون نسبته إلى الميل الأول مثل نسبة الزمان الأقصر إلى الزمان الأطول، فيتحرك بمثل تلك القوة في مثل زمان عديم الميل مثل مسافة؛ لأن الحركة تزداد سرعتها بقدر انتقاص القوة الميلية التي في الجسم؛ لأنه لو انتقص شيء من القوة التي في الجسم، ولا يزداد السرعة لم تكن القوة الميلية مانعة من الحركة، هذا خلف، فظهر أن الجسم القليل الميل والذي لا ميل فيه متساويان في السرعة، وهو محال، وهذا المحال إنما لزم من فرض تحرك ذلك الجسم الذي لا ميل فيه أصلا أو من فرض الميل الذي نسبته إلى الميل الأول كنسبة زمان عديم الميل إلى زمان ذي الميل... نسبة مثلا أن يكون الزمان الأقصر نصف الزمان الأطول، فلو كان الزمان الأقصر نصف ساعة كان الزمان الأطول ساعة مثل :زمان مثلا لو كان زمان حركة الجسم الأول الذي هو عديم الميل نصف ساعة كان زمان حركة ذي الميل الثاني أيضا نصف ساعة. القوة الميلية إلخ أي العائق الطبيعي الذي هو ذي الجسم في الميل الحركة. وهو محال ضرورة أن الحركة مع العائق لا تكون مثل الحركة من عائقا بلا عائق في السرعة.
أصلا ليس المراد به السلب الكلي للميل سواء كان طبيعيا أو قسريا، كيف وقد فرض تحرك ذلك الجسم بقاسر ، فلا بد أن يكون فيه ميل قسري، بل المراد به سلب الميل الطبيعي؛ لأن المفروض أن ذلك الجسم ليس فيه ميل طبيعي وتحرك من خارج. الميل: أي الميل الثاني الذي تحقق في الجسم الثالث.

الفن الثاني في الفلكيات
فصل في أن الفلك قابل
الأول، لكن فرض الميل على النسبة المذكورة ممكن، فهذا المحال إنما لزم من فرض تحرك الجسم الذي لا ميل فيه أصلا، فيكون محالا، ونقول أيضا إن الفلك لا يكون في طبعه مبدأ ميل مستقيم، وإلا لكانت الطبيعة الواحدة تقتضي الأثرين المتنافيين، هذا خلف.

The second art on celestial bodies. Chapter on that the celestial sphere is receptive to circular motion. ... This is a contradiction. That shortest time has a relationship necessarily to the longer time. If we suppose another body with an inclination weaker than the first inclination such that its ratio to the first inclination is like the ratio of the shortest time to the longest time, then it will move with that [weaker] force in a time equal to that of the body without inclination over the same distance; because speed increases as the inclinational force in the body decreases; because if something of the force in the body decreased and speed did not increase, then the inclinational force would not be an impediment to motion. This is a contradiction. So it appears that the body with little inclination and the body with no inclination are equal in speed, which is impossible. This impossibility resulted only from assuming the motion of that body which has no inclination at all, or from assuming an inclination whose ratio to the first inclination is like the ratio of the time of the body without inclination to the time of the body with inclination... For example, the shorter time is half the longer time. If the shorter time is half an hour, the longer time is an hour. For example, if the time of motion of the first body, which is without inclination, is half an hour, then the time of motion of the second body with inclination is also half an hour. The inclinational force etc., i.e., the natural impediment which belongs to the body in inclinational motion. It is impossible necessarily that motion with an impediment is equal in speed to motion without an impediment. Absolutely, by this is not meant the complete negation of inclination, whether natural or coercive; how could it, when it was assumed that the body moves by a coercer, so it must have a coercive inclination. Rather, by it is meant the negation of natural inclination; because it is assumed that that body has no natural inclination and moves from outside. Inclination: i.e., the second inclination which is realized in the third body. The second art on celestial bodies. [Chapter on that the celestial sphere is receptive]. The first [premise], but the assumption of inclination in the mentioned ratio is possible. So this impossibility resulted only from assuming the motion of the body which has no inclination at all, so it is impossible. We also say that the celestial sphere does not have in its nature a principle of straight inclination; otherwise one nature would require two contradictory effects; this is a contradiction. Assumption of inclination: If it is said: Inclination is from the category of quality, and time is from the category of quantity, so how can they be compared? Because comparison is a branch of homogeneity. I say: Inclination is quantified by considering the number that attaches to it, or by considering the body in which it is. By this consideration they become homogeneous and are compared, meaning a ratio of one to the other. If it is said: It is possible that the impossibility resulted from the combination; so how does it follow from the possibility of inclination in the mentioned ratio that the impossibility resulted only from assuming the motion of the body without inclination? I say: Since the combination is composed of the two mentioned parts, and the possibility of one of them, namely the inclination in the mentioned ratio, became clear, it is determined that the impossibility resulted only from the other part, namely the motion of the body without inclination, so that is impossible. Thus it is established that every moving body must have a principle of inclination, and the celestial sphere is also receptive to circular motion, so it must have a principle of circular inclination, which is what is sought. As for its nature, since it is established that the celestial sphere in its nature has a principle of circular inclination, it does not have a principle of straight inclination; otherwise it would be unavoidable: either the single nature would require both inclinations, leading to the combination of two contradictory things—because circular inclination requires the body to remain in its place and the positions of its parts to change, while straight inclination requires the body to be transferred from its place—or each of them would have a separate nature, which is false; because nature is the specific form, and a single reality being constituted by two forms is false; because when a single specific form is joined to matter, the reality is completed and actualized, and it has no need for another form at all. Commentary
This passage is a dialectical proof that every moving body must have a principle of inclination (mayl), and that the celestial sphere has a circular inclination, not a straight one. The author uses a reduction ad absurdum (khalaf) and responds to objections. The argument proceeds as follows: Suppose a body with no natural inclination moves; it will take some time to traverse a distance. Consider another body with a weaker inclination such that the ratio of its inclination to the first body's (nonexistent) inclination equals the ratio of the shorter time to a longer time. Then the weaker-inclined body would move in the same time as the no-inclination body over the same distance. But this is impossible because motion with an impediment (inclination as a natural resistance) cannot equal motion without impediment in speed. The only way out is that the assumption of a body with no inclination moving is impossible, so all moving bodies must have an inclination. The author then clarifies that 'no inclination' means no natural inclination, as the body may have coercive inclination if moved externally. Next, the author argues that the celestial sphere cannot have a straight inclination, because its nature (circular) would then require two contradictory effects: staying in place (circular) versus transferring (straight). An objection is raised: how can inclination (quality) be compared to time (quantity)? The response: inclination is quantified by the body it is in or by the number attaching to it, making them commensurable. Another objection: the impossibility might arise from the combination of assumptions. The response: since one part (the ratio assumption) is possible, the impossibility must stem from the other part (motion of the no-inclination body). Finally, the conclusion: every moving body has a principle of inclination; the celestial sphere moves circularly, so it has a circular inclination, not straight. This is a typical falsafah argument about celestial motion, drawing on Aristotelian physics and the concept of natural place.

Why does the author conclude that the celestial sphere cannot have a straight inclination?

The Syllogism
intermediate

Proving the Sphere's Circular Inclination via Categorical Syllogism
Author proves the sphere's circular inclination via a categorical syllogism and a reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism that proves the celestial sphere must have a principle of circular inclination.

Core Idea
Every moving body needs an inclination; the sphere moves; so it has circular inclination.

The author establishes that any moving body requires a principle of inclination (mayl) through a reductio argument. He then applies this to the celestial sphere (falak), which moves circularly, concluding that it must possess a circular inclination. This is a classic first-figure categorical syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
Find the statement that applies to all moving bodies: 'Every moving body must have a principle of inclination.' This is proven indirectly by showing that a body without inclination cannot move.
The text says 'فثبت أن كل جسم متحرك لا بد فيه مبدأ ميل'.
2
Identify the minor premise
Find the statement that the sphere is a moving body (specifically, it moves circularly). The text says 'والفلك أيضا قابل للحركة المستديرة'.
3
Extract the conclusion
The conclusion is that the sphere must have a principle of circular inclination: 'فلا بد فيه من مبدأ ميل مستدير'.
4
Verify the syllogistic structure
Check that the premises form a categorical syllogism in the first figure (Barbara): All M are P (major), All S are M (minor), therefore All S are P (conclusion). The middle term is 'moving body'.
This syllogism is always valid; the certainty of the conclusion depends on the certainty of the premises.

فصل في أن الفلك قابل للحركة المستديرة. الأول، لكن فرض الميل على النسبة المذكورة ممكن، فهذا المحال إنما لزم من فرض تحرك الجسم الذي لا ميل فيه أصلا، فيكون محالا، ونقول أيضا إن الفلك لا يكون في طبعه مبدأ ميل مستقيم، وإلا لكانت الطبيعة الواحدة تقتضي الأثرين المتنافيين، هذا خلف. فرض الميل: فإن قيل: الميل من مقولة الكيف، والزمان من مقولة الكم، فكيف يقاس بينهما؛ لأن القياس فرع التجانس، قلت: الميل يتكمم باعتبار العدد العارض له، أو باعتبار الجسم الذي هو فيه، فبهذا الاعتبار يتجانسان فيقاسان بأنه أي نسبة لأحدهما إلى الآخر، فإن قيل: يجوز أن يلزم المحال من المجموع، فكيف يلزم من إمكان الميل على النسبة المذكورة أن المحال إنما لزم من فرض تحرك الجسم الذي لا ميل فيه؟ قلت لما كان المجموع مركبا من الشقين المذكورين وتبين إمكان أحدهما أعني الميل على النسبة المذكورة، تعين أن المحال إنما لزم من الشق الآخر، وهو تحرك الجسم الذي لا ميل فيه فيكون ذلك محالا، فثبت أن كل جسم متحرك لا بد فيه مبدأ ميل، والفلك أيضا قابل للحركة المستديرة فلا بد فيه من مبدأ ميل مستدير، وهو المطلوب. طبعه إذ ثبت أن الفلك في طبعه مبدأ ميل مستدير لا يكون فيه مبدأ ميل مستقيم، وإلا فلا يخلو إما أن يقتضي الطبيعة الواحدة ذينك الميلين، فيلزم اجتماع الأمرين المتنافيين؛ لأن الميل المستدير يقتضي بقاء الجسم في حيزه وتبدل وضع الأجزاء، والميل المستقيم انتقال الجسم عن حيزه، وإما أن يكون لكل واحد منهما طبيعة على حدة، وهو باطل؛ لأن الطبيعة هي الصورة النوعية، وتقوم حقيقة واحدة بصورتين باطل؛ لأن الصورة النوعية الواحدة لما انضمت بالمادة كملت الحقيقة وتحصلت وليس لها افتقار إلى صورة أخرى أصلا، ولو انضمت الصورة بالأخرى إلى المادة لحصلت حقيقة أخرى، فلو تقومت حقيقة واحدة بصورتين لزم كون حقيقة واحدة ذات حقيقتين.
Section on the sphere being capable of circular motion. First, but assuming inclination in the mentioned proportion is possible, so this impossibility only follows from assuming the movement of a body that has no inclination at all, thus it is impossible. And we also say that the sphere does not have in its nature a principle of straight inclination, otherwise a single nature would necessitate two contradictory effects, which is absurd. The assumption of inclination: if it is said: inclination is from the category of quality, and time is from the category of quantity, so how are they measured? Because measurement presupposes homogeneity. I say: inclination becomes quantified either by the accident of number or by the body in which it is, so by this consideration they become homogeneous and are measured by whatever proportion one has to the other. If it is said: it is possible that the impossibility follows from the combination, so how does it follow from the possibility of inclination in the mentioned proportion that the impossibility only follows from assuming the movement of the body that has no inclination? I say: since the combination is composed of the two mentioned parts and the possibility of one of them, namely inclination in the mentioned proportion, has been shown, then it is determined that the impossibility only follows from the other part, which is the movement of the body that has no inclination, so that becomes impossible. Thus it is established that every moving body must have a principle of inclination, and the sphere is also capable of circular motion, so it must have a principle of circular inclination, which is what was desired. Its nature: since it is established that the sphere has in its nature a principle of circular inclination, it does not have a principle of straight inclination. Otherwise, either a single nature would necessitate both inclinations, leading to the combination of two contradictory things—because circular inclination requires the body to remain in its place while the parts change position, and straight inclination is the transfer of the body from its place—or each would have a separate nature, which is false, because nature is the specific form, and it is impossible for a single reality to be constituted by two forms, because when a single specific form combines with matter, the reality is completed and achieved, and it has no need for another form at all. And if another form were combined with matter, another reality would result. So if a single reality were constituted by two forms, it would entail a single reality having two realities.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a demonstration that the celestial sphere (falak) possesses a natural principle of circular inclination (mayl mustadir) and lacks any principle of straight inclination. The author begins by addressing an objection: if one assumes an inclination in a certain proportion, the resulting impossibility is due to the assumption of a body moving without any inclination, not due to the inclination itself. He then argues that the sphere cannot have a straight inclination because that would force a single nature (al-tabi'a al-wahida) to produce two contrary effects (circular and straight motion). Next, he discusses the measurement of inclination and time. Since inclination is a quality and time a quantity, homogeneity is required for comparison; but inclination can be quantified by its accidental measure or by the body it resides in, allowing comparison. A further objection is raised: the impossibility may arise from the combination of the two parts (inclination and no-inclination). The author replies that since one part (inclination in a certain proportion) is possible, the impossibility must stem from the other part (no inclination). This proves that every moving body requires a principle of inclination; the sphere moves circularly, so it has a circular inclination. Finally, he demonstrates that the sphere's nature precludes a straight inclination. If it contained both, either the same nature would necessitate contradictory motions (impossible) or it would have two distinct natures, which is false because one nature (al-sura al-naw'iyya) gives one reality. Therefore, the sphere's inclination is purely circular.

فلك

الحركة المستديرة

C) Moving body
D) Circular motion

Key Takeaway
A categorical syllogism in the first figure yields a certain conclusion when the premises are true; here, the unity of nature forces the sphere to have only circular inclination.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Why the Sphere Cannot Have Straight Inclination
Author disproves the possibility of straight inclination in the sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that a single nature cannot produce two contradictory effects, thereby proving the celestial sphere lacks straight inclination.

Core Idea
A single nature cannot cause both circular and straight motion; the sphere has one nature, so no straight inclination.

After establishing that the sphere has a circular inclination, the author argues that it cannot also have a straight inclination. He presents a dilemma: either the same nature would produce two contradictory effects (circular and straight motion), or the sphere would need two natures. Both are impossible, because a single specific form (sura naw'iyya) gives a single reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial assumption to be disproved
The assumption is that the sphere has a principle of straight inclination in addition to its circular inclination.
The text says 'وإلا' referring to the opposite of the established conclusion.
2
Extract the disjunction of possibilities
The author argues that if the sphere had straight inclination, then either a single nature would necessitate both inclinations, or each inclination would have its own separate nature.
The text says 'إما أن يقتضي الطبيعة الواحدة ذينك الميلين، وإما أن يكون لكل واحد منهما طبيعة على حدة'.
3
Refute the first option
A single nature producing both circular and straight motion is contradictory because circular motion preserves place while straight motion changes place; they cannot coexist from the same nature.
The author says 'لأن الميل المستدير يقتضي بقاء الجسم في حيزه وتبدل وضع الأجزاء، والميل المستقيم انتقال الجسم عن حيزه'.
4
Refute the second option
Having two natures for one body is impossible because nature (tab') is the specific form (sura naw'iyya), and a single substance cannot have two specific forms—that would create two realities.
The author explains: 'تقوم حقيقة واحدة بصورتين باطل' and 'لو انضمت الصورة بالأخرى إلى المادة لحصلت حقيقة أخرى'.
5
Conclude the negation
Since both possibilities lead to absurdity, the original assumption (that the sphere has straight inclination) must be false. Therefore, the sphere's inclination is purely circular.
The conclusion is implicit: the sphere has only circular inclination.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Analyzing the Celestial Sphere's Impermeability via Categorical Syllogism
Proof that the celestial sphere does not accept rupture or healing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a categorical syllogism from a natural philosophical argument and verify its validity using figure and mood.

Core Idea
Rupture and healing require straight motion—the sphere lacks it, so it remains intact.

In this passage, the author argues that the celestial sphere does not undergo rupture or healing. The logical skeleton of this argument is a categorical syllogism. By learning to identify its parts, you will see how Aristotelian logic structures proofs in Islamic natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Find the statement the author is trying to prove. It is the final clause: 'the celestial sphere does not accept rupture and healing.'
2
Extract the premises
The author gives two premises: (1) 'Rupture and healing only occur by straight motion.' (2) 'The celestial sphere does not accept straight motion.'
These are the grounds for the conclusion.
3
Identify the middle term
The term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. Here it is 'straight motion' (al-haraka al-mustaqima).
The middle term connects the major and minor terms.
4
Determine the figure and mood
Check the arrangement of terms. The middle term is predicate in both premises, which makes it the second figure. The major premise is universal affirmative (A), the minor universal negative (E), and the conclusion universal negative (E). Mood: Cesare (AEE-2).
In some classifications, this is Camestres if the major is A and minor E. But the figure is second.
5
Verify validity
Check that the mood satisfies the conditions for the second figure. AEE in the second figure is valid (Cesare). The conclusion necessarily follows: if all R are M and no S is M, then no S is R.
This matches the author's reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الفلك يتحرك الكائنة إما أن تحصل في حيز طبعي أو في حيز غريب، فإن حصلت في حيز غريب فكانت تقتضي ميلا مستقيما إلى حيزها الطبعي، وإن حصلت في حيز طبعي فالصورة الفاسدة كانت قبل الفساد حاصلة في حيز غريب، فكانت تقتضي ميلا مستقيما إلى حيزها الطبعي، وأما أنه لا يقبل الخرق والالتيام فلأن ذلك أيضا إنما يحصل بالحركة المستقيمة، والفلك لا يقبل الحركة المستقيمة، فلا يقبل الخرق والالتيام.
Section on the fact that the celestial sphere moves: The generated things either occur in a natural place or in a foreign place. If they occur in a foreign place, they require a straight inclination towards their natural place. If they occur in a natural place, then the corrupt form before corruption occurred in a foreign place, so it required a straight inclination towards its natural place. As for the fact that it does not accept rupture and healing, that is because that also only occurs by straight motion, and the celestial sphere does not accept straight motion, so it does not accept rupture and healing.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the celestial sphere (falak) does not undergo rupture (kharq) or healing (iltiyam). The author first discusses elemental substances (al-ka'ina) which have natural and foreign places, requiring straight motion. Then he argues that rupture and healing involve separation and reunion of parts, which necessarily involve straight motion. Since the celestial sphere does not have straight motion (it moves circularly), it cannot experience rupture or healing. The argument can be structured as a categorical syllogism: All rupture and healing result from straight motion. The celestial sphere does not undergo straight motion. Therefore, the celestial sphere does not undergo rupture or healing.

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
The Categorical Syllogism for Perpetual Circular Motion
Author proves that the celestial sphere moves perpetually in circular motion because it preserves time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism that proves the celestial sphere moves perpetually in circular motion.

Core Idea
Time is eternal, so its preserving motion must be perpetual – only circular motion fits.

In this passage, the author deploys a categorical syllogism to demonstrate that the motion preserving time cannot be straight (whether infinite or returning) but must be circular. Let's walk through the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
The motion that preserves time (al-haraka al-hafiza li al-zaman) must be perpetual, because time itself is eternal and everlasting. This is implicit but foundational.
Time is defined as the measure of motion (مقدار الحركة).
2
Identify the minor premise
No straight motion can be perpetual. The author proves this by showing that straight motion either goes to infinity (impossible due to infinite distance) or returns (which requires a moment of rest between departure and return).
The impossibility of infinite distance was established earlier.
3
Draw the conclusion
Therefore, no straight motion preserves time. Since the preserver of time must be either straight or circular, and it is not straight, it must be circular.
This is the conclusion of the disjunctive syllogism.
4
Understand the middle term
The middle term is 'perpetual motion' (الحركة الدائمة). It appears as predicate in both premises: the preserver is perpetual, and straight motion is not perpetual.
The syllogism is in the second figure (Camestres).
5
Apply to cosmology
The celestial sphere (falak) is the only body capable of perpetual circular motion, because the elements have natural straight inclination.
This is the final conclusion of the passage.

فصل في أن الفلك يتحرك على الاستدارة دائما لأن الحركة الحافظة للزمان إما أن تكون مستقيمة أو مستديرة، لا جائز ذلك لأن الخرق لا يكون بدون تباعد الأجزاء عن أحيازها، وذلك يستلزم الحركة المستقيمة، والالتيام لا يكون بدون تقارب الأجزاء في أحيازها بعد تباعدها، والتقارب بعد التباعد يستلزم الحركة المستقيمة. فصل إلخ قد ثبت في الفصل السابق كون الفلك قابلا للحركة المستديرة، وههنا يثبت كونه متحركا بالفعل بالحركة المستديرة دائما تقريره: أنك قد علمت في بحث الزمان أنه مقدار الحركة، وأنه أزلي وأبدي، فلا بد له من حركة أزلية وأبدية؛ لكونه قائما بها ومقدارا لها، وإن لم توجد حركة دائمة انعدم الزمان لانعدام محله فالحركة الدائمة الحافظة لوجود الزمان لا تخلو إما أن تكون مستقيمة أو مستديرة، لا جائز أن تكون مستقيمة؛ لأنها إما أن تذهب إلى غير النهاية أو ترجع، والأول محال وإلا لزم وجود بعد غير متناه؛ لأن الحركة لا بد لها من مسافة، والمسافة بعد قائم بالجسم، فلو ذهبت الحركة إلى غير النهاية للزم وجود مسافة غير متناهية، وهو محال لوجوب تناهي الأبعاد كما مر والثاني أيضا محال؛ لأنها لو رجعت لكانت منتهية إلى حد، فتكون الحركة الذاهبة منعدمة، ثم تحدث الحركة الراجعة؛ لأن بين الحركتين المستقيمتين سكونا؛ لأن الميل الموصل إلى ذلك الطرف موجود حال الوصول؛ لأنه يفعل الإيصال حال الوصول، فلو آنات الحركتين المستقيمتين سكونا، وبيانه أن الميل الموصل حين بلوغ الجسم المتحرك بالحركة الذاهبة إلى الحد موجود؛ لأنه فاعل للإيصال حين الوصول، فلو لم يكن موجودا حين الوصول لامتنع الإيصال؛ لامتناع المعلول بدون العلة، فامتنع الوصول؛ لأنه مترتب على الإيصال المترتب على الميل الموصل، وهذا باطل بعد ما فرض الوصول، ولما كان الميل الموصل موجودا حين الوصول لم يحدث فيه الميل المفارق ذلك الحين؛ لامتناع اجتماع الميلين المتنافيين في موضوع واحد في زمان واحد، ف "الآن" الذي فيه الميل الموصل غير الآن الذي فيه الميل المفارق، ولا بد بين الآنين من زمان لامتناع تتالي الآنات وإلا لزم تركب المسافة من الجزء الذي لا يتجزى؛ لأن الزمان ينطبق على الحركة وهي على المسافة، فلو كان الزمان مؤلفا من أجزاء غير متجزية لزم أن يكون بإزائها من الحركة والمسافة أيضا أجزاء غير متجزية؛ لامتناع انطباق الغير المنقسم على المنقسم، وهذا خلف، فثبت أن بين الآنين زمانا، فذلك الزمان زمان السكون؛ لعدم الميل فيه. وحاصل الكلام أن الحركة المستقيمة لا يمكن أن تكون دائمة لوجوب السكون بين الذاهبة والراجعة كما مر، تقريره، فلا تكون حافظة للزمان، فالحافظة ليست إلا المستديرة؛ لإمكان دوامها والعناصر لما ثبت في طبائعها الميل المستقيم ما كان لها أن تدور حول مركزها؛ لامتناع اجتماع الميلين المتضادين أعني الميل المستقيم والمستدير في جسم بسيط، فتعين أن المستديرة الدائمة الحافظة للزمان هي كة الفلك؛ حر لانحصار الجسم فيها وامتناع العناصر أن تكون صائرة للمستديرة. وهي
Section on that the celestial sphere moves circularly perpetually. Because the motion that preserves time is either straight or circular. That is not possible because rupture does not occur without separation of parts from their places, and that entails straight motion, and healing does not occur without approximation of parts in their places after their separation, and approximation after separation entails straight motion. Section etc. It has been established in the previous section that the celestial sphere is capable of circular motion, and here it is established that it is actually moving with circular motion perpetually. Its exposition: You have learned in the discussion of time that it is the measure of motion, and that it is eternal and everlasting, so it must have an eternal and everlasting motion, because it subsists by it and is its measure. If a perpetual motion did not exist, time would cease to exist due to the non-existence of its subject. So the perpetual motion that preserves the existence of time is either straight or circular. It cannot be straight, because it would either go to infinity or return. The first is impossible, otherwise an infinite distance would exist; because motion necessarily has a distance, and distance is an interval subsisting in body. So if motion went to infinity, an infinite distance would exist, which is impossible due to the necessary finitude of dimensions as passed. The second is also impossible, because if it returned, it would be limited to a limit, so the departing motion would cease, then the returning motion would occur, because between two straight motions there is rest. Because the motivating inclination that reaches that limit exists at the moment of arrival, because it effects the arrival at the moment of arrival. So if the inclinations of the two straight motions were consecutive instants, there would be rest. Its explanation: The motivating inclination when the body moving with the departing motion reaches the limit exists, because it is the agent of arrival at the moment of arrival. If it did not exist at the moment of arrival, arrival would be impossible, because of the impossibility of the effect without the cause. So arrival would be impossible, because it depends on the arrival which depends on the motivating inclination. This is false after assuming arrival. And since the motivating inclination exists at the moment of arrival, the opposite inclination does not occur at that moment, because of the impossibility of two opposing inclinations coexisting in one subject at one time. So the 'instant' in which the motivating inclination exists is different from the instant in which the opposite inclination exists. And between the two instants there must be time, because of the impossibility of consecutive instants, otherwise the distance would be composed of indivisible parts; because time corresponds to motion, and motion corresponds to distance. So if time were composed of indivisible parts, then corresponding to them of motion and distance there would also be indivisible parts, because of the impossibility of the indivisible matching the divisible. This is a contradiction. So it is established that between the two instants there is time, and that time is the time of rest, because there is no inclination in it. The summary of the discourse is that straight motion cannot be perpetual because of the necessary rest between the departing and the returning, as its exposition passed. So it is not preservative of time. So the preserver is only the circular motion, because of the possibility of its perpetuity. And the elements, since the straight inclination is established in their natures, they cannot rotate around their center, because of the impossibility of two opposing inclinations, namely the straight and the circular, in a simple body. So it is determined that the perpetual circular preserve of time is the motion of the celestial sphere, because the body is confined to it and the elements cannot become circular. And it is.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a detailed proof that the celestial sphere (falak) moves perpetually in circular motion. The author begins by stating that the motion that preserves time must be either straight or circular. He then refutes straight motion as a candidate: if straight motion goes to infinity, it requires infinite distance, which is impossible; if it returns, there must be a moment of rest between the departing and returning motions. The argument against returning motion relies on the concept of motivating inclination (mayl). When a moving body reaches the endpoint of its departure, the inclination that brought it there exists at that instant. The opposite inclination for the return cannot coexist at the same instant, so there must be a temporal interval between the two motions, which is a time of rest. Thus straight motion cannot be continuous and perpetual. Therefore, the only perpetual motion is circular motion. The author then notes that the four elements have a natural straight inclination, so they cannot move circularly; thus only the celestial sphere can have perpetual circular motion. This argument ties together physics (time, motion, inclination) and cosmology.

الحركة المستديرة
الميل الموصل
الآن

A) Because returning motion requires infinite distance

C) Because the celestial sphere only moves straight
D) Because time is composed of instants

Key Takeaway
The proof that the celestial sphere moves perpetually in a circle rests on a categorical syllogism eliminating straight motion as impossible due to required rest.

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
The Conditional Argument Against Straight Returning Motion
Author proves that the celestial sphere moves perpetually in circular motion because it preserves time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a returning straight motion must involve a moment of rest based on the impossibility of two opposing inclinations at the same instant.

Core Idea
At the instant of arrival, the motivating inclination exists, preventing the opposite inclination from occurring without a time gap.

The author refutes the possibility of perpetual straight motion by showing that a returning motion would require a momentary rest because two opposing inclinations cannot coexist. This argument uses the concept of mayl (impetus) and the nature of instants (aan).

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of mayl muwassil
The 'motivating inclination' (al-mayl al-muwassil) is the force that brings a body to its destination. It exists at the moment of arrival because it causes the arrival.
Without this inclination, arrival would be impossible, as the effect requires a cause.
2
Recognize the opposing inclination for return
For a returning motion to begin, an opposite inclination must exist. This inclination cannot coexist with the arriving inclination at the same instant in the same body.
Two opposing inclinations (ميلان متضادان) cannot be in one subject at one time.
3
Identify the temporal gap
Therefore, the instant of arrival (when the departing inclination exists) must differ from the instant the returning inclination starts. Since instants cannot be consecutive (otherwise time would be composed of atoms), there must be a time interval between them.
This interval lacks any moving inclination, so it is a time of rest (زمان السكون).
4
Formulate the conditional argument
If the returning motion were to follow the departing motion immediately, then the arriving and returning inclinations would coexist at the same instant – impossible. Therefore, a returning motion cannot be immediate; rest intervenes.
This is a modus tollens: If immediate return, then contradictory; not contradictory? Actually: If P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
5
Conclude that straight motion cannot be perpetual
Because any returning straight motion includes a time of rest, straight motion cannot be continuous and perpetual. Hence, only circular motion can preserve time.
Circular motion does not have this problem because it lacks a reversal point.

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
The Sphere's Circular Motion: A Compound Proof
Proof that celestial sphere moves circularly

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the compound syllogism proving that the celestial sphere moves circularly, and identify the key premises and intermediate conclusions.

Core Idea
Rectilinear motion cannot be perpetual; only circular motion can preserve time.

This passage from Hidayat al-Hikma presents a logical demonstration that the celestial sphere (falak) must move in a circle. The proof is a compound syllogism: it first rules out rectilinear motion by showing it leads to necessary rest, then argues that circular motion is the only alternative capable of being perpetual. Understanding this proof sharpens your ability to follow multi-step syllogistic reasoning in Islamic cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The author aims to prove that the celestial sphere moves with circular motion (haraka mustadira). What is the overall conclusion?
The conclusion is stated at the end: the perpetual circular motion that preserves time is the motion of the sphere.
2
Trace the initial disjunction
The author first assumes motion is rectilinear and splits it into two possibilities: going to infinity or reversing. Both are rejected. Why is infinity impossible? Why is reversal impossible?
Infinity is rejected because it would entail an infinite distance, which is impossible. Reversal is rejected because it requires rest between the two motions.
3
Analyze the reversal argument
The argument about reversal uses instantaneous inclinations (mayl). At the moment of arrival, the forward inclination exists; at that same moment, the backward inclination cannot arise because two opposing inclinations cannot coexist. So the forward inclination is at one instant, the backward at another. Between any two distinct instants there is time, and during that time there is no inclination, so the body rests.
This refutes the possibility of perpetual rectilinear motion with reversal. Understanding this requires grasping the concept of instantaneous inclined states (mayl aniyya).
4
Conclude the necessity of circular motion
Since rectilinear motion cannot be perpetual, the only alternative that can be perpetual is circular motion (which has no endpoint and no reversal). The celestial sphere must have perpetual motion to preserve time. Moreover, the elements have rectilinear natures and cannot rotate, so the sphere alone can sustain circular motion.
This step combines the disjunctive syllogism (rectilinear vs. circular) with a final determination of the sphere as the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الفلك يتحرك أن تكون مستقيمة؛ لأنها حينئذ إما أن تذهب إلى غير النهاية أو ترجع لا سبيل إلى الأول وإلا لزم وجود بعد غير متناه، ولا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأنها لو رجعت لكانت تنتهي إلى طرف، فتكون منقضية للسكون؛ لأن بين كل حركتين مستقيمتين سكونا؛ لأن الميل الموصل إلى ذلك الطرف موجود حال الوصول؛ لأنه يفعل الإيصال حال الوصول، فلو لم يكن موجودا حال الوصول لاستحال أن يفعل الوصول، وكلما كان الميل الموصل موجودا لم يحدث فيه ميل يقتضي كونه غير موصل؛ لاستحالة اجتماع الميلين المتنافيين فالحال الذي فيه ميل الوصول غير الحال الذي فيه ميل اللاوصول وكل واحد من الميلين آني؛ لأن الوصول وكونه غير موصل آني؛ لأن حال الوصول لو كان زمانا وانقسم، فحين ما يكون الجسم في أحد طرفيه لم يكن واصلا إلى المنتهى، هذا خلف وكذا حال صيرورته غير موصل، وإذا كان كل واحد من الميلين آنيا لم يجتمعا في آن واحد فكانا في آنين متغايرين وكل آنين لا يخلوان عن زمان بينهما لامتناع تتالي الآنات وإلا لزم تركب المسافة من الجزء الذي لا يتجزى؛ لأن الزمان ينطبق على الحركة وهي على المسافة، فلو كان الزمان مؤلفا من أجزاء غير متجزية لزم أن يكون بإزائها من الحركة والمسافة أيضا أجزاء غير متجزية؛ لامتناع انطباق الغير المنقسم على المنقسم، وهذا خلف، فثبت أن بين الآنين زمانا، فذلك الزمان زمان السكون؛ لعدم الميل فيه. وحاصل الكلام أن الحركة المستقيمة لا يمكن أن تكون دائمة لوجوب السكون بين الذاهبة والراجعة كما مر تقريره، فلا تكون حافظة للزمان، فالحافظة ليست إلا المستديرة؛ لإمكان دوامها والعناصر لما ثبت في طبائعها الميل المستقيم ما كان لها أن تدور حول مركزها؛ لامتناع اجتماع الميلين المتضادين أعني الميل المستقيم والمستدير في جسم بسيط، فتعين أن المستديرة الدائمة الحافظة للزمان هي حركة الفلك؛ لانحصار الجسم فيها وامتناع العناصر أن تكون صائرة للمستديرة.
Chapter on that the celestial sphere moves. It must be rectilinear; because then either it goes to infinity or returns. The first is impossible otherwise an infinite distance would exist. The second is impossible because if it returned, it would end at a limit, so it would terminate in rest; because between every two rectilinear motions there is rest; because the inclination that leads to that limit exists at the moment of arrival; because it effects arrival at the moment of arrival. So if it did not exist at the moment of arrival, it would be impossible for it to effect arrival. And whenever the leading inclination exists, there does not arise in it an inclination requiring it to be non-leading, because of the impossibility of two opposing inclinations coexisting. So the state in which there is the inclination of arrival is other than the state in which there is the inclination of non-arrival. And each of the two inclinations is instantaneous; because arrival and being non-arrival are instantaneous; because if the state of arrival were temporal and divisible, then when the body is in one of its parts, it would not have arrived at the endpoint – this is a contradiction. Similarly the state of becoming non-arrival. And if each of the two inclinations is instantaneous, they do not coexist in a single instant, so they are in two different instants. And every two instants cannot be free of a time between them, because of the impossibility of succession of instants, otherwise the composition of distance from indivisible parts would follow; because time corresponds to motion, and motion to distance. So if time were composed of indivisible parts, then corresponding to them from motion and distance would also be indivisible parts, because of the impossibility of an indivisible thing mapping to a divisible thing – this is a contradiction. So it is established that between the two instants there is time, and that time is time of rest, because there is no inclination in it. The conclusion of the discourse is that rectilinear motion cannot be perpetual because of the necessity of rest between the going and returning, as its explanation passed. So it is not preservative of time. The preservative is only circular motion, because of the possibility of its perpetuity. And the elements, since rectilinear inclination is established in their natures, they cannot rotate around their center; because of the impossibility of two opposing inclinations, namely rectilinear and circular, in a simple body. So it is determined that the perpetual circular motion that preserves time is the motion of the celestial sphere, because of the confinement of body to it and the impossibility of the elements becoming circular.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a syllogistic proof that the celestial sphere (falak) must move with circular motion (haraka mustadira), not rectilinear (mustaqima). The author begins by assuming the motion is rectilinear, then shows this leads to a contradiction. If rectilinear, the motion would either proceed to infinity or reverse. Infinity is impossible (no infinite distance). Reversal would require reaching a limit, then turning back. However, between the forward and backward motion, there must be a state of rest. This is argued through the concept of instantaneous inclinations (mayl). At the moment of arrival at the limit, the inclination to go forward is present. At that same moment, the inclination to go backward cannot arise because two opposing inclinations cannot coexist in the same body at the same instant. Therefore the forward inclination exists at one instant, and the backward inclination at a different instant. Between any two distinct instants there must be a time interval, because instants cannot follow each other without time (otherwise space would be composed of indivisible parts, which is rejected). During that interval, there is no effective inclination, so the body is at rest. Thus rectilinear motion of a going-and-returning type cannot be perpetual; it always involves an interruption of rest. Therefore such motion cannot be preservative of time (hafiza li-l-zaman). Only circular motion can be perpetual because it has no reversal and hence no necessary rest. The celestial sphere, being a simple body, cannot have both rectilinear and circular inclinations; its nature must be one. Since the elements (earth, water, air, fire) have rectilinear inclinations (they move up/down), they cannot rotate. Thus the sphere's motion must be circular. This argument uses the impossibility of infinite regress, the impossibility of two simultaneous opposing inclinations, and the rejection of atomism regarding time and space.

Key Terms
حركة مستقيمة
حركة مستديرة
ميل

The Syllogism

Compound Syllogism in the Proof of Circular Motion
Proof that the celestial sphere's motion is circular and continuous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the compound syllogism used to prove that the celestial sphere moves circularly, identifying each sub-argument and its role.

Core Idea
Time's continuity forces the celestial sphere to move circularly.

This passage from Hidayat al-Hikma presents a tight proof that the celestial sphere's motion must be circular to preserve time. The argument combines a reductio ad absurdum with a constructive syllogism. We will dissect each logical step to see how the conclusion follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reductio assumption
The author assumes that between two instants (āniyān) there is no motion. This is the hypothesis to be refuted.
The phrase 'منهما آنيا' indicates two instants without intervening motion.
2
Derive the atomicity of time
If no motion occurs between two instants, then those instants are directly consecutive, implying time is composed of indivisible atoms (aẓāʾ lā tatajazzaʾ).
3
Apply the correspondence between motion and distance
Since motion corresponds to distance (al-masāfa), the atomicity of time would force distance to be atomic as well. This is deemed absurd (خلف) because distance is continuous.
The author expects the reader to accept that distance is continuous—a standard view in natural philosophy.
4
Conclude the reductio
The contradiction shows that the original assumption (that there exist two instants with no motion between them) is false. Hence, between any two instants there is motion.
This establishes that the motion preserving time is continuous.
5
Determine the motion type
Rectilinear motion has endpoints and pauses, so it cannot satisfy the continuity condition. Therefore the motion must be circular, as circular motion is continuous without stops.
The author adds that this circular motion must be uninterrupted; otherwise time would be interrupted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الفلك يتحرك منهما آنيا وجب أن يكون بين الآنين زمان لا يتحرك فيه الجسم، وإلا لزم تعاقب الآنين، فيكون الزمان مركبا من أجزاء لا تتجزى، ويلزم منه تركب المسافة من أجزاء لا تتجزى؛ لانطباقها على الحركة، هذا خلف، فعلم أن الحركة الحافظة للزمان ليست مستقيمة، فتكون مستديرة، وهذه الحركة غير منقطعة وإلا لزم انقطاع الزمان، فإذن يكون الفلك يتحرك على الاستدارة دائما، وهو المطلوب.
Chapter on that the celestial sphere moves. If from them two instants, it would be necessary that between the two instants there be a time in which the body does not move, otherwise the succession of the two instants would occur, and time would be composed of indivisible parts, and from that would follow the composition of distance from indivisible parts because of its correspondence to motion. This leads to a contradiction. So it is known that the motion preserving time is not rectilinear, so it is circular. And this motion is uninterrupted, otherwise interruption of time would occur. Therefore the celestial sphere moves circularly always, and that is the required conclusion.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by contradiction that the motion preserving time cannot be rectilinear but must be circular. The author begins by supposing that if there are two instantaneous moments (āniyān) between which there is no motion, then time would be composed of indivisible parts (atoms), leading to the same for distance. This is absurd because motion is continuous and corresponds to distance. Therefore, the motion preserving time is not rectilinear (since rectilinear motion has starts and stops) but circular, which is continuous. Finally, such motion must be perpetual; otherwise time would be interrupted. The conclusion is that the celestial sphere (al-falak) moves in a circular path continuously.

Defining 'Motion Preserving Time'
Definition of motion preserving time as continuous and circular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the concept of 'motion preserving time' (al-ḥaraka al-ḥāfiẓa li-l-zamān) and identify its genus and differentia as used in the proof.

Core Idea
Only circular motion can preserve time's continuity.

The author introduces the term 'motion preserving time' (al-ḥaraka al-ḥāfiẓa li-l-zamān). What does this mean? This lesson unpacks the definition implicit in the argument, showing that it serves as a description (rasm) that distinguishes circular motion from rectilinear motion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term is 'al-ḥaraka al-ḥāfiẓa li-l-zamān' (motion preserving time). Locate it in the passage.
It appears near the conclusion: 'فعلم أن الحركة الحافظة للزمان ليست مستقيمة'.
2
Extract the genus
It is a type of motion (ḥaraka). So the genus is 'motion'.
Motion is defined broadly as change of position or state.
3
Extract the differentia
The differentia is that it is continuous (between any two instants there is motion) and circular (unlike rectilinear motion which has pauses).
The author shows that rectilinear motion cannot satisfy the continuity condition.
4
Classify the definition type
This is a description (rasm) rather than a real definition (hadd ḥaqīqī) because it gives accidental properties (continuity, circularity) rather than the essence.
A hadd would specify the essential nature, but here the author only describes what it must be.
5
Test completeness
Does it include all instances of motion preserving time? It includes circular motion, which is continuous. Does it exclude non-instances? It excludes rectilinear motion and any motion with pauses. So it is complete given the shared assumption that only circular motion is truly continuous.
The author assumes that circular motion is the only continuous motion without start/stop.

The Syllogism

Proving Voluntary Motion via Disjunctive Syllogism
Author begins to prove that the celestial sphere's motion is volitional by excluding natural and forced motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the disjunctive syllogism used to prove the celestial sphere's motion is voluntary and identify its logical structure.

Core Idea
To prove a conclusion by exclusion: eliminate all other possibilities.

The author wants to establish that the celestial sphere moves by will (irādī). He does so by framing its motion as one of three exclusive possibilities: voluntary, natural, or forced. By ruling out natural motion (and later forced motion, though the text is cut), only voluntary remains. This is a classic disjunctive syllogism. In this lesson, you will dissect its logical form and apply it to other Islamic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction
Find the three mutually exclusive categories the author lists for the celestial sphere's essential motion: voluntary (irādiyya), natural (ṭabī'iyya), forced (qasriyya). The argument assumes these are exhaustive.
The text says 'law lam takun irādiyya lakānat immā ṭabī'iyya aw qasriyya', which sets up the disjunction.
2
Determine the type of disjunction
Is it exclusive or inclusive? Here the categories are mutually exclusive: a motion cannot be both natural and voluntary at the same time in this context. So it is a 'manī'at al-jam' wa al-khulū' (exclusive).
In classical logic, a disjunctive syllogism relies on the disjunction being exhaustive and exclusive for a valid conclusion.
3
Locate the eliminated option
The author explicitly rules out natural motion: 'lā jā'iza an takūna ṭabī'iyya'. He gives a reason: natural motion involves flight from a disagreeable state, which does not apply to the celestial sphere.
Even though the argument is incomplete, the elimination of natural motion is clear.
4
Draw the conclusion
If the disjunction is exhaustive (voluntary, natural, forced) and natural is impossible, the remaining possibility is either voluntary or forced. The full argument (likely on the next page) would also eliminate forced motion, leaving voluntary as the only valid option.
The conclusion is the conclusion you would draw: therefore, the motion is voluntary.
5
Formalize the syllogism
Write the syllogism in schematic form: Either A, B, or C. Not B. Not C. Therefore A. Here A = voluntary, B = natural, C = forced.
This is a disjunctive syllogism with three terms; in classical logic, it reduces to a series of two-term disjunctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الفلك متحرك بالإرادة لأن حركته الذاتية لو لم تكن إرادية لكانت إما طبعية أو قسرية، لا جائز أن تكون طبعية؛ لأن الحركة الطبعية هرب عن حالة منافرة، وطلب
Chapter on that the celestial sphere moves by will because if its essential motion were not voluntary, it would be either natural or forced. It is not possible for it to be natural because natural motion is flight from a disagreeable state and seeking...
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage introduces a proof that the celestial sphere's motion is volitional (irādī). The author begins with a disjunctive syllogism: the essential motion (ḥaraka dhātiyya) of the celestial sphere must be either voluntary, natural (ṭabī'ī), or forced (qasrī). He then immediately rules out natural motion on the grounds that natural motion involves fleeing from a disagreeable state and seeking a suitable one. The text is unfortunately cut off before completing the argument, but the logical structure is clear: having excluded natural and forced motion (the latter presumably ruled out by later reasoning), the only remaining possibility is voluntary motion. This aligns with the Islamic philosophical tradition where celestial spheres are ensouled and move by choice.

Key Terms
حركة إرادية (ḥaraka irādiyya): Voluntary motion, motion that occurs by choice and will, attributed to the celestial sphere. [Note: In this text, it contrasts with natural (ṭabī'ī) and forced (qasrī) motion; in other contexts, irādī may also refer to human volition.]
حركة طبعية (ḥaraka ṭabī'iyya): Natural motion, defined as movement away from a contrary state and toward a suitable one, based on the body's nature. [Note: Standard Aristotelian concept; in Islamic philosophy, natural motion is typical of sublunary elements.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A disjunctive syllogism eliminates possibilities to arrive at a necessary conclusion; here, excluding natural motion forces the conclusion (eventually) that celestial motion is voluntary.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Using Disjunctive Syllogism to Prove Celestial Motion is Voluntary
Author proves celestial motion is voluntary by eliminating natural and compulsory motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the disjunctive syllogism used to prove that the celestial sphere moves by will, and verify its validity.

Core Idea
The celestial sphere's motion is voluntary because natural and compulsory motions are impossible in circular motion.

In this passage, the author argues that the essential motion of the celestial sphere must be voluntary. He uses a disjunctive syllogism: the motion is either voluntary, natural, or compulsory. By eliminating the last two options, he concludes it is voluntary. This is a classic logical move that you will learn to identify and evaluate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction
The author states that if the essential motion were not voluntary, it would be either natural or compulsory. This sets up a disjunctive syllogism with three options: voluntary, natural, compulsory. The conclusion is voluntary after eliminating the other two.
The disjunction is exhaustive because the author treats these as the only possible types of motion.
2
Analyze the elimination of natural motion
Natural motion is defined as flight from a contrary state or seeking a suitable state. The author shows that circular motion cannot be flight because moving away from a point is simultaneously moving toward it, and flight and approach cannot coexist naturally. Also, natural motion ends in rest when the goal is reached, but circular motion is perpetual. Therefore, circular motion cannot be natural.
This is a two-part argument: one against flight, one against seeking.
3
Analyze the elimination of compulsory motion
Compulsory motion is defined as an external force acting contrary to nature. The author argues that since the celestial sphere has no nature (because it has no natural place to seek), compulsion is impossible. However, he later doubts this premise.
The elimination of compulsory motion relies on the claim that the celestial sphere has no nature.
4
Verify the validity of the disjunctive syllogism
The argument is a valid form: (A or B or C), not B, not C, therefore A. If the disjunction is exhaustive and the eliminations are sound, the conclusion follows. Check for hidden assumptions: the author's doubt about the premise that the celestial sphere has no nature could affect the elimination of compulsory motion.
Validity is about form; soundness depends on premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الفلك متحرك بالإرادة لأن حركته الذاتية لو لم تكن إرادية لكانت إما طبعية أو قسرية، لا جائز أن تكون طبعية؛ لأن الحركة الطبعية هرب عن حالة منافرة، وطلب لحالة ملائمة، وذلك في الحركة المستديرة محال، أما أنها لا يمكن أن تكون هربا فلأن كل نقطة يتحرك عنها الجسم بالحركة المستديرة، فحركته عنها توجهه إليها، والهرب عن الشيء بالطبع استحال أن يكون توجها إليه، وأما أنها ليست طالبة لحالة ملائمة فلأن الطبعية إذا أوصلت الجسم بالحركة إلى الحالة المطلوبة أسكنته، والمستديرة ليست كذلك، ولا جائز أن تكون قسرية؛ لأن القسر على خلاف الطبع، فحيث لا طبع لا قسر فيه. وأما إذا كانت الحالة المطلوبة حاصلة فالحركة الطبعية لا تتصور وأنت تعلم أن الفلك في الحيز الطبعي، ولا يجوز عليه الانتقال عن حيزه والحركة الطبعية لطلب الحيز الطبعي إنما تتصور إذا كان الجسم فاقدا للحيز الطبعي فالحركة الطبعية لطلب الحيز الطبعي، لا تتصور على الفلك؛ لكونه في حيزه الطبعي دائما. في الحركة لا بد للحركة الطبعية من الهرب عن الشيء والطلب للشيء، وفي الحركة المستديرة لا يمكن كل منها، فلا يكون الحركة المستديرة طبعية، أما الأول فلأن كل وضع يفرض عنه الهرب يكون له المطلب؛ لأتحاد المبدأ والمنتهى في المستديرة، فالمبدأ الذي هرب عنه يرجع إليه بالآخرة، فلزم اتحاد المهروب عنه والمطلوب، وأما الثاني فلأن كل وضع يفرض له الطلب، يكون عنه الهرب لاتحاد المبدأ والمنتهى، فالمنتهى الذي يطلبه هو المبدأ الذي هرب عنه، فالمطلوب هو المهروب عنه. لأن القسر : القسر: تأثير أمر خارج على خلاف ما يقتضيه الطبع، فلما لم يكن هناك طبع لم يكن قسر؛ لأن تأثير القسر على خلاف الطبع يقتضي وجود الطبع وعندي فيه نظر؛ لأنهم أثبتوا أن كل جسم فله حيز طبعي، فالفلك له حيز طبعي ومعنى الحيز الطبعي ما يقتضيه الطبع، فثبت للفلك وجود الطبع؛ بناء على هذا الأصل .
Section: That the celestial sphere moves by will. Because if its essential motion were not voluntary, it would be either natural or compulsory. It is not possible that it be natural, because natural motion is flight from a contrary state and seeking a suitable state, and that is impossible in circular motion. As for it not being flight, because every point from which the body moves in circular motion, its motion from it is a direction toward it, and flight from something by nature cannot be a direction toward it. And as for it not seeking a suitable state, because natural motion, when it brings the body to the desired state, causes it to rest, and circular motion is not like that. And it is not possible that it be compulsory, because compulsion is against nature, and where there is no nature, there is no compulsion. And when the desired state is attained, natural motion is inconceivable. You know that the celestial sphere is in its natural place, and it is not permissible for it to leave its place. Natural motion seeking the natural place is only conceivable when the body lacks its natural place; thus natural motion is inconceivable for the celestial sphere because it is always in its natural place. In motion, natural motion must involve flight from something and seeking something, and in circular motion neither is possible, so circular motion is not natural. As for the first, because every position from which flight is supposed, that position is the sought object, due to the identity of beginning and end in circular motion; the beginning from which it fled returns to it ultimately, so the fled-from and the sought are identical. As for the second, because every position for which seeking is supposed, from it is flight due to the identity of beginning and end; the end it seeks is the beginning from which it fled, so the sought is the fled-from. As for compulsion: compulsion is the effect of an external thing contrary to what nature requires. Since there is no nature, there is no compulsion, because the effect of compulsion contrary to nature requires the existence of nature. But I have a doubt about this because they established that every body has a natural place, so the celestial sphere has a natural place, and the meaning of natural place is what nature requires, so the existence of nature for the celestial sphere is established based on this principle.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is an argument to prove that the celestial sphere (al-falak) moves by will (irada), not by nature or compulsion. The author uses a disjunctive syllogism: the essential motion is either voluntary, natural, or compulsory. He eliminates natural and compulsory, leaving voluntary. To eliminate natural motion, he shows that natural motion must be either flight from a contrary state or seeking a suitable state, or both. Circular motion cannot be flight because every departure from a point is also an approach to that point; you cannot naturally flee from something while moving toward it. Circular motion cannot be seeking a suitable state because natural motion ends when the goal is reached, causing rest, but circular motion is perpetual. Compulsory motion is also impossible because compulsion requires a pre-existing nature to be contrary to; the celestial sphere has no nature to be compelled against. However, the author adds a critical remark ('indī fīhi naẓar) questioning the argument that the celestial sphere has no nature. He notes that if every body has a natural place (al-ḥayyiz al-ṭabīʿī), then the celestial sphere must also have a nature, as it has a natural place. This introduces a potential objection against the proof. The argument is syllogistic, moving from general principles to a specific conclusion, and includes a self-critical note typical of philosophical inquiry. No fiqh or kalam examples are given, but the method of qiyas iqtirani (disjunctive) is clear.

Key Terms

Evaluating the Author's Self-Critical Doubt in Logical Proofs
Author proves celestial motion is voluntary by eliminating natural and compulsory motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical status of the author's doubt ('indī fīhi naẓar) and assess whether it undermines the proof.

Core Idea
A logical proof is only as strong as its premises; the author's doubt exposes a hidden assumption about nature and natural place.

At the end of his proof, the author adds a critical note: 'But I have a doubt about this.' He questions the premise that the celestial sphere has no nature, pointing out that if every body has a natural place, then the celestial sphere must have a nature because it has a natural place. This lesson shows you how to evaluate such self-critical remarks and their impact on the argument.

Guided Path
1
Locate the doubt in the passage
The author writes 'وعندي فيه نظر' (But I have a doubt about this) after concluding that compulsory motion is impossible because there is no nature. This indicates he is questioning a key premise of his own proof.
The doubt is about the claim that the celestial sphere has no nature.
2
Identify the premise being doubted
The premise is that the celestial sphere has no nature (ṭabʿ). The author earlier used this to argue that compulsion is impossible. Now he notes that if every body has a natural place, then the celestial sphere, having a natural place, must have a nature.
This premise is crucial for eliminating compulsory motion.
3
Analyze the objection
The objection is that the definition of natural place implies nature. Since the celestial sphere occupies its natural place, it must have a nature. This would contradict the earlier claim that it has no nature, thus potentially allowing compulsory motion.
The objection is based on a general principle: 'every body has a natural place.'
4
Assess the impact on the proof
If the doubt is valid, the elimination of compulsory motion fails, and the disjunctive syllogism is incomplete. The proof that motion is voluntary would then rely only on the elimination of natural motion. However, the author does not resolve the doubt; he leaves it as a point of further investigation.
This shows that logical proofs in Islamic philosophy often include self-criticism and invite further scrutiny.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الفلك متحرك بالإرادة لأن حركته الذاتية لو لم تكن إرادية لكانت إما طبعية أو قسرية، لا جائز أن تكون طبعية؛ لأن الحركة الطبعية هرب عن حالة منافرة، وطلب لحالة ملائمة، وذلك في الحركة المستديرة محال، أما أنها لا يمكن أن تكون هربا فلأن كل نقطة يتحرك عنها الجسم بالحركة المستديرة، فحركته عنها توجهه إليها، والهرب عن الشيء بالطبع استحال أن يكون توجها إليه، وأما أنها ليست طالبة لحالة ملائمة فلأن الطبعية إذا أوصلت الجسم بالحركة إلى الحالة المطلوبة أسكنته، والمستديرة ليست كذلك، ولا جائز أن تكون قسرية؛ لأن القسر على خلاف الطبع، فحيث لا طبع لا قسر فيه. وأما إذا كانت الحالة المطلوبة حاصلة فالحركة الطبعية لا تتصور وأنت تعلم أن الفلك في الحيز الطبعي، ولا يجوز عليه الانتقال عن حيزه والحركة الطبعية لطلب الحيز الطبعي إنما تتصور إذا كان الجسم فاقدا للحيز الطبعي فالحركة الطبعية لطلب الحيز الطبعي، لا تتصور على الفلك؛ لكونه في حيزه الطبعي دائما. في الحركة لا بد للحركة الطبعية من الهرب عن الشيء والطلب للشيء، وفي الحركة المستديرة لا يمكن كل منها، فلا يكون الحركة المستديرة طبعية، أما الأول فلأن كل وضع يفرض عنه الهرب يكون له المطلب؛ لأتحاد المبدأ والمنتهى في المستديرة، فالمبدأ الذي هرب عنه يرجع إليه بالآخرة، فلزم اتحاد المهروب عنه والمطلوب، وأما الثاني فلأن كل وضع يفرض له الطلب، يكون عنه الهرب لاتحاد المبدأ والمنتهى، فالمنتهى الذي يطلبه هو المبدأ الذي هرب عنه، فالمطلوب هو المهروب عنه. لأن القسر : القسر: تأثير أمر خارج على خلاف ما يقتضيه الطبع، فلما لم يكن هناك طبع لم يكن قسر؛ لأن تأثير القسر على خلاف الطبع يقتضي وجود الطبع وعندي فيه نظر؛ لأنهم أثبتوا أن كل جسم فله حيز طبعي، فالفلك له حيز طبعي ومعنى الحيز الطبعي ما يقتضيه الطبع، فثبت للفلك وجود الطبع؛ بناء على هذا الأصل .
Section: That the celestial sphere moves by will. Because if its essential motion were not voluntary, it would be either natural or compulsory. It is not possible that it be natural, because natural motion is flight from a contrary state and seeking a suitable state, and that is impossible in circular motion. As for it not being flight, because every point from which the body moves in circular motion, its motion from it is a direction toward it, and flight from something by nature cannot be a direction toward it. And as for it not seeking a suitable state, because natural motion, when it brings the body to the desired state, causes it to rest, and circular motion is not like that. And it is not possible that it be compulsory, because compulsion is against nature, and where there is no nature, there is no compulsion. And when the desired state is attained, natural motion is inconceivable. You know that the celestial sphere is in its natural place, and it is not permissible for it to leave its place. Natural motion seeking the natural place is only conceivable when the body lacks its natural place; thus natural motion is inconceivable for the celestial sphere because it is always in its natural place. In motion, natural motion must involve flight from something and seeking something, and in circular motion neither is possible, so circular motion is not natural. As for the first, because every position from which flight is supposed, that position is the sought object, due to the identity of beginning and end in circular motion; the beginning from which it fled returns to it ultimately, so the fled-from and the sought are identical. As for the second, because every position for which seeking is supposed, from it is flight due to the identity of beginning and end; the end it seeks is the beginning from which it fled, so the sought is the fled-from. As for compulsion: compulsion is the effect of an external thing contrary to what nature requires. Since there is no nature, there is no compulsion, because the effect of compulsion contrary to nature requires the existence of nature. But I have a doubt about this because they established that every body has a natural place, so the celestial sphere has a natural place, and the meaning of natural place is what nature requires, so the existence of nature for the celestial sphere is established based on this principle.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is an argument to prove that the celestial sphere (al-falak) moves by will (irada), not by nature or compulsion. The author uses a disjunctive syllogism: the essential motion is either voluntary, natural, or compulsory. He eliminates natural and compulsory, leaving voluntary. To eliminate natural motion, he shows that natural motion must be either flight from a contrary state or seeking a suitable state, or both. Circular motion cannot be flight because every departure from a point is also an approach to that point; you cannot naturally flee from something while moving toward it. Circular motion cannot be seeking a suitable state because natural motion ends when the goal is reached, causing rest, but circular motion is perpetual. Compulsory motion is also impossible because compulsion requires a pre-existing nature to be contrary to; the celestial sphere has no nature to be compelled against. However, the author adds a critical remark ('indī fīhi naẓar) questioning the argument that the celestial sphere has no nature. He notes that if every body has a natural place (al-ḥayyiz al-ṭabīʿī), then the celestial sphere must also have a nature, as it has a natural place. This introduces a potential objection against the proof. The argument is syllogistic, moving from general principles to a specific conclusion, and includes a self-critical note typical of philosophical inquiry. No fiqh or kalam examples are given, but the method of qiyas iqtirani (disjunctive) is clear.

The Syllogism

Analyzing the Argument on Celestial Motion
Author examines whether circular motion is natural, forced, or neither.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that the celestial sphere's circular motion is neither natural nor forced, identifying the key premises and the author's critical note.

Core Idea
Circular motion cannot be natural because its beginning and end coincide.

This passage examines whether the perpetual circular motion of the celestial sphere is natural, forced, or something else. The author uses a series of logical steps to eliminate both possibilities, then adds a critical observation that challenges the consistency of the philosophers' own principles. By breaking down the argument, you will learn how to isolate premises and conclusions in complex physical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find the conclusion the author is trying to prove. The passage states that the sphere's motion is neither natural nor forced.
2
Extract the argument against natural motion
The author gives two reasons: circular motion cannot be a flight (because fleeing from a point means moving away, but in circular motion you return to the same point) and it cannot be a seeking (because natural motion stops when the goal is reached, but circular motion continues).
These are two separate syllogisms with a common conclusion.
3
Extract the argument against forced motion
Forced motion requires a nature to act against; the author initially claims the sphere has no nature (since it's in its natural place), so force is impossible.
This is a conditional argument: if no nature, then no force.
4
Analyze the author's critical note
The author points out a contradiction: the philosophers themselves assert that every body has a natural place, implying the sphere does have a nature. This undermines the earlier premise.
This shows the author is aware of a potential flaw in the argument.
5
Reconstruct the overall logical structure
Combine the steps: the passage contains a disjunctive syllogism (either natural or forced) with two reductio-style arguments, plus a critical meta-comment.
Recognize that the final conclusion is left open: if neither natural nor forced, then the motion must be voluntary (a point often made in Islamic philosophy).
