الفن الثاني في الفلكيات
فصل في أن الفلك يتحرك

لحالة ملائمة، وذلك في الحركة المستديرة محال، أما أنها لا يمكن أن تكون هربا فلأن كل نقطة يتحرك عنها الجسم بالحركة المستديرة، فحركته عنها توجهه إليها، والهرب عن الشيء بالطبع استحال أن يكون توجها إليه، وأما أنها ليست طالبة لحالة ملائمة فلأن الطبعية إذا أوصلت الجسم بالحركة إلى الحالة المطلوبة أسكنته، والمستديرة ليست كذلك، ولا جائز أن تكون قسرية؛ لأن القسر على خلاف الطبع، فحيث لا طبع لا قسر فيه.

وأما إذا كانت الحالة المطلوبة حاصلة فالحركة الطبعية لا تتصور وأنت تعلم أن الفلك في الحيز الطبعي، ولا يجوز عليه الانتقال عن حيزه والحركة الطبعية لطلب الحيز الطبعي إنما تتصور إذا كان الجسم فاقدا للحيز الطبعي فالحركة الطبعية لطلب الحيز الطبعي، لا تتصور على الفلك؛ لكونه في حيزه الطبعي دائما. في الحركة لا بد للحركة الطبعية من الهرب عن الشيء والطلب للشيء، وفي الحركة المستديرة لا يمكن كل منها، فلا تكون الحركة المستديرة طبعية، أما الأول فلأن كل وضع يفرض عنه الهرب يكون له المطلب؛ لاتحاد المبدأ والمنتهى في المستديرة، فالمبدأ الذي هرب عنه يرجع إليه بالآخرة، فلزم اتحاد المهروب عنه والمطلوب، وأما الثاني فلأن كل وضع يفرض له الطلب، يكون عنه الهرب لاتحاد المبدأ والمنتهى، فالمنتهى الذي يطلبه هو المبدأ الذي هرب عنه، فالمطلوب هو المهروب عنه.

لأن القسر: تأثير أمر خارج على خلاف ما يقتضيه الطبع، فلما لم يكن هناك طبع لم يكن قسر؛ لأن تأثير القسر على خلاف الطبع يقتضي وجود الطبع وعندي فيه نظر؛ لأنهم أثبتوا أن كل جسم فله حيز طبعي، فالفلك له حيز طبعي ومعنى الحيز الطبعي ما يقتضيه الطبع، فثبت للفلك وجود الطبع؛ بناء على هذا الأصل.
The second art on celestial things. Chapter on that the celestial sphere moves. For a suitable state, and this in circular motion is impossible. As for that it cannot be a flight, because every point from which the body moves in circular motion, its motion from it is a direction towards it, and flight from something by nature cannot be a direction towards it. And as for that it is not seeking a suitable state, because if natural motion brings the body by movement to the desired state it settles it, and circular motion is not like that. And it is not possible that it be forced, because force is contrary to nature; where there is no nature there is no force in it. And if the desired state is already present, then natural motion is inconceivable, and you know that the celestial sphere is in its natural place, and it is not permissible for it to move from its place. Natural motion for seeking the natural place is only conceivable if the body lacks the natural place. So natural motion for seeking the natural place is not conceivable for the sphere, because it is always in its natural place. In motion, natural motion must involve fleeing from something and seeking something, and in circular motion neither is possible, so circular motion is not natural. As for the first [point], because every position from which fleeing is assumed, there is a sought [position] for it, due to the identity of beginning and end in circular motion: the beginning from which it fled returns to it ultimately, so the fled-from and the sought become identical. As for the second, because every position for which seeking is assumed, there is fleeing from it due to the identity of beginning and end, so the end which it seeks is the beginning from which it fled, so the sought is the fled-from. Because force is the effect of an external thing contrary to what nature requires. Since there was no nature, there was no force, because the effect of force contrary to nature requires the existence of nature. However I have a consideration: because they established that every body has a natural place, so the sphere has a natural place, and the meaning of natural place is what nature requires. So the existence of nature for the sphere is established based on this principle.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage argues that the circular motion of the celestial sphere cannot be natural or forced. The author begins by stating that circular motion cannot be a flight (harb) from something, because in circular motion, moving from a point means moving towards it, whereas flight by nature would be moving away. Next, it cannot be seeking a suitable state (talab li-hala malai'ma) because natural motion, when it reaches the desired state, stops, but circular motion does not stop. It also cannot be forced (qasriyya) because force is contrary to nature, and where there is no nature, there is no force. The author then elaborates: natural motion requires fleeing from a state and seeking a state, but in circular motion, the beginning and end are the same, so the fled-from and the sought are identical, which is contradictory. Finally, the author raises a critical note ('indi fihi nazar): the logicians/philosophers themselves hold that every body has a natural place (hiz tabi'i), and the sphere is in its natural place, so it does have a nature, which would allow for forced motion. The author questions the consistency of their position.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
The mover of the celestial sphere must be incorporeal
Author proves mover is incorporeal via syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and validate the categorical syllogism proving the incorporeality of the celestial sphere's mover.

Core Idea
A corporeal power is finite because it is divisible; infinite motion requires an incorporeal mover.

This passage from Hidayat al-Hikma presents a rigorous proof that the mover of the celestial sphere cannot be a body or a power in a body. The author uses a categorical syllogism and a detailed argument about divisibility to show that corporeal powers are inherently finite, while the sphere's motion is perpetual and infinite. By working through the logical steps, you will see how the principle of whole and part leads to the conclusion that the mover must be incorporeal—a key idea in Islamic natural philosophy and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is stated at the beginning: 'the mover of the sphere is not a corporeal power' (المحرك للفلك ليست قوة جسمانية). This is what we need to prove.
2
Extract the major premise
The major premise is 'no corporeal power is capable of infinite actions' (لا شيء من القوى الجسمانية تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية). This is a universal negative statement about all corporeal powers.
3
Extract the minor premise
The minor premise is 'the moving power of the sphere is capable of infinite actions' (القوة المحركة للفلك تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية). This is a universal affirmative statement about the sphere's mover.
4
Determine the syllogistic form
The middle term is 'capable of infinite actions', which is the predicate in both premises. This places the syllogism in the second figure. The mood is EAE (Cesare), which is valid: No P is M, All S is M, so No S is P.
P = corporeal power, M = capable of infinite actions, S = mover of sphere.
5
Analyze the supporting argument on divisibility
The author supports the major premise with a detailed argument showing that corporeal powers are divisible and thus finite. The total capacity is the sum of parts; if a part were capable of infinite, the whole would be more than infinite, which is absurd. This reinforces why no corporeal power can be infinite.
This is an expansion (probe) that justifies the major premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن القوة المتحركة للفلك يجب أن تكون مجردة عن المادة

لأن القوة المحركة للفلك تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية، ولا شيء من القوى الجسمانية كذلك، فالمحرك للفلك ليست قوة جسمانية، وإنما قلنا: إن القوة الجسمانية لا تقوى على تحريكات غير متناهية؛ لأن كل قوة جسمانية تقبل الانقسام حسب انقسام الجسم، فإذا قسمنا الجسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام مثلا، فقد انقسمت القوة الجسمانية التي فيه أيضا إلى ثلاثة أقسام، وكل جزء من القوة يقدر على أثر، وجملة القوة تقوى على مجموع آثار جميع الأجزاء، وإن لم تقدر جملة القوة على آثار جميع الأجزاء، فإما أن لا تقدر على شيء أصلا، وهو محال بالضرورة، أو تقدر على أثر مثلي أثر بعض الأجزاء، فيلزم مساواة الجزء للكل في التأثير، وهذا باطل، فتعين أن جملة القوة تقوى على مجموع آثار جميع الأجزاء، ومتى كان كذلك، فحملة القوة لا تقوى على آثار غير متناهية؛ لأن جزء القوة لا يخلو إما أن يقدر على آثار متناهية من مبدأ معين أو على آثار غير متناهية من ذلك المبدأ، لا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأن جملة القوة إذا كانت تقوى على مجموع آثار جميع الأجزاء والجزء يقدر على أثر معين، فلو قدر الجزء على غير متناهية لكانت جملة القوة تقوى على أكثر من غير متناه، وهو محال.
Chapter on that the moving power of the celestial sphere must be abstracted from matter. Because the moving power of the sphere is capable of infinite actions, and no corporeal power is like that, so the mover of the sphere is not a corporeal power. And we said that corporeal power is not capable of infinite motions because every corporeal power is divisible according to the division of the body. So if we divide the body into three parts, for example, then the corporeal power in it is also divided into three parts. Each part of the power is capable of an effect, and the total power is capable of the sum of the effects of all the parts. If the total power were not capable of the effects of all the parts, then either it is not capable of anything at all—which is necessarily impossible—or it is capable of an effect equal to twice the effect of some part, which would imply equality of the part and the whole in effect, and this is false. So it is determined that the total power is capable of the sum of the effects of all the parts. And when this is the case, the total power is not capable of infinite effects, because each part of the power cannot be but either capable of finite effects from a given starting point or of infinite effects from that starting point. The second is impossible, because if the total power is capable of the sum of the effects of all the parts and a part is capable of a specific effect, then if the part were capable of an infinite [number of effects], the total power would be capable of more than infinite, which is absurd.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents a syllogistic proof that the mover of the celestial sphere must be incorporeal (mujarrada 'an al-madda). The key premise is that corporeal powers are divisible along with their bodies, and thus each part has a finite capacity. The total capacity is the sum of the parts' capacities. If a corporeal power were capable of infinite actions, then a part would have to be capable of infinite actions as well (since the whole is the sum of parts), leading to the absurdity that the whole would be capable of more than infinite. Therefore, no corporeal power can perform infinite actions. Since the sphere's motion is perpetual (infinite in duration), its mover cannot be a corporeal power; hence it must be an incorporeal, i.e., intellectual or spiritual, power. The argument relies on the principle that the whole is the sum of its parts in terms of capacity.

Key Terms
قوة جسمانية
قوة مجردة
أفعال غير متناهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) 100 kg
D) Infinite

Key Takeaway
The incorporeality of the celestial mover is established by a valid second-figure syllogism and a physical argument that corporeal powers are necessarily finite due to divisibility.

In the divisibility argument, the author claims that if a part of a corporeal power were capable of infinite effects, the whole would be capable of more than infinite. Explain why this is considered absurd, and evaluate whether this reasoning is valid for any corporeal power.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Proximate Mover is a Corporeal Power: A Categorical Syllogism
Author proves that the proximate mover is a corporeal power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the implicit categorical syllogism in the proof that the proximate mover of the sphere is corporeal and verify its validity.

Core Idea
Particular motion demands a particular, divisible power – hence corporeal.

This passage from Hidayat al-Hikma tackles a key question in Islamic cosmology: what is the nature of the mover directly responsible for the sphere's daily rotations? The author argues it cannot be a purely immaterial intellect, but must be a corporeal power. To follow this proof, you will learn to extract the underlying categorical syllogism and assess its logical strength.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Find the main claim the author is trying to prove. It is stated at the beginning: 'the proximate mover of the sphere is a corporeal power'.
2
Find the minor premise
Locate the premise that links the subject (proximate mover) to the middle term. The author argues that the proximate mover has particular conceptions because its motions are particular and voluntary. So the minor premise is: 'The proximate mover has particular perception'.
The middle term is 'having particular perception'.
3
Find the major premise
The author establishes that anything with particular perception must be corporeal. This is proven by the divisibility argument: particular images differ in size, so their locus must be divisible, a property of bodies. So the major premise is: 'Everything with particular perception is corporeal'.
4
Construct the categorical syllogism
Combine the premises and conclusion: Major: All things with particular perception are corporeal. Minor: The proximate mover has particular perception. Conclusion: The proximate mover is corporeal. This is a first-figure Barbara syllogism (AAA-1).
5
Verify validity
Check that the middle term ('particular perception') is distributed in the major premise and appears in the minor. Both premises are affirmative and universal, and the conclusion follows necessarily. The proof is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن المحرك القريب للفلك قوة جسمانية لأن التحريكات الاختيارية لا تقع إلا عن إرادة إما أن تقع عن تصور كلي أو جزئي، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأن التصور الكلي نسبته إلى جميع الجزئيات على السوية، فلا يقع منه بعض الحركات الجزئية دون بعض وإلا لزم الترجيح بلا مرجح فمبدأ التحريكات الجزئية له تصورات جزئية، وكل ما له تصور جزئي فهو جسماني؛ لأن الصورة الجزئية ترتسم وهي أصغر، وترتسم وهي أكبر، فإما أن يكون الاختلاف في الصغر والكبر لاختلاف الصورتين بالحقيقة أو لاختلاف المأخوذ عنه الصورتان بالصغر والكبر، أو لاختلافهما في المحل من المدرك، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأنا أخذنا صورتين من نوع واحد، ذلك الاتحاد النوعي بينهما قد تختلفان بالصغر والكبر، فلا دخل لاختلاف الماهية في الصغر والكبر، ولا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأن الصورتين قد تختلفان بالصغر والكبر في التصور من غير أن تؤخذ من شيئين مختلفين بالصغر والكبر في الخارج، كما إذا تصورنا قبة من ياقوت وجبلا من ياقوت من غير أن نأخذ صورتيهما من شيء في الخارج؛ إذ ليس في الخارج قبة ياقوت ولا جبله حتى نأخذ صورتيهما منهما، فتبين أن اختلاف الصورتين بالصغر والكبر لا دخل فيه للمأخوذ عنه، فتبين القسم الثالث فمحل ارتسام الصغيرة غير محل ارتسام الكبيرة، فمحل ارتسامهما مختلف بالصغر والكبر، فينقسم محل ارتسامهما، فهو جسماني؛ لأن الانقسام إلى المقدار الصغير والكبير من خواص الأجسام، لا يمكن ذلك في الذات المجردة عن المادة. ويكفي لإثبات هذا المرام أن يقال: إن الصورة الجزئية المادية لكونها ذات وضع ومقدار لا يمكن أن ترتسم في ذات مجردة.
Chapter on that the proximate mover of the sphere is a corporeal power. Because voluntary motions only occur from will, either occurring from a universal or particular conception. The first is impossible because the universal conception is equally related to all particulars, so some particular motions would not occur from it without others, otherwise preference without a preponderant would be necessary. So the principle of particular motions has particular conceptions, and everything that has a particular conception is corporeal; because the particular image is depicted small and depicted large. So the difference in smallness and largeness is either due to a difference in the two images in reality, or due to a difference in that from which the two images are taken in smallness and largeness, or due to a difference in the locus of perception. The first is impossible because we have taken two images of the same type; that specific unity between them may differ in smallness and largeness, so difference in essence has no part in smallness and largeness. The second is also impossible because the two images may differ in smallness and largeness in conception without being taken from two things differing in smallness and largeness externally, as when we conceive a dome of ruby and a mountain of ruby without taking their two images from something external; since there is no dome of ruby nor its mountain externally from which we take their two images. So it is clear that the difference of the two images in smallness and largeness has no involvement from that from which they are taken. Thus the third division is established: the locus of depiction of the small is not the locus of depiction of the large. So their locus of depiction differs in smallness and largeness, so the locus of depiction is divisible, so it is corporeal; because division into small and large quantity is a property of bodies, not possible in an immaterial essence. It suffices to prove this point to say: the particular material image, because it has position and quantity, cannot be depicted in an immaterial essence.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the proximate mover (muharrik qarib) of the celestial sphere must be a corporeal power (quwwa jismāniyya), not a purely immaterial intellect. The author argues as follows: Voluntary motions of the sphere are particular and require a particular conception (taṣawwur juz’ī) because a universal conception (taṣawwur kullī) relates equally to all particulars and cannot explain why this motion occurs now and not that. (This is the familiar problem of preference without a preponderant.) Therefore, the mover must have particular conceptions. Now, anything that has particular conceptions is corporeal. Why? Because a particular image (ṣūra juz’iyya) can be depicted as small or large, and this difference cannot be due to the essence of the images (since they are of the same type) nor due to the external object from which they are taken (since we can conceive an image without an external object, e.g., a ruby dome). Hence, the difference must be in the locus of depiction (maḥall al-irtisām): the small image and the large image are depicted in different loci. Therefore, the locus is divisible into small and large parts, which is a property of bodies (ajsām), not of an immaterial substance (dhāt mujarrada). Thus, the faculty that receives these particular images is corporeal. The author then offers a simpler argument: a particular material image has position (waḍ‘) and quantity (miqdār), so it cannot inhere in an immaterial essence. This proof uses categorical syllogisms implicitly: Major: Everything with particular perception is corporeal (established by the divisibility argument). Minor: The proximate mover has particular perception (from the necessity of particular conception for particular motion). Conclusion: Therefore, the proximate mover is corporeal. This is a standard argument in Islamic philosophy regarding the celestial souls.

Key Terms
المحرك القريب
التصور الجزئي
قوة جسمانية
ارتسام

Key Takeaway
The proof that the proximate mover is corporeal rests on a valid categorical syllogism: the major premise (all things with particular perception are corporeal) is established via the divisibility argument, and the minor premise links the mover to particular perception.

The Proximate Mover of Partial Motions is Corporeal
Author proves that the proximate mover of particular motions must be a corporeal power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism proving that the proximate mover of partial motions is corporeal.

Core Idea
Particular perceptions require a divisible substrate; hence the proximate mover must be corporeal.

In this passage, the author demonstrates through syllogistic reasoning that the immediate mover of particular celestial motions cannot be an abstract intellect but must be a corporeal power. Understanding this argument sharpens your ability to apply categorical logic to natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The author's desired conclusion is that the proximate mover of partial motions is corporeal (جسماني). This is the proposition to be proven.
The conclusion is stated explicitly: 'فمبدأ التحريكات الجزئية جسماني'.
2
Find the major premise
The major premise is: 'Whatever has a particular perception (تصور جزئي) is corporeal.' This is established by the argument that particular perceptions require a divisible substrate.
The passage argues that particular perceptions cannot be imprinted in an abstract essence, so they must be in a corporeal power.
3
Find the minor premise
The minor premise is: 'The principle of partial motions (مبدأ التحريكات الجزئية) has a particular perception.'
This is implicit: the mover of partial motions must perceive those motions individually, hence has a particular perception.
4
Identify the middle term
The middle term is 'particular perception' (تصور جزئي), which links the subject (mover) to the predicate (corporeal).
The middle term appears in both premises.
5
Reconstruct the syllogism and verify validity
The syllogism is: All A are B (particular perception → corporeal). All C are A (mover has particular perception). Therefore, all C are B. This is first figure, Barbara mood, and valid because the middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor.
Barbara mood requires universal affirmative premises and conclusion, which holds here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني في الفلكيات فصل في أن المحرك القريب إلى الأول؛ لأنا نتكلم في الصورتين من نوع واحد، ولا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأن الصورة المختلفة بالصغر والكبر لا يجب أن تكون مأخوذة من خارج، فتعين القسم الثالث، فتكون الكبيرة منهما مرتسمة في غير ما ارتسمت فيه الصغيرة، فينقسم المدرك لا محالة في الوضع، فما هذا شأنه فهو جسماني، فهو المطلوب. - لأنها لو ارتسمت في ذات محردة لزم كون ذات محردة ذات وضع ومقدار، فلا تبقى مجردة، وهذا خلف فلا بد أن ترتسم في قوة جسمانية، فثبت أن ما له تصور جزئي فهو جسماني، وهو المطلوب. جسماني ولينتج فمبدأ التحريكات الجزئية جسماني، وهو المطلوب؛ لأن المراد بالمبدأ المحرك القريب.
The second art on celestial bodies. A section on that the proximate mover [is corporeal]. ... we speak of two forms of one type, and there is no way to the second, because the form differing by smallness and bigness should not be taken from outside, so the third division is determined, so the larger of the two is imprinted in what the smaller is not imprinted in, so the perceiver divides necessarily in position, and whatever has this property is corporeal, and that is what is desired. - Because if they were imprinted in an abstract essence, it would follow that an abstract essence has position and magnitude, so it would not remain abstract, and this is a contradiction, so it must be that they are imprinted in a corporeal power, so it is established that whatever has a particular perception is corporeal, and that is what is desired. Corporeal, so let it be concluded that the principle of particular motions is corporeal, and that is what is desired; because what is meant by the principle is the proximate mover.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a syllogistic proof that the proximate mover (المحرك القريب) of particular motions must be corporeal (جسماني). The author begins by considering two types of forms—presumably a larger and a smaller form—and argues that the larger form cannot be imprinted in the same substrate as the smaller because the difference in size requires a different substrate. Therefore, the perceiver of such forms must be divisible in position, which is a characteristic of bodies. The author then presents a second argument: if the forms were imprinted in an abstract essence (ذات محردة), that essence would acquire position and magnitude, contradicting its abstraction. Hence, they must be imprinted in a corporeal power (قوة جسمانية). From this it follows that whatever has a particular perception (تصور جزئي) is corporeal. Finally, since the principle of particular motions (مبدأ التحريكات الجزئية) has such perception, it too is corporeal. This is a classic demonstration in natural philosophy establishing the corporeal nature of the proximate mover, presumably the celestial intellect or soul that moves the spheres.

Conditional Proof of Specific Form Difference in Elements
Proof that elements differ in specific form via conditional argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the conditional proof that the four elements differ in specific form based on their natural places.

Core Idea
Specific form determines natural place; different natural places prove different specific forms.

This passage from Hidayat al-Hikma presents a logical proof for a key claim in natural philosophy: the four elements (water, earth, fire, air) differ in their specific forms (sura naw'iyya). The proof uses a conditional argument (qiyas istithna'i muttasil) that links specific form to natural place. Understanding this argument helps you see how early Islamic thinkers used logic to ground physical theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author asserts that each element differs from the others in specific form. The difference is specific (naw'i) not generic or individual.
2
Extract the conditional premise
If the specific forms were not different, then each element would naturally occupy the place of the other. This is the 'if' part (antecedent) leading to a consequence (consequent).
3
Identify the exception (istithna')
The consequent is false: fire and air naturally rise, while water and earth naturally fall. They do not occupy each other's places; their natural motions are opposite.
This is a modus tollens: deny the consequent.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the consequent is false, the antecedent must also be false. Therefore, the specific forms are different. The author concludes: 'the requirement for place is the specific form, so the specific forms are different.'
5
Reflect on the middle term
The requirement for place (muqtada li-l-hayyiz) is identified as the specific form. This links the natural place to the essence of each element.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الماء والأرض والنار والهواء كل واحد منها يخالف الآخر في صورته. أشار بهذا الكلام إلى أن الاختلاف بينهما نوعي لا صنفي ولا شخصي؛ لاختلاف صورها النوعية، وإن لم تكن صورها النوعية مختلفة لكان كل واحد منها شاغلا لمكان الآخر بالطبع، والتالي باطل؛ ضرورة أن النار والهواء طالبان بالطبع لجهة الفوق هاربان بالطبع عن جهة التحت والماء والأرض طالبان للسفل هاربان عن العلو، ولما كان حيز كل واحد منهما مختلفا بالطبع علم أن المقتضي للحيز أيضا مختلف المقتضي لحيز الصورة النوعية فالصورة النوعية مختلفة.
Water, earth, fire, and air—each one of them differs from the other in its form. He indicated by this statement that the difference between them is specific (nawʿī) not generic or individual; because of the difference of their specific forms. And if their specific forms were not different, then each one of them would naturally occupy the place of the other; but the consequent is false, necessarily because fire and air naturally seek the upward direction and flee from the downward direction, while water and earth seek the downward direction and flee from the upward direction. And since the place of each one of them is naturally different, it is known that the requirement for place is also different, and the requirement for place is the specific form; so the specific forms are different.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage provides a logical proof that the four elements differ in specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya). The author first states the claim: each element differs from the others in its form. He then explains that this difference is at the level of species (nawʿī), not genus (ṣinfī) or individual (shakhṣī). The proof is a conditional argument: if the elements did not differ in specific form, then each would naturally occupy the place of the other. This consequent is false because fire and air naturally rise, while water and earth naturally fall. Their natural places are different, which indicates that their specific forms—which determine their natural tendencies—are also different. This is a standard argument in Islamic natural philosophy.

The Falling Stone Argument Against the Earth's Rotation
Author refutes the rotation of the elements using a conditional argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional syllogism (modus tollens) used in the refutation of the earth's rotation and evaluate its logical validity.

Core Idea
A conditional argument can refute a false claim by showing its consequence contradicts observation.

This passage from Hidayat al-Hikma presents a classic argument against the rotation of the earth. The author claims that if the earth rotated eastward, a falling stone would not land directly below its release point but to the west. Since we observe stones falling straight down, the rotation claim must be false. Let's unpack the logical structure of this argument and see how modus tollens works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Find the position that the author is attacking. The text says: 'And some people claimed that they move in rotation to the east, and the daily motion is attributed to them.' So the claim is that the elements (specifically earth) rotate daily eastward.
The 'elements' here include earth, water, air, fire. The daily motion is the apparent rotation of the heavens.
2
Extract the conditional statement
The author sets up a conditional: 'if it were so, then a falling stone would not land on the spot... but to the west.' Formally: If the earth rotates, then a falling stone would land west of its release point.
This is the antecedent (earth rotates) and consequent (stone lands west).
3
State the observed fact
The author relies on direct observation: 'this is evidently false' referring to the idea that the stone lands west. In reality, stones fall directly below. So the consequent is false.
The falsity of the consequent is taken as obvious (badihiyya).
4
Apply modus tollens
The logical form is: If P then Q. Not Q. Therefore not P. Here P = earth rotates, Q = stone lands west. Since Q is false (stone lands directly below), P must be false. The author concludes the rotation claim is false.
This is a valid form of conditional syllogism.
5
Evaluate the argument's assumptions
The argument assumes that the stone would not be carried along with the earth's motion (e.g., by air or by inertia). In medieval physics, this was plausible. Modern physics shows that the atmosphere and the stone share the earth's motion, so the argument fails.
Understanding the assumptions helps us see why the argument was accepted in its time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم قوم أنها متحركة بالاستدارة إلى المشرق، والحركة اليومية منسوبة إليها ويبطله أنه لو كان كذلك لما وقع الحجر الهابط على الموضع الذي حاذاه من الأرض حين هبوطه؛ لأن ذلك الموضع قد تحرك نحو المشرق بتبعية الأرض فراسخ، بل يقع في المغرب من ذلك الموضع، وهذا باطل بالبداهة.
And some people claimed that they move in rotation to the east, and the daily motion is attributed to them. He refutes it: if it were so, then a falling stone would not land on the spot on earth that was directly below it at the time of its fall, because that spot would have moved eastwards by following the earth for several leagues; rather, it would land to the west of that spot, and this is evidently false.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the view that the elements, specifically the earth, rotate daily from west to east. The author argues by conditional proof: if the earth rotated, then a stone dropped from a height would not land directly below the release point because the earth would have moved eastward during the fall. Instead, it would land to the west. But experience shows that stones fall directly below, so the rotation claim is false. This is a classic argument against the earth's rotation, using the example of a falling stone.

Key Terms
الحركة اليومية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It would land to the west
C) It would be caught by the air
D) It would not fall at all

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism of the form 'If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P' (modus tollens) is a valid argument used to refute claims when the observed consequence contradicts the claim.

Proof of the Soul's Temporal Origination via Conditional Syllogism
Author argues that the soul must be temporally originated because it depends on the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional syllogism proving the soul's temporal origination and identify its antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
The soul's dependence on the body proves its createdness.

This passage from Hidayat al-Hikma presents a key argument in Islamic philosophy: the soul cannot be eternal because its existence depends on the body. The author uses a conditional syllogism to demonstrate that the soul must be temporally originated. Let's unpack the logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main conclusion
The author concludes that the soul is temporally originated (haditha). Locate this claim in the Arabic: 'فتكون حادثة ضرورة'.
2
Find the conditional premise
The argument rests on a conditional: 'If bodies do not exist, then souls do not exist' (implied by 'فمتى لم تكن الأبدان موجودة لم تكن النفوس موجودة'). Identify the antecedent (bodies do not exist) and consequent (souls do not exist).
3
Identify the exception (istithna')
The conditional is applied by affirming the antecedent: bodies are indeed contingent (temporally originated). This is not explicitly stated but is a given in Islamic theology. The argument assumes the body is temporally originated, leading to the conclusion.
In a conditional syllogism, affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) yields the consequent.
4
Reconstruct the full syllogism
Write out the syllogism: Premise 1: If the body is temporally originated, then the soul is temporally originated (because it depends on the body). Premise 2: The body is temporally originated. Conclusion: Therefore, the soul is temporally originated. Confirm the validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا جائز أن يكون بالعوارض المفارقة؛ لأن العوارض المفارقة إنما تلحق الشيء بسبب القوابل؛ لأن الماهية لا تستحق العوارض لذاتها، وإلا لكان العارض لازما، والقابل للنفس إنما هو البدن، فمتى لم تكن الأبدان موجودة لم تكن النفوس موجودة، فتكون حادثة ضرورة. للنفس: أي للعوارض المفارقة التي بسببها تختلف تشخصاتها.
It is not permissible that [the soul] be [defined] by separable accidents; because separable accidents only attach to a thing due to subjects; because the quiddity does not merit accidents by itself, otherwise the accident would be necessary. The subject for the soul is only the body. So when bodies do not exist, souls do not exist, thus they are necessarily temporally originated. For the soul: meaning the separable accidents by which its individuations differ.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the soul is temporally originated (haditha). The author begins by denying that the soul can be defined by separable accidents (al-a'rad al-mufariqa). The reason is that such accidents only attach to a subject (qabil) because the quiddity (mahiyya) does not deserve accidents on its own—otherwise the accident would be inseparable. For the soul, the subject is the body. The argument proceeds: if bodies are not present, souls are not present. Therefore, souls are necessarily created, not eternal. The last part glosses that the separable accidents are what cause individual differences. The logic is a conditional syllogism: if the body does not exist, then the soul does not exist; but bodies are contingent; thus souls are contingent. This aligns with the Islamic theological position that the soul is created and not pre-eternal.

The Syllogism

The Proof that a Simple Agent Produces One Effect and Its Refutation
Author presents and refines the proof that a simple cause cannot produce more than one effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism underlying the proof that a simple agent produces only one effect, and evaluate the critique based on mental considerations.

Core Idea
Conceptual distinctness does not entail real composition—the proof collapses if we mistake mental relations for extra-mental distinctions.

This passage presents a well-known philosophical argument in Islamic metaphysics: a simple agent cannot produce more than one effect. The argument proceeds by syllogistic reasoning, establishing that any agent producing two effects must be composite. However, a powerful objection, attributed to al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani, challenges the move from conceptual to real composition. In this lesson, you will unpack the syllogism and assess the force of the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Claim
Determine what the author is trying to prove. The claim is that a simple agent (fa'il basit) cannot produce more than one effect. Note that the text appears to say 'cannot produce a single effect' but the argument is about multiple effects; the correct claim is about more than one.
The first sentence may be garbled, but the rest of the passage clarifies the intended thesis.
2
Extract the Syllogism
Find the categorical syllogism underlying the proof. The major premise: 'Every agent that produces two effects is composite.' The minor premise: 'No simple agent is composite' (by definition). Conclusion: 'No simple agent produces two effects.' This is a valid second-figure syllogism (Camestres). Identify the middle term: 'composite'.
The syllogism is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming a simple agent produces two effects leads to a contradiction.
3
Analyze the Justification of the Major Premise
Why does the author claim that any agent producing two effects is composite? Because there would be two distinct concepts (e.g., 'source of effect A' and 'source of effect B'). If these concepts are part of the agent's essence, it is composed; if external, the agent must be their source, leading to infinite regress or composition. Thus, the agent is composite.
This step highlights the move from conceptual distinctness to real composition.
4
Identify the Objection
The objection states that the two concepts are mere mental considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyya), not extra-mental realities. In external reality, there is only the cause and the effect; relations like 'source-ness' are posterior to the intellect and have no independent existence. Therefore, conceptual distinctness does not imply real composition.
This objection is attributed to al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani and is a key refinement in the discussion.
5
Evaluate the Force of the Objection
Assess whether the objection invalidates the syllogism. The syllogism itself is formally valid, but its soundness depends on the truth of the major premise. The objection undermines the justification of that premise by showing that conceptual distinctions do not necessitate real composition. Thus, the argument fails unless the opponent can show that the concepts are real.
This evaluation shows the importance of distinguishing between mental and extra-mental distinctions in Islamic metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then the efficient cause, its conclusion is that the simple agent cannot produce a single effect? [rather: cannot produce more than one], because every agent from which two effects proceed is composite, so if from a simple agent two effects proceeded, the simple would be composite, which is absurd. They argued for their statement: that it cannot produce more than one [effect], because that from which two effects proceed is composite; that the thing's being such that this effect proceeds from it is other than its being such that that effect proceeds from it. So the sum of these two concepts, or one of them, if it is included in the essence of the source, then composition in its essence is necessary; and if they are external, then it is a source for them, so its being a source for this is other than its being a source for that, thus it ends inevitably in what necessitates composition and multiplicity in the essence. And we also say that the effect must exist when its complete cause exists, namely: "That from which two effects proceed is composite" — because the thing's being such that this effect proceeds from it is different from its being such that that effect proceeds from it, because each can be conceived without the other. And when it is established in the agent from which two effects proceed that there are two distinct concepts, then the sum of these two concepts or one of them — if it is included in its essence, composition in its essence is necessary; and if they are external, then that agent is a source for them, because if they were dependent on something else, that agent would not be the source of the two effects, since the two effects would be dependent on those two concepts, and the supposition is the contrary. Then we transfer the discussion to the two external concepts: either the chain goes to infinity or it ends at the origin of multiplicity in the essence, thus composition is necessary. So it is established that that from which two effects proceed is composite. 'And if they are etc.' It was objected against this that these two concepts are mental considerations, they have no reality in the external so that a cause and source be sought for them, requiring considering multiplicity in their source, leading to composition and multiplicity in the essence. Sayyid said: For the intellect, when it observes a thing in relation to its effect, apprehends for them two relations, namely source-ness and effect-ness. They are relational matters that accrue to them in the intellect, posterior to them in intellection, and have no existence in the external at all. For in the external there is only the essence of the source, i.e., the cause, and the essence of the effect, i.e., the effect, and the fact that the first is a source and the second an effect is not among the things realized in the external.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical proof that a simple (non-composite) agent cannot produce more than one effect. It begins by stating the conclusion: a simple agent cannot produce a single effect? Actually, the text seems garbled: it says 'cannot produce a single effect' but the argument is about more than one. The correct claim is that a simple agent cannot produce more than one effect. The proof is that any agent producing two effects is composite, so if a simple agent produced two effects, it would be both simple and composite—a contradiction. The detailed argument examines the concepts corresponding to each effect. If an agent produces two effects, there are two distinct concepts (e.g., 'source of this effect' and 'source of that effect'). If these concepts are part of the agent's essence, the essence is composed. If they are external to the essence, the agent must be the source of these concepts themselves, leading to an infinite regress or ending in multiplicity in the essence, again implying composition. Thus, anything producing two effects is composite. The passage then introduces a powerful objection: these concepts are merely mental considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyya) and have no external reality. The distinction between 'source-ness' and 'effect-ness' is only a relation in the mind, not extra-mental. In reality, there is only the cause and the effect as individuals. Therefore, the argument's move from conceptual distinctness to real composition is invalid. This objection, attributed to 'al-Sayyid' (likely al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani), refines the understanding of causality and the limits of logical analysis.

The Compound Reductio Proof of the Necessary Existent
Compound reductio proof of the Necessary Existent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the compound reductio argument for the Necessary Existent from contingency and identify each step's logical role.

Core Idea
A compound reductio: if no Necessary Being, all existents are contingent, needing an external cause that turns out to be Necessary—contradiction.

This passage from Hidayat al-Hikma presents a cornerstone proof in Islamic philosophy: the proof from contingency. The author builds a multi-step argument that reduces the denial of the Necessary Existent to an absurdity. We will unpack each logical layer, from the initial assumption to the final contradiction, showing how Islamic thinkers used formal reasoning to establish foundational theological truths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reductio assumption
The proof begins by assuming that there is no Necessary Existent in existence. This is the hypothesis to be refuted.
The author states: 'إن لم يكن في عالم الوجود موجود واجب لذاته'
2
Derive that all existents are contingent
Given no Necessary, every existent must be possible (contingent) in itself, since an impossible being cannot exist. The aggregate of existents is thus composed solely of contingents.
The author rules out impossibility and necessity, leaving only contingency.
3
Apply the causal principle to each contingent
Each contingent requires an efficient cause for its existence, because without a preponderant cause, its equal relation to existence and non-existence would remain unresolved.
This is presented as self-evident: knowledge of contingency entails knowledge of need for a cause.
4
Show the cause cannot be within the chain
The cause cannot be the aggregate itself or any part of it, because the cause must be prior to its effect, and a thing cannot be prior to itself.
The author argues: 'لا تكون عين تلك الجملة ولا جزءها؛ لامتناع تقدم الشيء على نفسه'
5
Conclude the cause is Necessary and produces contradiction
Thus the cause must be external to all contingents and existent. What is existent and not contingent is necessary. This establishes the Necessary Existent on the assumption of its non-existence, which is a contradiction (both existing and not existing). Hence the assumption is false.
The contradiction proves that the Necessary Existent necessarily exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني في العلم بالصنائع وصفاته
فصل في إثبات الواجب لذاته
وهو الذي إذا اعتبر من حيث هو هو لا يكون قابلا للعدم وبرهانه
من حيث إلخ قيده بالحيثية؛ لأن الممكن أيضا بالنظر إلى علته لا يكون قابلا للعدم فكونه واجبا بالنظر إليها.
برهانه خلاصته أنه إن لم يكن في عالم الوجود موجود واجب لذاته لزم المحال منه، وكل أمر يلزم منه المحال فهو باطل، فعدم وجود الواجب باطل، فوجود الواجب حق؛ ضرورة أن بطلان أحد النقيضين يستلزم حقية الآخر، وبيان الملازمة أن الموجودات حينئذ تكون جملة مركبة من آحاد كل واحد منها ممكن لذاته؛ لأن الموجود لا يكون ممتنعا لذاته، وقد فرض عدم الواجب، فلا احتمال سوى الإمكان، وكل ممكن محتاج في وجوده إلى علة موجدة؛ لامتناع الترجيح بلا مرجح، والعلم باحتياج الممكن إلى العلة بديهي لا حاجة إلى الاستدلال؛ لأن من علم معنى الإمكان - وهو استواء النسبة إلى العدم والوجود - علم بالضرورة احتياجه إلى العلة، وتلك العلة الموجدة لا تكون عين تلك الجملة المركبة من الممكنات ولا جزءها؛ لامتناع تقدم الشيء على نفسه والعلة متقدمة على معلولها فالعلة الموجدة لجميع الممكنات الخارجة عن سلسلة الممكنات تكون واجبة لذاتها؛ لأنها تكون موجودة؛ ضرورة أن المعدوم لا يكون جاعلا لشيء؛ لامتناع وقوع التأثير من معدوم، وقد ثبت أن تلك العلة الموجدة خارجة عن الممكنات فالموجود الخارج عن الممكنات واجب لذاته، فيلزم ثبوت وجود الواجب لذاته على تقدير عدمه، وهو محال؛ لكونه اجتماع النقيضين، أعني الوجود على تقدير عدمه، وهذا المحال إنما لزم من عدمه، فعدمه محال، فوجوده واجب، وهو المطلوب.
The Second Art: On Knowledge of the Maker and His Attributes. Chapter on Proving the Necessary Existent in Itself. It is that which, when considered in itself, is not receptive to non-existence. Its proof, from the aspect etc., he restricted it by the aspect because the contingent also, with respect to its cause, is not receptive to non-existence, so it is necessary with respect to it. The summary of its proof: If there were not in the world of existence an existent necessary in itself, then an absurdity would follow from that. Everything from which an absurdity follows is false. Therefore the non-existence of the Necessary is false, so the existence of the Necessary is true; necessarily, because the falsity of one of the two contradictories entails the truth of the other. The explanation of the entailment is that the existents then would be a composite aggregate, each one of which is contingent in itself, because an existent is not impossible in itself, and the non-existence of the Necessary is assumed, so there is no possibility except contingency. Every contingent is in need, for its existence, of an efficient cause, because preference without a preponderant is impossible. The knowledge of the contingent's need for a cause is self-evident, requiring no proof, because whoever knows the meaning of contingency—which is equal relation to non-existence and existence—knows necessarily its need for a cause. That efficient cause cannot be that same aggregate composed of contingents, nor a part of it, because a thing's preceding itself is impossible, and the cause is prior to its effect. So the efficient cause of all contingents, being outside the chain of contingents, is necessary in itself, because it is existent; necessarily, because non-existence cannot be a maker of anything, since the occurrence of influence from non-existence is impossible. And it has been established that that efficient cause is outside the contingents, so the existent outside the contingents is necessary in itself. Thus the existence of the necessary in itself would be established on the assumption of its non-existence, which is absurd because it is a conjunction of contradictories, i.e., existence on the assumption of its non-existence. This absurdity follows only from its non-existence, so its non-existence is absurd, so its existence is necessary. That is what was sought.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents a classical proof for the existence of the Necessary Existent (al-wajib li-dhatihi), a central concept in Islamic theology and philosophy. The author begins by defining the Necessary Existent: that which, considered in itself, is not receptive to non-existence. He then notes a nuance: a contingent being, with respect to its cause, is also not receptive to non-existence, but that is a conditional necessity, not absolute. The proof itself is a reductio ad absurdum. It assumes that there is no Necessary Existent in existence. Then all existents would be contingent, because an existent cannot be impossible. Each contingent needs a cause for its existence, as the concept of contingency (equal possibility of existence and non-existence) intuitively requires an external preponderant. This cause cannot be any of the contingents, nor the whole aggregate of contingents, because that would entail circularity or self-precedence. Therefore, the cause must be outside the chain of contingents, and since it is existent and outside the contingent realm, it is necessary in itself. The proof concludes that the assumption of no Necessary Existent leads to the absurdity that the Necessary Existent would both exist (as the cause) and not exist (by assumption), which is a contradiction. Hence the assumption is false, and the Necessary Existent must exist. The argument uses the principle of non-contradiction and the impossibility of an infinite regress of causes. It is a compound syllogism involving several steps: if no Necessary, then all existents are contingent; each contingent needs a cause; that cause cannot be among them; thus it is necessary; therefore the Necessary exists on the assumption of its non-existence, contradiction. The sub-arguments are each immediate or categorical in nature.

Key Terms

الممكن
العلة الموجدة
العدم

C) Because the aggregate is composed of parts that are also contingent.
D) Because the cause must be simple.

Key Takeaway
The proof of the Necessary Existent by contingency is a compound reductio: assuming no Necessary leads to an external cause that is Necessary, producing a contradiction and thus demonstrating the necessity of the Necessary Existent.

Proving the Necessary Existent by Conditional Argument
Proof of the Necessary Existent via conditional syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof of the Necessary Existent as a conditional syllogism (modus tollens) and identify the self-evident premise that every possible needs a cause.

Core Idea
If no necessary being exists, an absurdity follows—so its non-existence is impossible.

This passage presents one of the most famous arguments in Islamic philosophy: the proof of the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud). The author uses a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) to show that the assumption of no necessary being leads to a contradiction. By mastering this argument, you will see how logical form structures metaphysical proofs in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise (if...then)
Find the sentence: 'If in the world of existence there were no existent necessary in itself, then an absurdity would follow.' This is the major premise of the conditional syllogism. Mark it clearly.
The conditional is implicit in the phrasing 'it is necessary that...' but the logical form is 'If non-existence of Wajib, then absurdity'.
2
Locate the minor premise (denying the consequent)
The author states: 'Every affair from which an absurdity follows is false.' This asserts that the consequent (absurdity) is impossible. So the minor premise is: 'The absurdity does not occur' or 'The consequent is false'.
This is the modus tollens pattern: If P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
3
Determine the conclusion
Since the consequent is denied, the antecedent must be false. Therefore, 'It is not the case that no necessary being exists', i.e., 'A necessary being exists'. The author concludes: 'So its existence is necessary.'
The conclusion is explicitly stated at the end.
4
Analyze the sub-argument for the entailment
Why does assuming no necessary being lead to absurdity? The author argues: all existents would be possible, each possible needs a cause, causes cannot regress infinitely, so there must be a cause outside the chain which is necessary—but that would be a necessary being existing even on the assumption of its non-existence, a contradiction. This establishes the conditional premise.
The sub-argument itself uses a chain of reasoning but the overall structure is still conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو الذي إذا اعتبر من حيث هو هو لا يكون قابلا للعدم وبرهانه من حيث إلخ قيده بالحيثية؛ لأن الممكن أيضا بالنظر إلى علته لا يكون قابلا للعدم فكونه واجبا بالنظر إليها. برهانه خلاصته أنه إن لم يكن في عالم الوجود موجود واجب لذاته لزم المحال منه، وكل أمر يلزم منه المحال فهو باطل، فعدم وجود الواجب باطل، فوجود الواجب حق؛ ضرورة أن بطلان أحد النقيضين يستلزم حقية الآخر، وبيان الملازمة أن الموجودات حينئذ تكون جملة مركبة من آحاد كل واحد منها ممكن لذاته؛ لأن الموجود لا يكون ممتنعا لذاته، وقد فرض عدم الواجب، فلا احتمال سوى الإمكان، وكل ممكن محتاج في وجوده إلى علة موجدة؛ لامتناع الترجيح بلا مرجح، والعلم باحتياج الممكن إلى العلة بديهي لا حاجة إلى الاستدلال؛ لأن من علم معنى الإمكان - وهو استواء النسبة إلى العدم والوجود - علم بالضرورة احتياجه إلى العلة، وتلك العلة الموجدة لا تكون عين تلك الجملة المركبة من الممكنات ولا جزءها؛ لامتناع تقدم الشيء على نفسه والعلة متقدمة على معلولها فالعلة الموجدة لجميع الممكنات الخارجة عن سلسلة الممكنات تكون واجبة لذاتها؛ لأنها تكون موجودة؛ ضرورة أن المعدوم لا يكون جاعلا لشيء؛ لامتناع وقوع التأثير من معدوم، وقد ثبت أن تلك العلة الموجدة خارجة عن الممكنات فالموجود الخارج عن الممكنات واجب لذاته، فيلزم ثبوت وجود الواجب لذاته على تقدير عدمه، وهو محال؛ لكونه اجتماع النقيضين، أعني الوجود على تقدير عدمه، وهذا المحال إنما لزم من عدمه، فعدمه محال، فوجوده واجب، وهو المطلوب. الفصل الثاني في العلم بالصنائع وصفاته 45 فصل في إثبات الواجب لذاته أن نقول: إن لم يكن في الوجود موجود واجب لذاته يلزم منه المحال؛ لأن الموجودات بأسرها حينئذ تكون جملة مركبة من آحاد كل واحد منها ممكن لذاته، فتحتاج إلى علة خارجية، والعلم به بديهي، والموجود الخارج عن جميع الممكنات واجب لذاته فيلزم وجود واجب الوجود على تقدير عدمه، وهو محال، فوجوده واجب.
It is that which, when considered in itself, is not receptive of non-existence. Its proof: Briefly, if in the world of existence there were no existent necessary in itself, then an absurdity would follow. And every affair from which an absurdity follows is false. Therefore, the non-existence of the Necessary is false, so the existence of the Necessary is true – necessarily, because the falsehood of one of the two contradictories entails the truth of the other. The explanation of the entailment is that existents then would be a composite aggregate of individuals each of which is possible in itself (since an existent cannot be impossible in itself, and the non-existence of the Necessary has been assumed, so no possibility remains except possibility). And every possible is in need of an efficient cause for its existence, because preponderance without a preponderator is impossible. The knowledge of the possible's need for a cause is self-evident, requiring no proof; for whoever knows the meaning of possibility – equality of relation to non-existence and existence – knows necessarily its need for a cause. That efficient cause cannot be identical to that aggregate of possibles nor a part of it, because a thing's priority over itself is impossible, and the cause is prior to its effect. Thus the efficient cause of all possibles, external to the chain of possibles, is necessary in itself because it exists (since non-existence cannot make anything, as effect from non-existent is impossible). And it has been established that that efficient cause is external to possibles, so the existent external to possibles is necessary in itself. Hence the existence of the Necessary in itself would follow on the assumption of its non-existence, which is absurd – being the conjunction of contradictories, i.e., existence on the assumption of non-existence. This absurdity follows only from its non-existence, so its non-existence is absurd, thus its existence is necessary, which is what was sought. The second art on knowledge of the Maker and His attributes – Chapter on proving the Necessary in itself: Let us say: If in existence there were no existent necessary in itself, an absurdity would follow; because then all existents as a whole would be a composite aggregate of individuals each of which is possible in itself, so they would need an external cause, and knowledge of this is self-evident. The existent external to all possibles is necessary in itself. Hence the existence of the Necessary of existence would follow on the assumption of its non-existence, which is absurd, so its existence is necessary.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) for the existence of a necessary being, fundamental in Islamic philosophy. The author first defines the Necessary as that which is not receptive of non-existence in itself. He then presents a proof by contradiction: assume no necessary being exists. Then all existents are possible, each needing a cause. This leads to an infinite regress or a cause outside the chain, which must be necessary. If that cause were not necessary, it would be possible and need another cause, ad infinitum. But a cause external to all possibles must be necessary. Then he shows that if we assume no necessary being, the existence of a necessary being would be entailed, which is absurd (contradiction). Therefore, the non-existence of the necessary is absurd, so its existence is necessary. The proof hinges on the self-evident principle that every possible needs a cause (preponderator). The author emphasizes that this is a priori, not empirical. For example, in Islamic theology, this is similar to the kalam cosmological argument but uses the concept of possibility rather than temporal origination. The summary at the end restates the core argument concisely.

B) Modus tollens: If P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
C) Disjunctive syllogism: Either P or Q, not P, therefore Q.
D) Categorical syllogism: All A are B, all B are C, therefore all A are C.

Key Takeaway
The proof of the Necessary Existent is a conditional syllogism (modus tollens) where the impossibility of absurdity forces the existence of a necessary being.

Proof of the Necessary Being's Self-Sufficiency
Author argues that the Necessary Being's essence suffices for all its attributes, with a proof by contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof by contradiction that the Necessary Being has no attributes dependent on external causes.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being's essence suffices for all its attributes; any dependency would contradict its necessity.

This passage presents a classic metaphysical proof that the Necessary Being (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi) cannot have any attributes caused by something external. Using a conditional syllogism and proof by contradiction (khalaf), the author demonstrates that all attributes of the Necessary Being must be essential and non-contingent. By following the logical structure, you will see why the Necessary Being is necessary from all aspects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the conditional antecedent
The author claims the Necessary Being has no pending state (hala muntazara). The conditional assumption is: if an attribute depended on another being (ghayr), then that other would be its cause.
The antecedent states that the existence of another is the cause of the attribute's existence and its absence is the cause of the attribute's absence.
2
Distinguish the two subcases
The essence of the Necessary Being must have existence either with the attribute present or with it absent. These two cases are mutually exclusive.
Case 1: existence with attribute present. Case 2: existence with attribute absent.
3
Derive contradictions in each subcase
In case 1, if the attribute exists with the essence, then its existence is not from the other (since the essence alone suffices), contradicting the assumption. In case 2, if the attribute is absent with the essence, then its absence is not from the other's absence, again contradicting the assumption.
Both subcases lead to khalaf (contradiction).
4
Conclude the antecedent is false
Since the conditional assumption leads to contradiction in every possibility, it must be false. Thus, no attribute of the Necessary Being depends on an external cause.
Therefore, the Necessary Being is necessary from all aspects, with all attributes inherent in its essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته واجب من جميع جهاته أي ليس له حالة منتظرة؛ لأن ذاته كافية فيما له من الصفات. ولو كان أي لو كان وجود الغير علة لوجود تلك الصفة، وعدمه علة لعدمها، لم يكن أن يجب لذات الواجب الوجود إذا اعتبرت من حيث هي هي بلا لحاظ الغير، والتالي باطل فالمقدم مثله. وبيان الملازمة أن ذات الواجب إما أن يجب لها الوجود مع وجود تلك الصفة أو مع عدمها، فإن كان لها الوجوب مع وجود تلك الصفة لم يكن وجود تلك الصفة من الغير؛ لأنا اعتبرنا ذات الواجب من حيث هي هي بدون الغير، وقد وجبت تلك الصفة في تلك المرتبة بدون ذلك الغير، فلم يكن ذلك الغير علة لها، وقد فرضناه علة لها هذا خلف. وإن كان لها الوجوب مع عدمها لم يكن عدمها من عدم ذلك الغير؛ لحصول عدم تلك الصفة بذات الواجب بدون اعتبار عدم الغير، فلم يكن عدمه علة لعدمها هذا خلف. وإذا لم يجب وجود ذات الواجب بلا شرط لم يكن الواجب واجبا لذاته هذا خلف.
Chapter that the Necessary Being in itself is necessary from all its aspects, i.e., it has no pending state; because its essence is sufficient for the attributes it has. And if it were—i.e., if the existence of another were the cause of the existence of that attribute, and its absence the cause of its absence—then it would not be necessary that existence belong to the essence of the Necessary Being when considered in itself without regard to the other. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. The explanation of the entailment is that the essence of the Necessary Being either has existence with the existence of that attribute or with its absence. If it has existence with the existence of that attribute, then the existence of that attribute is not from the other; for we have considered the essence of the Necessary Being in itself without the other, and that attribute obtains at that level without that other, so that other is not its cause—yet we assumed it was its cause; this is a contradiction. And if it has existence with its absence, then its absence is not from the absence of that other; because the absence of that attribute occurs from the essence of the Necessary Being without considering the absence of the other, so the absence of the other is not the cause of its absence; this is a contradiction. And if the existence of the essence of the Necessary Being is not necessary without condition, then the Necessary Being is not necessary in itself; this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a definitional proof that the Necessary Being (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi) is necessary in all aspects, meaning it has no attributes that depend on anything external. The author begins by stating the claim: the essence of the Necessary Being is sufficient for all its attributes. Then he gives a conditional argument: if any attribute of the Necessary Being depended on another being (ghayr) for its existence, then that attribute would be caused by that other. In that case, the essence of the Necessary Being, considered in itself without regard to that other, would not necessarily have existence—because existence would require either the presence or absence of that other. If existence accompanies the attribute when the other is present, then the attribute's existence is not from the other (contradicting the assumption). If existence accompanies the attribute when the other is absent, then the attribute's absence is not from the other's absence (again contradiction). Therefore, the antecedent (that the attribute depends on another) is false. Hence the Necessary Being has no external dependency for any attribute, so it is necessary from all aspects. This argument uses conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) with a proof by contradiction (khalaf).

The Trilemma Proof: Existence Not Shared Between God and Contingents
Author proves through a trilemma that existence is not shared between God and contingent beings, with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and reconstruct the trilemma argument used to prove that existence is not univocally shared between the Necessary Being and contingent beings.

Core Idea
If existence were common, absolute existence forces an absurdity on every branch.

The author presents a rigorous logical proof (burhan) that the Necessary Being (God) does not share existence with contingent beings. The argument is structured as a trilemma (taqsim) that systematically eliminates the possibility of shared existence. This proof is foundational in Islamic metaphysics, distinguishing God's existence as unique and non-accidental. The passage also includes an objection and a response, showing the dialectical depth of the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim being proved
First, locate the thesis: the Necessary Being does not share existence (wujud) with contingent beings. Note the distinction between real original existence (which produces effects) and the verbal noun (which is a general concept). The proof focuses on the former.
The author clarifies that 'existence' here is the real, effect-producing existence, not the general concept that applies to everything.
2
Extract the trilemma (taqsim) structure
The proof assumes for reductio that existence is shared. Then it considers absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq) in itself. It must either (a) require disengagement (tajarud: non-accidentality to essence), (b) require non-disengagement (accidentality), or (c) require neither. These three branches form the trilemma.
The three options are mutually exclusive and exhaustive conditions for the nature of existence if it were common.
3
Evaluate each branch for absurdity
Branch (a): If existence requires disengagement, then contingent existence would also be non-accidental, but we know contingent existence is accidental (e.g., we can conceive a heptagon and doubt its existence). Branch (b): If existence requires non-disengagement, then God's existence would be accidental, but it is identical to His essence. Branch (c): If neither, then both disengagement and non-disengagement are possible, which would require a cause, making God dependent—contradicting His necessity.
Each branch leads to a contradiction (batil), showing the initial assumption of shared existence is false.
4
Analyze the objection and response
An objector argues that the doubt about the heptagon is only about existence relative to the mind, not about the essence itself, so no real contradiction. The author responds: a thing's subsistence for itself (thubut al-shay' li nafsihi) is necessary (daruri); if existence were identical to essence, the existence would be necessarily known, not doubtful. Thus the objection fails.
This exchange shows how logical precision refutes a potential counterargument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته لا يشاركه الممكنات في وجوده لأنه لو كان مشاركا للممكنات في وجوده، فالوجود المطلق من حيث لا يشركه إلخ بأن يكون الوجود حقيقة واحدة نوعية يكون فرد منها عين الواجب و آخر منها عارضا للممكن. وجوده المراد بهذا الوجود الوجود الحقيقي الأصلي الذي يترتب عليه الآثار لا المصدري؛ فإنه من الأمور العامة عارض للموجودات كلها ومشترك بينهما، والدليل على أن الوجود ليس مشتركا بين الواجب والممكن أنه لو كان مشتركا بينهما فالوجود المطلق من حيث هو هو إما أن يقتضي التجرد أي عدم العروض للماهية أو اللاتجرد، أو لا يقتضي شيئا منهما، فإن اقتضى التجرد لزم أن يكون وجود الممكنات غير عارض لماهياتها، وهو باطل؛ لأن وجود الممكنات عارض لها ليس عينها؛ لأنا ندرك المسبع مثلا ونشك في وجوده، فلو كان وجوده عين ماهية لزم كون الشيء الواحد معلوما ومشكوكا؛ لأن ماهيته معلومة، ووجوده مشكوك وهما على تقدير كون الوجود عين الماهية ،واحدا ومن البين أن الأمر الواحد لا يكون معلوما ومشكوكا فلاح إذا أنهما يتغايران دليل على هذا البيان معلوم بالتصور، ومشكوك باعتبار النسبة فلا منافاة، وأجيب عنه: أن ثبوت الشيء لنفسه ،ضروري، فإذا كان الوجود عين الماهية كان ضروري الثبوت، فكيف يكون مشكوكا، وإن اقتضى الوجود اللاتجرد لما كان وجود الباري مجردا؛ لأن مقتضى الوجود المطلق يجري في كل فرد والتالي - أعني عدم تجرد وجود الباري - باطل؛ لأن وجوده عين ماهية ليس عارضا لها، فكذا المقدم، وإن لم يقتض شيئا منهما كان كل واحد منهما ممكنا له، والممكن معلول بعلة، فيكون تجرد وجود الباري معلولا لعلة مفتقرا إلى الغير فلا يكون ذات الباري فيما له من الصفات كافية هذا خلف؛ لما مر من قبل.
Chapter on the Necessary Being Itself does not share existence with contingent beings. Because if it shared existence with contingent beings, then absolute existence inasmuch as it is not shared etc., such that existence is one specific reality having individuals one of which is the Necessary Being itself and another accidental to the contingent. Existence meant here is real original existence which produces effects, not the verbal noun; for it is among general matters accidental to all existents and common between them. The proof that existence is not common between the Necessary and the contingent is that if it were common between them, then absolute existence in itself either requires disengagement (i.e., non-accidentality to essence) or non-disengagement, or requires neither. If it requires disengagement, then the existence of contingent beings would not be accidental to their essences, which is false because the existence of contingent beings is accidental to them, not identical to them; for we perceive a heptagon for example and doubt its existence. If its existence were identical to its essence, then one thing would be both known and doubtful because its essence is known and its existence doubtful— and if existence were identical to essence, they would be one, and it is clear that one thing cannot be both known and doubtful. So it is clear that they differ. An objection: the proof in this demonstration is known conceptually and doubtful in terms of relation, so no contradiction. It is answered: that a thing's subsistence for itself is necessary; if existence were identical to essence, it would be necessarily subsistent, so how could it be doubtful? And if existence required non-disengagement, then the existence of the Necessary Being would not be disengaged, but the consequent—I mean the non-disengagement of the Necessary Being's existence—is false because His existence is identical to His essence, not accidental to it. Thus the antecedent is false. And if it required neither, then each of the two is possible for it, and the possible is caused by a cause, so the disengagement of the Necessary Being's existence would be caused by a cause, dependent on another, so His essence would not be sufficient for His attributes—this is a contradiction, as previously stated.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) that the Necessary Being (God) does not share existence in common with contingent beings. The author argues that if absolute existence were common, it would have to be either necessarily disengaged from essence (i.e., non-accidental), necessarily accidental, or neither. Each option leads to an absurdity. If existence were necessarily non-accidental, then contingent existence would also be non-accidental, which is false because contingent existence is accidental to essence (as shown by the example of a heptagon: we conceive its essence but doubt its existence). If it were necessarily accidental, then God's existence would be accidental, which is false because His existence is identical to His essence. If it were neither, then both non-accidentality and accidentality would be possible, requiring a cause, which would make God dependent. The objection that the conceptual knowledge versus doubt is only a matter of relation is answered: if existence were identical to essence, it would be necessarily known, not doubtful. Thus, existence is not shared.
