The Trilemma of Existence
Concise trilemma proving existence is not shared

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the trilemma argument that proves the Necessary Being's existence is not shared with contingent beings, and evaluate each horn's logical impossibility.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be common to God and creation; otherwise, God's essence would be dependent or contradictory.

This passage presents a concise, memorizable version of a core Kalam proof. The author uses a trilemma—three mutually exclusive options—to show that existence (wujud) cannot be a shared attribute between the Necessary Being and possible beings. Each option leads to an absurd conclusion (khalaf), leaving only the conclusion that existence is not common. We will walk through each horn of the trilemma to understand its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three options of the trilemma
The passage states three mutually exclusive possibilities for existence with respect to the Necessary Being: either disengagement (tajarud) is necessary, or non-disengagement (la tajarud) is necessary, or neither is necessary.
2
Analyze the first horn: if disengagement is necessary
If existence is essentially disengaged from essence, then all contingent existence would also be disengaged, meaning existence is not accidental. But we can conceive a heptagon while doubting its external existence, proving existence is not the same as essence—contradiction.
3
Analyze the second horn: if non-disengagement is necessary
If existence is essentially non-disengaged (accidental), then the Necessary Being's existence would be accidental, contradicting His necessity—contradiction.
4
Analyze the third horn: if neither is necessary
If neither disengagement nor non-disengagement is necessary, then both are possible for the Necessary Being, requiring an external cause to determine which applies. This makes the Necessary Being dependent on another for His attributes—contradiction.
5
Draw the conclusion
Since all three options lead to absurdity (khalaf), the original assumption that existence is a shared attribute between God and contingent beings is false. Existence is not common.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته هو هو إما أن يجب له التجرد أو اللاتجرد أو لا يجب شيء منهما، فإن التجرد وجب أن يكون وجود الممكنات بأسرها مجردا غير عارض للماهيات، وهو محال؛ لأنا نعقل المسبع مع الشك في وجوده الخارجي، فلو كان وجوده نفس حقيقته لكان الشيء الواحد معلوما و مشكوكا في حالة واحدة، وهو محال، وإن وجب له اللاتجرد لما كان وجود الباري تعالى مجردا ، هذا خلف، وإن لم يجب له شيء منهما كان كل واحد منهما ممكنا له فيكون معلولا لعلة فيلزم افتقار واجب الوجود في تجرده إلى غيره، فلا تكون ذاته كافية فيما له من الصفات هذا خلف.
Chapter on the Necessary Being Itself: Either disengagement must belong to it, or non-disengagement, or neither. If disengagement belongs, then the existence of all contingent beings would be disengaged, not accidental to essences, which is impossible; for we conceive a heptagon while doubting its external existence, so if its existence were identical to its reality, one thing would be known and doubted in one state—impossible. If non-disengagement belongs, then the existence of the Most High Necessary Being would not be disengaged—this is a contradiction. If neither belongs, then each of them is possible for it, so it is caused by a cause, necessitating that the Necessary Being is dependent on another in its disengagement, so His essence is not sufficient for His attributes—this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a concise demonstration (burhan) that the Necessary Being does not share existence with contingent beings. It is a compressed version of the earlier argument, suitable for memorization. The author poses a trilemma: either existence (absolute) must be disengaged from essence, or non-disengaged, or neither. Each horn leads to an impossible conclusion. If existence is essentially disengaged, then contingent existence would also be disengaged, but we know contingent existence is accidental (as shown by the heptagon example: we conceive its essence but doubt its existence, so they are distinct). If existence is essentially non-disengaged, then God's existence would be accidental, contradicting His nature. If neither is necessary, then both disengagement and non-disengagement are possible, requiring an external cause, which would make God dependent. Thus, the only option left is that existence is not common at all.

C) Existence must be self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The trilemma shows that any attempt to treat existence as a common property between God and contingent beings leads to logical contradiction, thus preserving divine transcendence.

The Necessary Being Knows Itself: A Categorical Proof from Immateriality
Proof that the Necessary Being knows Himself because He is immaterial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism proving God's self-knowledge and evaluate each premise using the definition of knowledge given in the passage.

Core Idea
God's self-knowledge follows from His immateriality and the definition of knowledge as presence of the thing known.

How do Islamic philosophers prove that God knows Himself? This passage presents a crisp categorical syllogism that moves from God's immateriality to His self-knowledge. The key is the precise definition of knowledge as the attainment of the reality of a thing stripped of matter. By understanding this syllogism, you will see how foundational logical tools are used in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Extract the major premise
Find the statement that links immateriality and self-knowledge. The passage says: 'every immaterial being that perceives knows itself.' This is the general rule that God falls under.
The author clarifies that 'immaterial' here means self-subsistent, not like an intelligible form.
2
Extract the minor premise
Identify the statement about God: 'He is immaterial.' This places God in the category of the major premise.
The author argues that if God were material, He would be originated.
3
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion follows: 'Therefore God knows Himself.' This is the claim being proved.
4
Analyze the definition of knowledge
The passage defines 'ilm as 'the attainment of the reality of a thing stripped of matter and its accidents in the perceiver.' This definition is essential to understand why the conclusion holds.
Since God's essence is immaterial and present to Himself, the condition for knowledge is met.
5
Check the validity of the syllogism
Determine the figure and mood. This is a first-figure syllogism: all M are P (major), all S are M (minor), so all S are P. Here M=immaterial being that perceives, P=knows itself, S=God.
The syllogism is valid by the rules of categorical logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته عالم بذاته لأنه مجرد عن المادة، وكل مجرد عن المادة مدرك فهو عالم بذاته؛ لأن ذاته حاصلة عنده فيكون عالما بذاته؛ لأن العلم هو حصول حقيقة الشيء مجردة عن المادة ولواحقها عند المدرك، فالباري عالم بذاته. لمجود إلخ لأنه لو كان ماديا لكان حادثا؛ لأن كل ما يتعلق وجوده بها فهو حادث، ولو كان ماديا بمعنى المركب من الهيولى والصورة لكان أيضا حادثا؛ لتقدم الجزء على الكل. كل مجرد المراد به القائم بذاته؛ لئلا ينتقض بالصورة المعقولة، فإنها أنها ليست بعالمة لأن العلم حاصله أن العلم هو حصول المعلوم المجرد مجردة مع عن هي العالمة، المادة وعوارضها عند الذات المجردة القائمة بنفسها، وهذه الذات فلما كان وجودها عندها حاصلا، فتكون عالمة بنفسها؛ لتحقق مناط المعلومية. اعلم أن علم الأشياء بالعقل يسمى تعقلا، وعلم الأشياء بالحواس يسمى إحساسا.
Chapter on the Necessary Being Itself is knowing of itself. Because it is immaterial, and every immaterial thing that perceives is knowing of itself; because its essence is present to it, so it is knowing of itself. For knowledge is the attainment of the reality of a thing stripped of matter and its accidents in the perceiver. Thus the Necessary Being is knowing of itself. For if it were material, it would be originated; because everything whose existence depends on matter is originated. And if it were material in the sense of composed of hyle and form, it would also be originated because the part precedes the whole. By 'every immaterial' is meant the self-subsistent, so as not to be refuted by the intelligible form, for it is not knowing because knowledge is the attainment of the known stripped of matter and its accidents in the self-subsistent immaterial essence. And this essence, since its existence is present to it, is knowing of itself because the condition of being known is realized. Know that knowledge of things through intellect is called intellection (ta'qqul), and knowledge of things through senses is called sensation (ihsas).
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) that the Necessary Being (God) knows Himself. The argument is a categorical syllogism: God is immaterial (minor premise); every immaterial being that perceives knows itself (major premise); therefore God knows Himself. The author clarifies that immateriality means self-subsistence (qaim bi-dhatihi), not like an intelligible form which is an accident in a mind. The reason God knows Himself is that His essence is present to Him, and knowledge is precisely the presence of the reality of a thing stripped of matter. The argument also notes that if God were material, He would be originated (hadith) because matter is a condition for origination. The passage ends with a terminological note: intellectual knowledge is ta'qqul, sensory knowledge is ihsas.

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism

Proving God's Knowledge of Universals via Immateriality
Author proves that the Necessary Being knows universals because He is immaterial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism proving the Necessary Being's knowledge of universals and explain how the author moves from mental possibility to external actuality.

Core Idea
God's immateriality implies knowledge of all intelligibles; general possibility bridges mental conception and external actuality.

This passage uses a categorical syllogism to prove that the Necessary Being (God) knows all universals. The author starts with the premises that the Necessary Being is immaterial, and that all immaterial beings know universals. However, a difficulty arises because the Necessary Being is a particular, and particulars cannot be mentally apprehended. The author resolves this by shifting the argument from mental conjunction to external reality, using the principle of general possibility and the necessity of actualizing all potential perfections in the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial categorical syllogism
Find the minor premise (الصغرى) and major premise (الكبرى) in the Arabic. The minor is 'the Necessary Being is free from matter' (أما الصغرى فقد مر ذكرها). The major is 'every being free from matter must know universals' (وأما الكبرى فلأن كل مجرد...). The conclusion is that the Necessary Being knows universals.
The author says the minor was mentioned before, so we focus on the major.
2
Recognize the objection about mental conjunction
The argument faces a problem: the Necessary Being is a particular, and particulars cannot be present in the mind as themselves (والشيء المتشخص يستحيل حصوله بنفسه في الذهن). Only universal natures are mentally apprehended. So how can the Necessary Being be conjoined with other intelligibles in the mind?
3
Follow the author's resolution via general possibility
The author shifts from mental conjunction to external reality. He argues that what is logically possible in the mind (ممكن بالإمكان العام) is also possible absolutely (ممكن مطلقا). Since we can conceive the Necessary Being together with all intelligibles without contradiction, it is possible in reality that He is conjoined with them.
General possibility (الإمكان العام) is the mere logical absence of contradiction.
4
Apply the principle of necessary actuality
For the Necessary Being, all possibilities are actual (كل ما يمكن للواجب من الصفات فهو حاصل له بالفعل). Therefore, He actually knows all universals. The argument concludes that the Necessary Being is the substrate (محل) for intelligible attributes because He cannot inhere in anything else.
This step uses the necessary being's completeness: no potential perfection remains unrealized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته عالم بالكليات لأنه مجرد عن المادة ولواحقها، وكل مجرد عن المادة ولواحقها يجب أن يكون عالما بالكليات، أما الصغرى فقد مر ذكرها، وأما الكبرى فلأن كل مجرد يمكن بالإمكان العام أن يعقل، وهذا بديهي لا خفاء فيه، وكل ما يمكن أن يعقل وحده يمكن أن يعقل مع كل واحد من المعقولات لا محالة، فيمكن أن يقارنه سائر المعقولات في النفس، فإن الإدراك والتعقل هو حضور صورة المعقول في العقل مجردة عن المادة ولواحقها وكل ما يمكن أن يقارنه سائر المعقولات في العقل يمكن أن يقارنه = كما ثبت من قبل، والشيء المتشخص يستحيل حصوله بنفسه في الذهن، إنما الحصول الذهني للطبائع الكلية نعم يتصور بالوجه وهذا لا يفيد في هذا المقام؛ لأن مناط الاستدلال على اقتران الواجب لذاته بالصور المعقولة في الذهن، ولما امتنع حصوله في الذهن امتنع الاقتران. ما يمكن حاصل الاستدلال أن الواجب لذاته يمكن أن يكون معقولا، وكل ما يمكن أن يكون معقولا وحده يمكن أن يكون معقولا مع سائر المعقولات؛ لأنه لا حجر في التصور، فينتج أن الواجب لذاته يمكن أن يكون معقولا مع سائر المعقولات، فيمكن أن يقارن الواجب لذاته سائر المعقولات في الذهن، وكل ما يمكن لذات الواجب لذاته في الذهن يمكنه مطلقا؛ لأن إمكان العام مقدم على إمكان الخاص، وقد ثبت إمكان اقتران المعقولات بالواجب لذاته في الذهن، فقد ثبت إمكان اقترانها مطلقا بدون الذهن فقد ظهر إمكان اقتران المعقولات بالواجب لذاته في الخارج، ولا يمكن اقتران المعقولات بالواجب لذاته في الخارج إلا بأن يكون الواجب محلا لها؛ لامتناع حلول الواجب في شيء، وكل ما يمكن للواجب من الصفات فهو حاصل له بالفعل؛ لما مر من أن الواجب لذاته واجب من جميع جهاته، ليس له كمال منتظر.
Chapter: On that the Necessary Being knows universals. Because He is free from matter and its accidents, and every being free from matter and its accidents must be knowing of universals. As for the minor premise, it was mentioned before; as for the major premise, because every being free from matter is, in general possibility, capable of being intellected, and this is self-evident without concealment. And whatever can be intellected alone can be intellected with each one of the intelligibles inevitably, so it is possible for Him to be conjoined with other intelligibles in the soul. For perception and intellection is the presence of the image of the intelligible in the mind abstracted from matter and its accidents. And everything that can be conjoined with other intelligibles in the mind can be conjoined = as established before. And an individuated thing cannot possibly be obtained in the mind itself; mental attainment is only of universal natures. Yes, it is imagined in a certain way, but this does not benefit in this respect; because the basis of the inference is the conjunction of the Necessary Being with intelligible forms in the mind, and since His attainment in the mind is impossible, conjunction is impossible. The summary of the inference: The Necessary Being can be intellected. And whatever can be intellected alone can be intellected with all intelligibles because there is no obstacle in conception. So it follows that the Necessary Being can be intellected with all intelligibles. So it is possible that the Necessary Being is conjoined with all intelligibles in the mind. And whatever is possible for the essence of the Necessary Being in the mind is possible absolutely, because general possibility precedes specific possibility. And it has been established that the conjunction of intelligibles with the Necessary Being is possible in the mind, so it is possible absolutely without the mind. Thus it has appeared that it is possible for intelligibles to be conjoined with the Necessary Being in the external world. And it is not possible for intelligibles to be conjoined with the Necessary Being in the external world except that the Necessary Being be a substrate for them, because it is impossible for the Necessary Being to inhere in anything. And every attribute that is possible for the Necessary Being is actually present in Him, because it has passed that the Necessary Being is necessary from all aspects, and He has no potential perfection.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed proof that the Necessary Being (God) knows universals. The author begins with a categorical syllogism: the minor premise is that the Necessary Being is immaterial (free from matter and its accidents), and the major premise is that every immaterial being necessarily knows universals. The conclusion is that the Necessary Being knows universals. The author then expands the major premise: every immaterial being can be intellected (i.e., is an object of intellectual apprehension) in a universal sense, and anything that can be intellected alone can be intellected together with other intelligibles. However, a problem arises: the Necessary Being is a particular existent, and particular or individuated things cannot be represented in the mind as such—only universal natures can be mentally apprehended. Therefore, how can the Necessary Being be conjoined with other intelligibles in the mind? The author resolves this by shifting from mental to external reality. He argues that what is possible in the mind is possible in reality, and vice versa. Since it is possible in the mind to conceive the Necessary Being together with other universals (by the general possibility), it is possible in reality that the Necessary Being has these universals as attributes. But in reality, conjunction means that the Necessary Being is the substrate (mahall) for those attributes, i.e., He possesses them. Since no possibility for God remains unrealized, He actually knows all universals. This argument uses the principle that for the Necessary Being, all potential perfections are actual. The passage is rich with logical moves: from possibility in the mind to possibility in reality, and from possibility to actuality for God.

Key Terms

الإمكان العام (al-imkan al-'amm)
المعقولات (al-ma'qulat)

Disjunctive Proof of the First Intellect
Author proves that the first emanation must be intellect by eliminating matter, form, accident, and soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the disjunctive syllogism that proves the first emanation is intellect and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
The first emanation must be intellect because every other candidate is impossible.

This passage presents a classic disjunctive syllogism (qiyas munfasil) from Avicennan metaphysics. The author, al-Abhari, aims to prove that the first emanation from God is the First Intellect. The argument divides all possibilities for that first simple emanation into five categories and eliminates four of them by logical and ontological reasoning. Understanding this proof requires you to follow the structure of an exhaustive disjunction and the principle 'from the one only one proceeds.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Find the statement that presents all possible options for the first emanation. The author says: 'That one must be either matter, form, accident, soul, or intellect.'
This is a five-part exhaustive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluw).
2
List the five disjuncts
Write down each option: hayula (matter), sura (form), 'arad (accident), nafs (soul), 'aql (intellect). These are the only possible types of simple substances.
3
Analyze each elimination
For each of the first four options, note the reason given for its impossibility: matter lacks actuality without form; form does not precede matter in causality; accident requires prior substance; soul requires a body to act through, which does not yet exist.
The reasons rely on ontological priority and the nature of each candidate.
4
Confirm the conclusion
Since the first four are eliminated, intellect alone remains. The argument concludes that the first emanation must be intellect. This is a valid disjunctive syllogism if the disjunction is truly exhaustive.
The validity depends on the exhaustiveness of the list; the author assumes no other simple substance exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثالث في الملائكة وهي العقول المجردة، وهو يشتمل على أربعة فصول.
فصل في إثبات العقل
وبرهانه أن الصادر من المبدأ الأول إنما هو الواحد؛ لأنه بسيط، والبسيط لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد كما مر، وذلك الواحد إما أن يكون هيولى أو صورة أو عرضا أو نفسا أو عقلا، لا جائز أن يكون هو الهيولى؛ لأنها لا تقوم بالفعل بدون الصورة، ولا جائز أن يكون صورة؛ لأنها لا تتقدم بالعلية على الهيولى كما مر، ولا جائز أن يكون عرضا؛ لاستحالة وجوده قبل وجود الجوهر، ولا جائز أن يكون نفسا وإلا لكان فاعلا قبل وجود الجسم، وهو محال؛ إذ النفس هي التي تفعل بواسطة الأجسام، فتعين أن يكون عقلا، وهو المطلوب.
The third art: on the angels, which are the intellects separate from matter. It comprises four chapters. Chapter on proving the intellect. Its proof: that which proceeds from the First Principle is only one; because He is simple, and from the simple only one proceeds, as previously established. That one must be either matter, form, accident, soul, or intellect. It cannot be matter, because it has no actuality without form. It cannot be form, because it does not precede matter in causality, as previously established. It cannot be accident, because it is impossible for it to exist before the existence of substance. It cannot be soul, because then it would be an agent before the existence of body, which is impossible; for the soul acts through the mediation of bodies. So it is determined that it be intellect, which is what was sought.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents a disjunctive syllogism (qiyas munfasil) to prove that the first emanation from God (the First Principle) is an intellect. The argument proceeds by dividing the possibilities for the first simple emanation into five categories: matter (hayula), form (sura), accident ('arad), soul (nafs), and intellect ('aql). Each of the first four is eliminated by specific reasons: matter lacks actuality without form; form is ontologically dependent on matter in terms of causality; accident cannot exist before substance; soul would require a body to act through, but body does not yet exist. By elimination, the only remaining possibility is intellect, which is a self-subsistent, immaterial substance. This proof relies on the principle 'from the one only one proceeds' (min al-wahid la yasduru illa al-wahid), a cornerstone of Avicennan emanationism. The elimination uses logical necessity and ontological priority.

Key Takeaway
The first emanation is proved to be intellect by a disjunctive syllogism that eliminates all other possibilities.

Proof of the Multiplicity of Intellects: A Disjunctive Syllogism
Author proves that celestial spheres require multiple intellects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof for the multiplicity of celestial intellects and identify the logical structure of disjunctive syllogism with conditional reductio.

Core Idea
The multiplicity of spheres proves the multiplicity of intellects via elimination and impossibility of sphere-to-sphere causation.

In this passage, the author presents a decisive proof (burhan) for the existence of multiple separate intellects. The argument proceeds by enumerating the possible causes of the spheres and systematically eliminating all but one. Understanding this argument sharpens your ability to apply disjunctive syllogism in metaphysical contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction
The cause of the spheres is either one intellect, one sphere, or multiple intellects. This is a three-part disjunction that forms the backbone of the proof.
2
Eliminate the first alternative
One intellect cannot produce multiple spheres because of the principle 'from one only one proceeds'. Since there are many spheres, a single intellect is impossible.
This principle is a key metaphysical axiom in falsafah.
3
Eliminate the second alternative: two sub-possibilities
If a sphere caused another sphere, it could be either the container causing the contained or vice versa. Both must be examined.
4
Refute the vice versa
The contained sphere is lower and smaller (akhass wa asghar) than the container. It cannot be the cause of the higher, greater sphere, due to the metaphysical hierarchy.
This step uses the principle that the lower cannot cause the higher.
5
Refute the container causing the contained
If the container caused the contained, then the contained's existence would be posterior to the container's. Then the nonexistence of the contained with the container's existence would not be impossible, which would imply the possibility of void (al-khala'), which is absurd. Therefore this path is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إثبات كثرة العقول وبرهانه أن المؤثر في الأفلاك إما أن يكون عقلا واحدا أو فلكا واحدا أو عقولا متكثرة، لا جائز أن يكون عقلا واحدا؛ لاستحالة صدور جميع الأفلاك عن عقل واحد؛ لما بينا أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد، ولا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأن الفلك لو كان علة لفلك آخر، فإما أن يكون الحاوي علة لوجود المحوي أو على العكس، لا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأنه أخس وأصغر، والأخس الأصغر استحال أن يكون سببا للأشرف الأعظم، ولا جائز أن يكون الحاوي علة لوجود المحوي؛ لأنه لو كان كذلك لكان وجوب وجود المحوي متأخرا عن وجوب وجود الحاوي؛ لأن وجوب وجود المعلول مؤخر عن وجوب وجود العلة، وإذا كان كذلك فعدم المحوي مع وجود الحاوي لا يكون ممتنعا لذاته وإلا لكان وجوده معه لا متأخرا عنه، وقد فرضناه متأخرا، هذا خلف، وإذا كان عدم المحوي مع وجود الحاوي ممكنا كان وجود الخلاء ممكنا لذاته، هذا خلف، فظهر أن المؤثر في الأفلاك عقول متكثرة.
Chapter on proving the multiplicity of intellects. Its proof: The effect on the spheres is either one intellect, one sphere, or multiple intellects. It is not permissible to be one intellect, because it is impossible for all spheres to emanate from one intellect, as we have shown that from one only one proceeds. Nor is the second [one sphere] possible, because if a sphere were a cause of another sphere, then either the container is a cause of the existence of the contained or vice versa. The second [vice versa] is not possible because it is lower and smaller, and the lower and smaller cannot be a cause of the higher and greater. Nor is it permissible that the container be a cause of the existence of the contained, because if so, the necessary existence of the contained would be posterior to the necessary existence of the container, since the necessary existence of the effect is posterior to the necessary existence of the cause. If that is so, then the nonexistence of the contained with the existence of the container is not impossible in itself; otherwise its existence with it would not be posterior, but we have assumed it posterior, which is a contradiction. And if the nonexistence of the contained with the existence of the container is possible, then the existence of void is possible in itself, which is a contradiction. Thus it appears that the effect on the spheres is multiple intellects.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) that the celestial spheres are caused by multiple intellects (angels) rather than a single intellect or a sphere. The author begins by stating the conclusion: the effect on the spheres (their motion or existence) must be either one intellect, one sphere, or many intellects. The first alternative is rejected because, as established earlier, from a cause that is one in every respect only one effect can proceed (the principle 'from one only one proceeds'). Since there are multiple spheres, a single intellect cannot produce them all. The second alternative (a sphere causing another sphere) is examined by considering two possibilities: that the containing sphere (al-hawi) causes the contained sphere (al-mahwi), or vice versa. The vice versa is impossible because the contained sphere is lower (akhass) and smaller (asghar), and the lower cannot be cause of the higher. The other possibility (the container causing the contained) is then refuted by a reductio ad absurdum. If the container caused the contained, then the contained's existence would be posterior to the container's existence. But then the nonexistence of the contained with the existence of the container would not be intrinsically impossible, which leads to the possibility of void (al-khala'), which is impossible. Therefore, the only remaining possibility is that the spheres are caused by multiple intellects. This argument is a classic example of a disjunctive syllogism combined with conditional reasoning, emphasizing the metaphysical principle of emanation.

المحوي

Key Takeaway
The proof for the multiplicity of intellects demonstrates the power of disjunctive syllogism combined with metaphysical principles and reductio ad absurdum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a similar disjunctive syllogism to prove that the celestial spheres must be multiple, using the same principle but with a different classification of causes (e.g., material, efficient, formal, final).

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Compound Disjunctive Syllogism for Event Precedence
Author proves event precedence via compound disjunctive syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the compound disjunctive syllogism proving that every event is preceded by another event and identify the role of burhān al-taṭbīq in eliminating simultaneous existence.

Core Idea
Every temporal event must have a preceding cause; otherwise an infinite simultaneous chain would exist—which is impossible.

This passage from Hidayat al-Hikma presents a rigorous logical proof that every temporal event (ḥādith) is preceded by another event. The argument uses two disjunctive syllogisms and an infinite regress argument to show that events cannot be eternal nor simultaneous, leading to an essentially ordered series requiring a first cause. Understanding this compound syllogism is essential for grasping the classical philosophical proof for the existence of an eternal creator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first disjunctive syllogism
The author begins with a dichotomy: events are either eternal (exist perpetually) or preceded by another event. The first alternative is rejected because events are temporal (they come into being after not existing). Therefore, every event must be preceded by another event.
This is a simple modus tollendo ponens (affirmative disjunctive syllogism).
2
Identify the second disjunctive syllogism
Now consider the series of preceding events: they either exist simultaneously (all at once) or successively (one after another). The simultaneous case is ruled out by the proof of superimposition (burhān al-taṭbīq), which shows that an infinite set of ordered things cannot coexist. Therefore, the series must be successive.
The proof of superimposition is a key argument against actual infinites in an ordered series.
3
Analyze the infinite regress argument
The author provides a further proof: the cause of an event cannot be entirely eternal, otherwise the effect would also be eternal (which it is not). Hence the cause must contain a temporal event itself, and that event's cause also contains an event, ad infinitum. This shows that the series of events is essentially ordered and cannot be self-sufficient.
This regress is not vicious; it leads to the necessity of a first cause that is eternal and contains no temporal event.
4
Combine the arguments into a compound syllogism
The overall proof is compound: the first disjunctive syllogism establishes that every event is preceded; the second establishes that the preceding series is successive; and the infinite regress argument shows the series is essentially ordered. Together they prepare for the conclusion that a first eternal cause must exist (though not stated in this passage).
In formal terms, the conclusion of the first syllogism serves as a premise for the second, and the regress argument reinforces the need for an external cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل حادث سواء كان حركة أو غيرها مسبوق بحادث آخر؛ لأن الحوادث إما أن توجد دائما أو بعد حدوث حادث آخر، والأول باطل؛ ضرورة أن الحادث لا يوجد دائما، فتعين الثاني، فنقول: هذه الحوادث المسبوقة بحوادث أخر إما أن توجد على سبيل الاجتماع أو على التعاقب، والأول باطل؛ لاستحالة الأمور الغير المتناهية الترتبة في الوجود ببرهان التطبيق، فتعين الثاني وقيل الأولى أن يقال في إثبات أن كل حادث مسبوق بحادث آخر: إن العلة الحادث لا تكون بجميع أجزائها قديمة، وإلا لزم قدم الحادث، فلا بد أن تكون علته مشتملة على حادث، وذلك الحادث أيضا لا تكون علته قديمة، فتكون علته أيضا مشتملة على حادث، وهكذا إلى غير النهاية.
Every event, whether motion or something else, is preceded by another event. Because events are either existent perpetually or after the occurrence of another event. The first is false, necessarily because the event does not exist perpetually, so the second is determined. Then we say: These events that are preceded by other events either exist simultaneously or successively. The first is false due to the impossibility of infinite things ordered in existence by the proof of superimposition (burhān al-taṭbīq). So the second is determined. And it was said—rather, it should be said in proving that every event is preceded by another event: The cause of an event is not entirely eternal in all its parts; otherwise the event would be eternal. Thus its cause must contain an event, and that event also its cause is not eternal, so its cause also contains an event, and so on to infinity.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage provides a proof that every temporal event must have a preceding event. The argument is a compound disjunctive syllogism. First, the author states that events are either eternal or preceded by another event. Since events are not eternal (they come into being), they must be preceded. Second, the series of preceding events either exists all at once (simultaneously) or successively. Simultaneous existence of an infinite ordered series is impossible, as proven by the 'proof of superimposition' (an argument that an actual infinite cannot exist). Therefore, the events must be successive. The author then offers a further proof: the cause of an event cannot be entirely eternal, for then the effect would be eternal as well. Hence every cause must itself contain a temporal event, and that event's cause must also contain a temporal event, leading to an infinite regress. This argument is used in Islamic philosophy to establish that the temporal series of events is essentially ordered and requires a first cause (which is eternal). Note that the passage does not explicitly mention the first cause here; it only establishes the necessity of preceding events.

Key Terms
حادث
برهان التطبيق
علة
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because they are temporal and would require an infinite simultaneous number, which is impossible.

D) Because events are preceded by other events.

Key Takeaway
The passage uses a compound disjunctive syllogism to prove that every event is preceded by another event, and the series cannot be simultaneous; it must be successive and essentially ordered, requiring an external eternal cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the compound syllogism from the passage in your own words. Clearly state each premise, the intermediate conclusions, and the final conclusion. Explain why the simultaneous alternative is impossible using the proof of superimposition.
?
Explain how the infinite regress argument (the cause contains an event, and so on) leads to the need for a first cause that is eternal. How does this argument avoid circularity?
